HEADLINE: Educators Generally Favor Clinton's College Proposal

BYLINE: By KAREN W. ARENSON 

 BODY:
President Clinton's proposals for making college more affordable were generally greeted positively yesterday by college presidents and other higher education officials, though some said the plan emphasized tax relief for the middle class too much.
"This plan would help insure that a college education is within the reach of virtually every American," said Stanley O. Ikenberry, president of the American Council on Education, a nonprofit organization that represents the nation's colleges and universities in Washington. 
 Thomas Kane, a professor at the John F. Kennedy School of Government at Harvard University, called it "good news for the lowest income college students." But Professor Kane added: "While it is probably a relief to middle-income families, it's not educational policy. It won't be driving enrollments up."
The centerpieces of the program are tax deductions of up to $10,000 a year for college tuition or job training and tuition tax credits, in which the Government would refund up to $1,500 a year for the first two years of college. The President said these were intended to insure that every American could afford at least two years of community college.
When Mr. Clinton laid out these ideas in the Presidential campaign, some education officials and policy analysts criticized the plan for directing too many dollars to the middle class at the expense of the lowest-income students. Yesterday's proposal was modified to address those criticisms, Federal education officials said.
As originally proposed, low-income students without taxable earnings might still have been eligible for refunds. As modified, they will not. Instead, Mr. Clinton proposed using the money to expand direct aid, like Pell grants, to the neediest students.
Mr. Clinton proposes raising the amount available to needy students through Pell grants to $3,000 a year. The maximum grant is now $2,470 and is scheduled to rise to $2,700 for the next school year. He would also make more students eligible for Pell grants by raising income limits and by including many students who are not dependent on their parents for support.
"It will also open the doors of college education wider than ever before," he said yesterday.
Other highlights of the President's higher education proposals include a reduction in fees for Federal student loans, which now amount to 4 percent of the loan, and a measure that would allow families to use savings in their tax-deferred individual retirement accounts, or I.R.A.'s, for educational expenses. Currently, savers must pay a penalty if they withdraw their money before the age of 59 1/2.
Some higher education officials interviewed yesterday welcomed the changes, but many also said they did not go far enough.
Michael S. McPherson, president of Macalester College in St. Paul, a specialist on the economics of higher education, also criticized the tax breaks as a way to make college more affordable.
"Once you introduce a whole new category of tax breaks into the tax code, you start down a certain road that is worrisome," Mr. McPherson said. "You are going to have a lot of people lobbying to expand the program over time and that money is going to tend to be siphoned away from other forms of higher education support."
He, like others, also expressed a concern that some colleges might try to raise their tuition to try to share some of the subsidy that would be available through the tax breaks. And that, he said, could lead to proposals to try to regulate tuition.
L. Jay Oliva, president of New York University, said he was generally pleased with the president's focus on higher education, but had some reservations about the package.
"Anything that gives encouragement to students to go to college is terrific," Mr. Oliva said. 'But we have to make sure Pell grants don't get clobbered in the process. I don't see any evidence of it here, but the political process is a long one."
But officials at the Department of Education said they were concerned both with helping the neediest students and with helping middle class families who have been squeezed by tuition prices that are still going up faster than inflation and incomes.
"We've listened to some of the concerns that were raised," said David Longanecker, assistant secretary for post-secondary education. "Although the program was designed unashamedly to help middle-income people, it did help lower-income people too."

HEADLINE: Mob Fighter Named Chief of Javits Center as It Switches Focus to Growth

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Gerald T. McQueen, a veteran police executive and specialist in organized crime who has been a central figure in the fight against corruption at the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center, was nominated to be the center's chief executive yesterday. The nomination comes as the center shifts its focus from fighting mob influence to expanding its marketability and size.
Mr. McQueen, 60, has been the center's inspector general since April 1995, having taken the job after a 30-year career that included posts as head of the organized crime unit under the Manhattan District Attorney, Robert M. Morgenthau, and chief investigator for the Ethics Committee of the House of Representatives. In his new post, he replaces Robert E. Boyle, who was named yesterday as head of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey. 
 "He was a vital part of Bob Boyle's team as they rooted out corruption," Gov. George E. Pataki said in making his recommendation, which is expected to be taken up by the center's board before the end of February.
In separate interviews, both Mr. Boyle and Mr. McQueen said a top item on the convention center's agenda had become plans to expand the center by adding enough space so that it could compete with far larger -- and more profitable -- convention halls in Chicago, Las Vegas and other cities.
"We are back," said Mr. McQueen, referring to the center's recent efforts to root out corrupt labor union employees and attract more business. "Now expansion becomes more important because other cities continue to grow. We could get lost in the pack."
Although proposals to expand the center have been circulating for years, and have been supported by Governor Pataki, none have won the endorsement of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, whose support is considered essential. Indeed, economic advisers to the Mayor have differed, sometimes angrily, with those to the Governor, as Mr. Giuliani's aides have suggested the focus should be more on creating convention space elsewhere in Manhattan.
But in recent days, city officials appear to have become more receptive to some form of expansion plan at the Javits site.
"There is no question that we have a thoroughly inadequate convention center," Fran Reiter, the Deputy Mayor for Planning and Economic Development, said yesterday.
Although Ms. Reiter said she and other top city officials had yet to examine the plans in detail, Mr. Boyle said yesterday that a proposal would be made to double the center's existing 900,000 square feet of exhibition space. The addition would extend the center, now bounded by 34th Street, 39th Street, 11th Avenue and 12th Avenue, three blocks north to the south side of 42d Street. The plan was first reported yesterday by Crain's New York Business.
"We think this plan is good in every way," said Charles Gargano, the chief economic adviser to Governor Pataki. The Governor has suggested several times that the best way to expand Javits Center would be to make it accessible from a rejuvenated 42d Street.
Several hurdles would have to be mounted for the expansion plan to succeed, including how to finance what Mr. Boyle estimated at $700 million in land acquisition and construction costs.
After years of bickering over the future of the center, city and state officials would also have to come to terms over political control. Mayor Giuliani is known want more influence over the operations of the center -- which is largely state-controlled -- in return for his support of an expansion plan.

HEADLINE: S.E.C. Adopts Derivatives-Disclosure Rules

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
The Securities and Exchange Commission adopted rules yesterday that will force most American companies to provide additional information to investors about the derivative securities they own and the risks those securities entail.
The rules were altered in several ways from preliminary proposals announced last year, largely to give companies more discretion in how they make the disclosures and, in some cases, to allow companies to combine disparate items in the reports to avoid giving competitors access to sensitive information. 
 "Derivatives are terribly important, if properly used," said Arthur Levitt Jr., the chairman of the S.E.C. "But there has to be greater understanding by investors of how derivatives should be used."
Derivatives include options, futures contracts and swaps.
The rules had been subject to substantial lobbying by companies worried about the cost of compliance and fearful that they would have to disclose competitive information. Representative Michael Oxley, an Ohio Republican and a member of the Energy and Commerce Committee, sent a letter to the S.E.C. asking for details on how the commission had concluded that the benefits of the rules would be greater than the costs.
In an interview, Mr. Levitt said that the S.E.C. estimated that complying with the rules would cost American business $40 million. He played down the size of this figure, saying that he had previously served on the board of a charitable foundation that had suffered a $30 million loss from the misuse of derivatives by a money manager.
The rules would require disclosures of financial risk -- measured in one of three ways -- stemming from derivatives and other financial instruments. For financial institutions and large companies with a market value of at least $2.5 billion, they take effect for fiscal years that end after June 30, 1997. They will apply to smaller companies a year later.
The new rules, while they will require disclosures, will not change the accounting for derivatives. The Financial Accounting Standards Board is considering a rule on that and is expected to act later this year.
By giving companies the right to use any of three methods of disclosing financial risk, the S.E.C. has reduced the comparability of information among companies, and it will look at the issue again in three years. But it has also given in to the fact that there is a widespread dispute as to the best way to disclose such risk.
The ways that will be allowed include providing a table showing the fair value and contract terms of instruments, a choice that is likely to be chosen by companies with few derivative instruments to disclose. The other two are sensitivity analysis, which expresses the potential loss or gain stemming from possible changes in interest rates or other relevant market rates or prices, and value-at-risk calculations, showing the potential loss from market movements with a certain likelihood of occurrence.

HEADLINE: Clinton Concedes China Policy Hasn't Helped Much on Rights

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
President Clinton conceded today that his doctrine of "constructive engagement" with China had failed to engender the progress on human rights last year that he had hoped it would, but he added that he still believed "that the policy we're following is the correct one."
Speaking at the first news conference of his second term, at a moment when Washington and Beijing are in the process of setting up reciprocal visits between Mr. Clinton and President Jiang Zemin of China, the President came close to predicting the ultimate demise of the Communist government. 
 "I believe that the impulses of the society and the nature of the economic change will work together, along with the availability of information from the outside world, to increase the spirit of liberty over time," he said.
"I don't think that there's any way that anyone who disagrees with that in China can hold that back. I just think it's inevitable, just as inevitably the Berlin wall fell."
Administration officials said on Monday that the annual United States report on human rights around the world, due on Thursday, would sharply criticize China. It is said to conclude that there are no active dissidents left in China, all having been jailed or sent into exile. One official said the report makes no effort "to disguise China's retreat on human rights in 1996."
On another pressing foreign policy topic, the President said he still expects to meet in March with President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia, despite reports from Moscow that Mr. Yeltsin's fragile health following a heart operation might force a postponement or cancellation of the summit conference. After quintuple bypass surgery last Nov. 5, Mr. Yeltsin contracted double pneumonia this month and has been little seen in public since then.
In theory, the March meeting should be in Washington, since the two men last met in Moscow last April. But American officials said today that the meeting might be held in a third country, closer to Moscow, to ease the strain on Mr. Yeltsin.
He appeared on Russian television today for the first time in three weeks, smiling and seemingly alert, but he has canceled a trip to the Netherlands on the advice of his doctors, who said he should not fly yet.
"We have a huge, full agenda," Mr. Clinton declared, naming the expansion of NATO, which Moscow has vigorously opposed, and Russian ratification of the START II treaty on reducing nuclear arsenals as major items on the list. "So I think we'll go right on, and I expect to have that meeting in March. I expect it to be an important one and, I hope, a successful one."
Final details of the conference are to be worked out by Prime Minister Viktor Chernomyrdin of Russia and Vice President Al Gore here next week.
Mr. Clinton also sought, in an indirect but unmistakable way, to hold the feet of the Saudi leadership to the fire in the investigation of the bombing that killed 19 American soldiers last year. Twice he said that he had been personally assured by the Saudi Government that it would provide answers to all questions posed by the American investigators.
At one point, he said that he had received such assurances from "the very highest levels" of the Saudi Government -- a formulation that might be taken to mean King Fahd himself.
The director of the F.B.I., Louis Freeh, who is in charge of the American investigation, and Attorney General Janet Reno complained last week that the Saudis were not cooperating fully enough. Mr. Clinton did not contradict them directly, declining to answer a question as to whether he was satisfied with Saudi cooperation to date.
"It's still in process," he said. "We have to see if it comes out all right. But there are further requests for information that are ongoing. We'll see how it comes out."
The President's concession that his China policy had produced disappointing results represented a distinct shift for the Administration. Over the past year or two, when asked when results would begin to be visible, such varied officials as Mr. Gore, former Secretary of State Warren Christopher and the late Commerce Secretary, Ron Brown, had suggested they were imminent.
Mr. Clinton has declined to treat progress on human rights or on restricting the spread of nuclear weapons as prerequisites for high-level meetings and preferential trade status. His critics say this policy has the effect of putting economic considerations ahead of all others and taking the pressure off China's leadership.
The President disagreed.
"I still believe," he said, "that over the long run being engaged with China, working with them where we can agree, which helps us on a whole range of security issues that directly bear on the welfare of the American people, like the problems on the Korean Peninsula, and continuing to be honest and forthright where we disagree, has the greatest likelihood of having a positive impact on China."
In response to another question about China, Mr. Clinton said that he was "concerned" about indications that the Chinese might restrict civil liberties when they take over Hong Kong from the British later this year. Noting that the Chinese had promised to leave the system of government in Hong Kong intact, even though it will be a part of China, he said "there may be some ambivalence" as to exactly what the Chinese leadership has in mind.
"I'm not so sure that it can exist," the President added, "with all of its potential to help China modernize its own economy and open opportunities for its own people if the civil liberties of the people are crushed.
"So I would hope very much that they would look for ways to maximize the continuation not only of the economic system, but of the personal freedoms that the people of Hong Kong have enjoyed in making it such an economic engine."

LENGTH: 401 words

HEADLINE: Balanced-Budget Politics

 BODY:
The House Republicans pledged an early vote on the balanced-budget amendment in part to bolster their wounded Speaker by giving him a quick victory. But the two-thirds majority that Newt Gingrich and his allies thought they had may be slipping away, a reflection of Mr. Gingrich's diminished control and the 10-seat electoral swing to the Democrats. The outcome is also uncertain in the Senate, where the first committee vote is scheduled for this week.
Even so, President Clinton's steadfast opposition is essential to defeating this dangerously misguided amendment. After the elections he temporized, telling Trent Lott, the Senate majority leader, that they could reach a compromise on the popular amendment if it could be written to protect the economy during recessions. But goaded by Treasury Secretary Robert Rubin, Mr. Clinton has steadied and, at yesterday's news conference, strongly rejected an idea he knows would recklessly tamper with the Constitution and, no matter how worded, threaten the economy. 
 Mr. Clinton would do well to focus his persuasive powers on two first-term Senate Democrats, Robert Torricelli of New Jersey and Tim Johnson of South Dakota, who backed the idea of an amendment during hard-fought campaigns but appear to harbor reservations -- as well they should.
The amendment would prohibit the Government from spending beyond its revenues unless three-fifths of both houses vote to suspend the prohibition. The economic threat is obvious. When the economy slows and tax revenues decline, the amendment would drive Congress to cut spending and hike taxes, precisely the opposite of what a wounded economy needs. The three-fifths waiver provision offers little consolation because Congress is always slow to recognize downturns and never nimble enough to react in time.
Worse, the waiver provision gives a minority of legislators the power to throw government into default and the economy into a downturn unless their colleagues fork over concessions.
The amendment contains no enforcement mechanism, which raises the possibility that the courts or the President would replace Congress in dictating how the Government will spend its money during years of looming deficits.
Mr. Clinton's first responsibility is to press pivotal Democrats like Mr. Torricelli and Mr. Johnson to reject an idea that looked good in the abstract but is dangerous in reality.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Market for PC's Rose 16% in '96

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: FRAMINGHAM, Mass., Jan. 28

 BODY:
The worldwide market for personal computers rose 16 percent last year, a slowdown from the 24 percent growth in 1995 and the slowest growth rate since 1991, a market research firm said this week.
The Compaq Computer Corporation led the market, followed by I.B.M., which gained market share for the first time since 1993, the International Data Corporation said. Apple Computer Inc. lost market share, while the Hewlett-Packard Company moved into the top five for the first time. 
 Compaq increased its market share to 10.3 percent from 9.8 percent. I.B.M. increased its share to 8.9 percent from 8.1 percent last year.
Apple's market share fell to 5.2 percent from 7.9 percent last year, knocking it to fourth place from third. Packard Bell NEC Inc. moved into the No. 3 spot worldwide, with 6.2 percent of the market. Hewlett-Packard was No. 5, with 4.4 percent.
The slower worldwide growth was caused by poor retail sales in the United States and sluggish economies in some regions. The slowdown is expected to continue this year, International Data said.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Prices for Bonds Advance on Wage Data

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The prices of Government securities increased yesterday after a four-day slide, with investors showing relief as a report on wage increases in the fourth quarter indicated no likely near-term spur to inflation. Prices had been falling because of anxiety that the Federal Reserve might raise interest rates in an effort to head off inflation.
The 30-year bond finished trading in the New York session up 6/32, to a price of 94 22/32. The yield, which moves in the opposite direction of the price, fell to 6.92 percent, from 6.94 percent on Monday. 
 Interest rates could still come under pressure in the days ahead just from supply alone, as a number of issues come to market. Today, the Treasury will sell $7 billion in the new inflation-indexed securities, and tomorrow, $19.25 billion in one-year bills. Corporate issuance is also expected to reach about $3.5 billion during the week.
In when-issued trading, the inflation-indexed security was being offered at a price to yield 3.51 percent, up three-hundredths of a percentage point from the previous session.
Early yesterday, buyers drove up the price of the long bond by as much as 1 10/32. But the release of a report showing a slight rise in consumer confidence in January shaved off some of the gains.
The Conference Board's index of consumer confidence jumped to 116.8 points in January from a revised 114.2 last month. Gains in consumer confidence can be negative for the bond market because they may foreshadow increased inflation, which erodes the value of bonds.
Bonds were helped, though, by the Labor Department's report on wages. The employment cost index, which measures wages and benefits, rose only eight-tenths of 1 percent in the fourth quarter and a smaller-than-expected 2.9 percent for all of 1996.
Kenneth J. McCarthy, founder of the Economic Intelligence Company, said this measure, more than any other, showed that the fear of wage-driven inflation had been overstated.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, noted that while wages and salaries had continued to climb, benefit costs had lagged noticeably.
The relatively benign overall employment cost figure, he said, would give the Federal Open Market Committee, the Fed's policy-setting arm, greater freedom to keep interest rates steady at its next two-day meeting, which begins on Tuesday.
"At the same time, however, employment costs certainly appear to be on an upward path, albeit a gradual one, and could well be a cause for considerable concern at the Fed at some future point," he said.
Mr. Fine said that the bond market's response to the consumer confidence report was muted. "That's because at this stage of the economic cycle, the consumer confidence index tends to be a lagging indicator vis-a-vis consumption patterns," and may be overstating current confidence levels.
Today's release of December durable goods orders will be closely watched by market participants to see whether the anticipated rise reverses the decline reported the month before.
"Over the last few years," Mr. Fine said, "there have been a number of times that the December figure has increased substantially, and certainly with the decline we saw in November, 1996 could well repeat the pattern."
Since 1982, Mr. Fine said, there have been only two years in which December orders for durable goods have declined. The average rise over that period, including negative years, was 2.9 percent, with the average increase in the last four years 3.5 percent in December. Analysts surveyed by Bloomberg News expect a 1.1 percent rise.
After the report, the market will probably turn its attention to testimony by Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, before the Senate Finance Committee tomorrow.

HEADLINE: THE SECOND TERM: THE DISTRICT;
Clinton Agrees on Idea of Tax Relief in Capital

BYLINE: By DAVID STOUT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
President Clinton said today that he agreed in principle with the idea of tax relief for the District of Columbia and that in fact he would introduce his own plan to revive the flagging economy of the nation's capital.
"I think there needs to be an economic incentive in the form of tax relief," the President said at a news conference in which he was asked to comment on a plan for the District that was introduced today by Eleanor Holmes Norton, the capital's nonvoting delegate to Congress. 
 But Mr. Clinton declined to embrace the specifics in Ms. Norton's plan, especially her proposal to place a 15-percent ceiling on Federal taxes paid by Washington residents.
"The last time this came up, the folks at Treasury and O.M.B. thought that the proposal was more costly than we could afford," the President said, referring to the Office of Management and Budget.
Ms. Norton has argued that her plan will help stem the flight of middle-class residents from the District to the suburbs, and it has been backed by Speaker Newt Gingrich and Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi, the Senate majority leader.
As for his own ideas, Mr. Clinton said he intended to offer a program that would be "a significant incentive for people to invest in D.C. and to help to grow the economy there."
The head of the District's financial control board, Andrew Brimmer, said that no plan would be effective if it was meant to substitute for the $660 million that Congress gives the District each year and that the District must be allowed to tax the incomes of suburban commuters who work in Washington.
Mr. Clinton agreed with that point, saying, "You can't expect any city to function and be successful if they have to pay the state's costs as well as the city's costs, raise taxes, when people can go right across the Potomac River or right up the road into Maryland."
Up to two-thirds of the people who work in the capital live in Maryland or Virginia, and they pay no commuter tax. Moreover, the District government must bear many costs normally paid by state government because it is not part of any state.
The President said he agreed with Ms. Norton that the Federal Government ought to assume costs now carried by the city that in every other municipality would be the responsibility of the state government.
The discussion of Ms. Norton's plan, whose main points have been made before, was the latest reminder that Washington has problems (crime, decaying streets, sewers and bridges, not enough money) that are common to older American cities and also has problems that are unique.
Besides its inability to impose a commuter tax and have it collected by a state government, the District is handicapped in raising money because more than 40 percent of its prime real estate is tax exempt, since it consists of government buildings, foreign embassies and universities.
And having been more or less a stepchild of Congress for decades, the District is still struggling to arrive at a consistently efficient, honest city government.

HEADLINE: THE SECOND TERM: THE BUDGET;
Clinton Toughens Stand On Changing Constitution

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
With the issue at the top of the Congressional docket, President Clinton took his strongest stand ever today against a constitutional amendment that would require a balanced Federal budget.
"The Congress has an obligation to think of what could happen here in the future and ask themselves whether they really want to straitjacket the United States," the President said at his news conference. 
 The Senate Judiciary Committee is planning to approve a constitutional amendment this week that would require the budget to be balanced in 2002 and every year after that unless the Senate and the House voted by three-fifths majorities to waive the requirement.
Both houses are expected to vote on the amendment next month, and vote counters of both parties say the outcome is too close to call.
Polls show that the public favors a balanced budget amendment by a large margin. But the amendment would do nothing to bring the budget into actual balance. The difficult spending and tax measures would still have to be enacted, and those presumably would be less popular.
For an amendment to become part of the Constitution, the Senate and the House must approve it by two-thirds majorities -- 67 yes votes in the Senate and 290 in the House, if all members are voting. It must then be ratified by the legislatures of three-quarters of the states, or 38.
The balanced budget amendment has been a central part of Republican doctrine since the party won control of Congress in the 1994 elections. In early 1995, the last time the proposed amendment was fully debated, the House approved it, but the Senate fell one vote short.
In the Senate this year, all 55 Republicans are committed to voting for the amendment, and the 9 Democrats who voted for it in 1995 are expected to do so again. That means the proponents need 3 votes from the 6 freshman Democrats.
Two of the freshmen, Richard J. Durbin of Illinois and Jack Reed of Rhode Island, voted against the amendment when they were Representatives in 1995 and have announced their opposition this time. Two others, Tim Johnson of South Dakota and Robert G. Torricelli of New Jersey, voted for the amendment when they were in the House two years ago, but they have sent signals to both sides that their votes cannot be counted on for certain this year. Mr. Torricelli may announce his position at a news conference on Wednesday.
The only two freshmen who have never cast votes on the matter, Max Cleland of Georgia and Mary L. Landrieu of Louisiana, have not publicly tipped their hands. Both said during their campaigns that they favored a balanced budget amendment, but they never said they favored the measure now being offered by the Republicans. So they could vote against the Republican amendment without being inconsistent.
At a news conference on Monday, Senator Orrin G. Hatch of Utah, the chief sponsor of the Republican amendment, predicted victory but conceded that he did not have firm commitments for 67 votes. The Democratic leader, Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, said the vote could go either way.
The situation is even more unpredictable in the House, where advance vote-counting is more difficult than in the smaller Senate.
"I think that it's within probably two or three votes either way," Representative Robert L. Livingston of Louisiana, the Republican chairman of the Appropriations Committee, said in a television interview last weekend. Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the Democratic leader, said the vote was "too close to call."
A staff assistant who is keeping a vote count for the proponents in the House offered this arithmetic: All but 1 of the 227 Republicans (John Hostettler of Indiana) are expected to vote for the measure. So are the 47 Democrats who voted for it in 1995 and 10 freshman Democrats who have signed on as co-sponsors, for a total of 283. Assuming no Democrat who voted against the measure two years ago changes sides, this means that the proponents must get the votes of 7 of the remaining 32 freshman Democrats, at least 6 of whom have already announced their opposition.
At his news conference on Monday, Senator Hatch described the amendment as the Republicans' "No. 1 priority" and said it was essential "for our children's economic freedom."
But the Clinton Administration maintains that the proposal would hamstring the Government's efforts to spend its way out of a severe economic downturn and in any case is a distraction to pending bipartisan negotiations to achieve balance in the budget.
At his news conference today, the President said he would work with Congress to produce a balanced budget by 2002, but he described the amendment as a risky enterprise. He declared:
"It would almost certainly require -- after a budget is passed, if the economic estimates turn out to be wrong -- the executive branch, the President and the Treasury Department, to impound Social Security checks or to turn it over to the courts to decide what to be done. And it would put us in a position, in my view, of doing things that are counterproductive."
As they had in the past, Democrats in Congress offered a counterproposal today that was meant to give political cover to lawmakers who want to vote against the amendment but fear repercussions in their next campaigns.
Led by Senators Dianne Feinstein of California and Byron L. Dorgan of North Dakota, the Democrats offered their own amendment, which would require a balanced budget by 2002 but exclude Social Security from the calculations.
This amendment, certain to be rejected, is based on a practical impossibility. Without counting Social Security revenue, lawmakers would have to find an additional $465 billion in savings to bring the budget into balance by 2002, according to the latest calculations of the Congressional Budget Office. Congress would never vote for that amount of spending cuts or tax increases, and the Democrats did not suggest how so large a sum might be found.
But the counterproposal gives Democrats in a shaky position politically a way to say in their next campaigns that they voted for a balanced budget amendment, one that protected Social Security benefits.
Asked today where savings could be found to accommodate the Democratic amendment, Senator Feinstein did not reply directly but said, "I really think it's possible to do it."
The Senate Republican leader, Trent Lott of Mississippi, described the Democrats' proposal as merely a guise to conceal their opposition to a workable constitutional amendment.

HEADLINE: Campus Journal;
Princeton's Fees To Rise 3.9% Next Year

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: PRINCETON, N.J.

 BODY:
Undergraduate tuition, room and board at Princeton University will rise 3.9 percent to $29,439 for the 1997-98 school year. It is the second year that Princeton has announced a low rate of increase, and it is the first increase below 4 percent in 30 years. 
 An undergraduate education at Princeton now costs $28,325 a year; that represents a 4.6 percent increase over the previous academic year. Next fall, tuition will rise 4.2 percent to $22,920, while room and board will increase 3 percent to $2,987 and $3,528, respectively.
For graduate students, tuition will rise to $22,920, from $22,000, and room and board will increase about 3 percent.
The new fees were approved by the university's trustees last Saturday, when they adopted Princeton's fiscal 1998 operating budget of $502.6 million.
To keep costs down, the university said, "the faculty will not expand for the foreseeable future," although $1.05 million will be added to the faculty and staff salary pools to allow pay increases above the inflation level.
This year's budget is projected to be in balance, unlike last year's, when the university predicted a budget deficit of nearly $4 million. (AP)

HEADLINE: Noninflationary Pay Report Relieves Markets (for a While)

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
Despite a tight labor market, American workers have not won pay and benefit increases large enough to push inflation up at a faster rate, Government figures showed today.
The report came as a vast relief to inflation-sensitive financial markets, which soared after the Labor Department issued a report that its Employment Cost Index had climbed just eight-tenths of 1 percent in the final three months of 1996, the same as in the first half of the year. But the stock and bond markets retreated later in the day as traders questioned whether the figures were as reliable as initially thought. 
 Economists said the eagerly awaited tally, which many had feared would show a surge of 1 percent or more, virtually extinguished the already remote chance that the Federal Reserve would decide to raise interest rates at its two-day strategy meeting next week. And it could well postpone any increase past the Fed's March meeting unless other data point to the emergence of fresh wage pressures.
"You can probably call off Fed tightening on Feb. 5," said Paul L. Kasriel, chief domestic economist at the Northern Trust Company in Chicago.
Alan Greenspan, the central bank's chairman, and other Fed officials have suggested in recent months that they are paying much greater attention to pay and benefit indicators in judging whether higher interest rates will be needed to help curb the threat of inflation. Mr. Greenspan has specifically cited the Employment Cost Index as one statistic he follows closely.
Although the unemployment rate is flirting with eight-year lows, workers' insecurity about holding on to jobs has so far kept them from pressing hard for higher pay. But Mr. Greenspan warned in Congress last week that "at some point" this implicit bargain in which workers accept smaller raises in return for greater job security "has to come to an end."
The Labor Department's reassuring report sent interest rates tumbling across the board and powered the Dow Jones industrial average to an early advance of nearly 100 points. Subsequently, however, the bond market lost part of its gains and the Dow ended the day with a small decline. The dollar, which gained across the board against other major currencies, remained strong.
Blunting the morning gains was a separate report from the Conference Board, a business-oriented research group, that consumer confidence jumped unexpectedly to the highest point of the current economic expansion that began in March 1991. The report would indicate more buying contributing to a stronger economy and possibly more inflation.
The markets were also unnerved by a rumor that swept Wall Street in midafternoon that the Bureau of Labor Statistics had made an error that caused the index to understate compensation.
The agency's staff was swamped with inquiries, officials said. The talk among traders originated from figures deep in the report showing a decline, six-tenths of 1 percent, in overall compensation for workers in the finance-insurance-real estate category.
Wall Street brokers, many of them flush with record bonuses, found this suspicious and William G. Barron Jr., the bureau's deputy commissioner, said in a late afternoon interview that he did not disagree.
He said the relatively small samples on which the index was based inevitably produced some erratic figures. "We're not supposed to create confusion," Mr. Barron said. "If that is what we're doing here, we should reconsider publishing this particular number."
Since bonuses account for under 3 percent of benefits for this group of workers -- themselves only 8 percent of the private-sector work force -- the effect on the overall index would be no more than one-tenth of a percentage point, B.L.S. officials said.
The report also showed sizable increases in compensation of workers in retail trade, a relatively low-paid category that includes many who began receiving an extra 50 cents an hour from the increase in the Federal minimum wage that took effect Oct. 1. Pay and benefits for those in stores selling general merchandise climbed 1.4 percent -- and 1.7 percent for wages and salaries alone -- with retail trade as a whole up 1 percent for the quarter.
Analysts said they considered it a plus that the index absorbed the initial effects of this upward pressure without accelerating.
Still, economists said that the report was not entirely reassuring since the index has been creeping higher -- 2.9 percent in 1996 compared with 2.7 percent in 1995 -- and there was little prospect that fringe benefits, which have been a moderating force, could help much more.
"The report does not completely put to rest the concerns that in a tight labor market you could see an emergence of accelerating labor costs," said Richard D. Rippe, senior economist at Prudential Securities.
Indeed, there are growing signs of worker militancy, reflected in strike threats by airline pilots and the walkout today of 500 United Automobile Workers who make seats at Johnson Controls plants in Michigan and Ohio.
The Labor Department's report found wages and salaries of civilian workers up eight-tenths of 1 percent for the final 1996 quarter and 3.3 percent for the full year. Benefits rose seven-tenths of 1 percent for the quarter and 2 percent for all of 1996, the skimpiest annual rise since the index was created in 1982.
The Conference Board's confidence index, which had been expected to retreat a couple points, vaulted instead to 116.8 in January from 114.2 in December.
"The rising tide of consumer optimism that was characteristic of 1996 has spilled over into 1997," said Lynn Franco, a board official.
Consumers' appraisal of current conditions climbed 4.1 points, to 139.8, while expectations moved up 1.6 points, to 101.5.
Those who viewed jobs as "hard to get" declined for the third straight month, a sign that job insecurity might be on the wane.
But while consumers may be willing to spend, Mr. Rippe observed, "the key determinant" of the economy's course is what happens to their jobs and incomes.

HEADLINE: Market Place: Starting Today, 10-Year Bets On Inflation;
New U.S. Bond Promises To Match Cost of Living

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
The Government that helped bring America rampant inflation 15 years ago, and that since then seems to have brought it under control, has a deal for you: Today it will offer bonds that provide protection against a general rise in prices. Buyers of the bonds will prosper if inflation returns, but not if it remains quiescent.
The Treasury will auction $7 billion in 10-year inflation-indexed bonds, creating what could eventually become a staple in the investment portfolios of millions of Americans. The bonds are likely to pay an interest rate of slightly above 3.5 percent. 
 That may sound low compared with the 6.64 rate available on normal 10-year Treasury bonds, but it may prove to be quite good, because it is a "real" yield. Inflation can eat away at the returns on normal Treasury bonds, but buyers who hold the new bonds until maturity will be guaranteed that roughly 3.5 percent return, above and beyond any inflation from now until 2007.
Just how good such an investment turns out to be depends on how vigorous inflation becomes. In the 1970's, such a bond would have outperformed almost any other financial investment. But in recent years such bonds would have provided relatively poor returns, compared with gains as high as 30 percent or more in some years on blue-chip stocks.
If inflation averages 3 percent a year for the next decade, about the current rate, an investor would do just about as well with these bonds as with normal 10-year bonds. If it is higher, the new inflation-adjusted bonds would perform better.
Still, for investors seeking to preserve wealth, the bonds offer a level of protection not available to most investors before today. Indeed, one of academia's most prominent advocates of buying stocks, Jeremy J. Siegel, a finance professor at the Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania, has declared himself "most enthusiastic about inflation-indexed bonds." Mr. Siegel, best known as the author of "Stocks for the Long Run," a 1994 book arguing that stocks were the best long-term investment, notes that a major problem with bonds in recent decades has been their exposure to inflation, which can demolish the principal of normal bonds.
The new bonds will work like this:
While they will be sold in initial amounts of $1,000, as with other Treasury notes and bonds, that principal amount will be adjusted daily, based on changes in the Consumer Price Index three months earlier. The increases for each day of the month, when added together, will be enough to offset the monthly rise in prices.
If the bond carries a 3.5 percent coupon rate -- and that will be determined in today's auction -- every six months it will pay half that amount, or 1.75 percent, on the then current principal value. So, assuming that consumer prices continue rising, every six months the coupon payment will be a little higher. At the end of 10 years, the buyer will get back the inflated principal. To the extent the Consumer Price Index is an accurate gauge of inflation, the money returned will be worth just as much as the $1,000 invested in 1997.
And since the interest that was paid also rose with inflation, the investor will have received -- pretax -- a real, inflation-adjusted return of the amount promised when the bond was sold. "For the first time," Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin said, "the United States is offering investors protection from inflation."
Until today, about the only option for investors truly worried about inflation was buying short-term securities like Treasury bills, whose rates tend to rise when inflation fears grow. But their rates are in effect for only a few months and they provide a lower yield than would be expected from longer-term, inflation-indexed securities.
At today's auction, the betting is that most of the buying will be from Wall Street and from two types of institutional investors: pension funds, whose ultimate obligation -- payments to retirees -- is closely tied to inflation, and endowments, which seek to preserve their principal while spending the income.
Individuals who want to buy at today's auction can do so through brokers or directly from any Federal Reserve Bank, until noon, Eastern standard time, today. But the expectation is that relatively little of the issue will go to individuals.
If so, that may be too bad. It appears that the initial auction will effectively be charging what amounts to a very small premium for giving investors insurance against inflation, said Gerald Lucas, a Government bond strategist for Merrill Lynch.
There are three likely reasons for low individual demand. The first is unfamiliarity with the product. The second is that inflation is not very scary right now, and the final one is the tax treatment of the bonds.
An investor must pay tax each year not just on the interest that has been received, but on the rise in the principal value, even though the cash from that increase will not come in for years. As a result, the bonds are probably better suited for tax-deferred retirement accounts.
For now, few investors can put such securities into 401(k) retirement accounts, which normally restrict investor options to a menu that changes infrequently. But both Dreyfus and the Benham group of American Century have announced plans for mutual funds that will buy these securities, so they will become available for at least some such accounts.
One of the major imponderables is whether these bonds will draw money from stock investments. Certainly, the returns on stocks in recent years have vastly outpaced anything that can be expected from inflation-adjusted bonds. The real return -- after inflation -- on the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index in the 10 years that ended in December was a very impressive 11.2 percent a year. Compared with a real rate of 3.5 percent or so to be expected on these bonds, stocks would seem to have it going away.
But such stock returns are far from guaranteed. Indeed, in the 10 years through July 1982 -- a period that ended just as the great bull market of the 1980's was about to begin -- the real return on stocks was a negative 3.7 percent a year.
For people saving for retirement, it might make sense as retirement nears to take some money out of stocks and put it into inflation-protected bonds, since those bonds, unlike normal bonds, are assured of maintaining their purchasing power. On the other hand, since the new bonds are safer than regular bonds, some investors might decide that they could buy some of them and then have more money to risk in the stock market.
For now, these bonds are plain Jane things. But that won't last long. They can be "stripped," that is, sold in pieces like zero-coupon bonds. So if you lay out a certain amount now, you'll know that you will have a given amount -- expressed in 1997 dollars -- when you need the money, perhaps at the time of retirement or when a child enters college.
Soon, too, there may be companies issuing their own inflation-indexed bonds. For most companies, assuming the risk of inflation would seem to be very risky indeed: unlike the Government, corporations do not have their own dollar printing presses. But the expectation is that Wall Street will create derivatives that will allow companies to "swap" out of that obligation, passing on some or all of the inflation risk.
For the Government, of course, these bonds could prove very expensive, even given Washington's ability to print more dollars. Had all old Treasury securities been issued with inflation protection, the national debt -- and its burden on taxpayers -- would be much greater than it is now. But given the relatively small amount of these new bonds that will be outstanding in coming years, that risk is not large.
Indeed, economists say that over time, the cost to the Government of issuing inflation-indexed bonds ought to be lower than that of issuing traditional bonds. That is because most investors are only willing to buy bonds if they receive a premium for the risk that inflation might be higher than expected. By eliminating that risk and offering what is in effect an insurance policy against inflation, the Government should be able to persuade investors to lend it money for less.
One risk investors in these bonds are taking is that the Government will "improve" the Consumer Price Index in a way that would make it rise more slowly. Many economists believe it now overstates inflation and have called for changes. These bonds tread a fine line on that question. If the changes are technical, the investors will have to live with the revised C.P.I. If the changes are deemed political, however, the changes will not affect them. It will be up to the Treasury Department to categorize any changes.
The Treasury is worried about making sure the bonds have adequate liquidity. It wants them to trade with a narrow spread between the price at which dealers are willing to buy and the price at which they want to sell. An adequate supply would encourage that, and that is one reason $7 billion in bonds are being offered, $2 billion more than many had expected. And the Treasury has indicated that in three months it will auction more such bonds, raising the supply even further.
Once the Treasury concludes that there are enough bonds outstanding with maturities in 2007, it is expected to move on and issue 5-year and 30-year inflation-indexed bonds.
In some ways, inflation-indexed bonds hark back to the situation that existed before the Depression, when all Treasury bonds -- and all corporate bonds, as well -- were payable in gold. Then, it was clear that any shrinkage in the value of paper dollars was the problem of the Government, not of the investor. But Congress changed the rules, making bonds redeemable in paper money, and the Supreme Court, in a 5-to-4 decision in 1935, said that was legal.
Since then, all bonds have posed the risk of their principal's eroding because of inflation, and investors have demanded varying interest rate premiums depending on how worried they were that prices would escalate. With the introduction of these bonds, investors have the choice of who should take that risk -- them or the Government.
At the moment, passing on the risk to the Government may be a wise strategy. Inflation worries are low, and it appears that inflation insurance is going to have to be priced very low to sell these bonds. That implies a higher rate of interest than might have been expected. Some people may indeed enjoy getting them while they're not so hot.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "Floating Above Inflation"
The new inflation-indexed 10-year bonds that the Treasury puts on sale today guarantee investors both the safety of their principal and a return that will not be eroded by rises in the cost of living. Investments in stocks and other kinds of bonds may, of course, continue to outstrip the promised returns on the new securities. But the inflation-busting bonds can serve the needs of several kinds of investors:
 
SAVING FOR COLLEGE OR SIMILAR EXPENSES
 These investors can calculate the amount that they will need 10 years in the future and invest now, knowing their money will grow safely toward that goal.
 
SAVING FOR RETIREMENT
 Particularly as part of a tax-deferred retirement account, like an I.R.A. or a 401(k), the bonds provide guaranteed returns without short-term risk.
 
CHARITABLE FOUNDATIONS
 Protects their principal while allowing increase in giving to match the pace of inflation.
 
PENSION FUNDS
 Funds, which must keep abreast of inflation to meet their beneficiaries needs may want to keep some money in inflation-resistant bonds. (Illustrations by Randy Jones)
 
Graph: "Guaranteed, but Not Always Competitive, Returns"
 At the returns that they are expected to yield, the Treasury's new inflation-indexed bonds, which go on sale today, would have generally underperformed regular 10-year bonds as well as stocks over the last 10 years. But they would have done better than three-month Treasury bills, the usual investment for keeping ahead of inflation. And they would have done better than stocks in the weak market years before 1982.
 Graph tracks average annual real rate of return for the S. & P. 500, 10-year Treasury bonds, and 3-month Treasury bills, over the past ten years. Those figures are compared to the expected real yield of the new 10-year inflation bonds. (Sources: Standard & Poor's; Ryan Labs)

HEADLINE: First Bill to Fail in 5 Years Shows Republicans' Rift

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Jan. 28

 BODY:
It is an article of faith here that by the time a bill gets to the floor of the Senate or the Assembly for a vote, it is certain to pass. The leaders of each chamber, who strictly control what bills come before the membership, simply do not allow a vote until they are sure they have enough support for passage.
In fact, the last time the Senate voted down a bill was in 1992, and the Assembly last did it in 1982. But today's Senate vote against allowing casinos in several regions of the state was an exception to the rule, illustrating the unusually deep divisions on the issue among the Republicans who control the Senate. 
 Rather than risk an all-out fight, the majority leader, Senator Joseph L. Bruno of Rensselaer County, who supported the casino bill, did little to enforce the usually ironclad party discipline. Nor did he try to make a deal with Democrats who might have given him the votes he needed.
Mr. Bruno told his members they were free to vote as they wished, though he admitted later that it was hard to give up control. "To see a bill on the floor and hear some of the conversations and know what's happening and to sit there and just watch it happen, I can tell you, is very difficult," he said.
Some of his colleagues suggested that he had no choice, because he could not have held the Republican majority together by force. Others, noting Mr. Bruno's stated ambivalence about gambling, thought he was just unwilling to expend any political capital on a difficult vote.
The five Republican Senators from Nassau County all voted against the bill. They were torn between allegiances to Senator Bruno and to Joseph N. Mondello, the county's powerful Republican leader. Mr. Mondello is president of the Nassau Downs Off-Track Betting Corporation, which could have lost gaming money to casino competition, and he opposed the bill.
Most of the Democratic Senators, particularly those from New York City, walked into the chamber without having taken a public position on the bill, signaling that they were open to a deal for their support. ButMartin Connor of Brooklyn, the minority leader, and other Democrats, said Mr. Bruno made no offer.
Many New York City legislators embraced Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's position, that the bill should be defeated because it would allow casinos in the Catskills and some upstate locations, but not in the city. Senator Alton R. Waldon Jr., a Queens Democrat, said, "I think that the people in the Rockaways deserve a casino," and voted no.
Republican leaders suggested that the Democrats had been influenced by Donald Trump, the real estate mogul and Atlantic City casino owner, who contributed $100,000 last year to two campaign committees controlled by Mr. Connor.
"I'm not going to accuse the minority leader of any impropriety, but a review of contributions to that committee would be appropriate," said Senator Nicholas A. Spano, a Republican from Westchester County who strongly backed the bill. Mr. Connor called the implication "absurd," and noted that Mr. Trump has long been a heavy contributor to politicians of both parties.
Just 3 of the 25 Democratic Senators present voted for the bill. Two of them, Anthony R. Nanula and William T. Stachowski, are from the Buffalo area, and said they needed a casino to keep gambling dollars from flowing across the Canadian border to a casino in Niagara Falls, Ontario. The third Democratic vote came from Efrain Gonzalez Jr., of the Bronx.
Republican Senators insisted that the loss did not undermine Mr. Bruno's authority because he had invested so little in the vote. But some, particularly those from Long Island, were clearly uncomfortable defying their majority leader, and said little during the debate. Senators from upstate, where Republicans dominate, were almost evenly divided on the bill, torn between the economic case for casinos in their regions and arguments that gambling would bring social ills.
The only vehement arguments in favor of casinos were made by lawmakers who felt that their districts had something to gain, economically. Religious groups that had lobbied hard against the bill claimed a measure of credit for its defeat. "In one way or another, the social, moral arguments had an effect," said John M. Kerry, executive director of the New York State Catholic Conference.

HEADLINE: In Reversal, Mt. Sinai Accepts City's Terms in Hospital Pact

BYLINE: By ESTHER B. FEIN 

 BODY:
In an abrupt turnaround, Mount Sinai Medical Center agreed yesterday to link the amount of money it is paid to run two city hospitals to how well it meets certain health care goals and to give the city more control over who works in the hospitals.
Less than a week ago, Mount Sinai rejected similar terms offered by the city, derailing negotiations over a $100 million contract to staff Queens and Elmhurst Hospital Centers. 
 Earlier last week, after negotiations broke down, city officials said they would seek a new partner for the deal through open bidding.
But on Friday, Mount Sinai's president, Dr. John Rowe, asked the hospitals corporation president, Dr. Louis Marcos, to delay the opening of the bidding, saying he thought their differences could still be worked out. And by yesterday, the two sides agreed to a deal even more favorable to the city than the one under consideration last week.
The deal represents a significant victory for the Giuliani administration and the hospitals corporation, which has been determined to take greater control over its 11 public hospitals by demanding more oversight and accountability from its partners before signing new affiliation contracts. Under such contracts, the city pays private medical institutions set fees to provide doctors, nurses and administrators for its public hospitals.
In the deal signed yesterday, the hospitals corporation agreed to pay Mount Sinai $94 million to run the two hospitals in fiscal year 1998 -- $6 million less than it would have paid last week. That amount drops to $91.7 million the next year and may come down about another $20 million if the city decides to shift some technical workers from Mount Sinai's payroll to its own.
More importantly for the city, the new contract gives the hospitals corporation the authority to appoint and remove the medical directors at Queens and Elmhurst -- a point Mount Sinai had refused to cede in the earlier round of negotiations, people familiar with the deal said.
Mount Sinai also agreed to let the corporation levy financial penalties if the hospital failed to meet certain health care benchmarks. For example, the deal requires that 95 percent of the children getting their primary health care at the hospitals must be fully immunized by age 2 or Mount Sinai could face sanctions of up to $20,000 a year.
Earlier, Mount Sinai had said it would only agree to such penalties if the corporation agreed to pay comparable fines for failing to provide adequate support, like sufficient doses of vaccines or timely retrieval of charts.
Mount Sinai dropped that demand and settled instead for an agreement that the hospital would not be penalized if it could prove that it failed to meet its prescribed goals because of problems within the corporation's control.
"This deal proves that the value that H.H.C. brings to the city health care marketplace should not be undervalued," said Dr. Marcos, who had already printed 40 copies of the guidelines for bidding on the project and had taken out ads in The City Record seeking bidders. "Mount Sinai coming back and agreeing to these demands proves that this is a new era for H.H.C., in which we will take an increasingly powerful position."
Dr. Rowe said that he was relieved and pleased that he reached an agreement with the hospitals corporation. He said that for Mount Sinai, the importance of the deal was not the roughly $100 million it stood to lose if the contract went to somebody else.
For his hospital, he said, the decision was based on strategic economic considerations and the longstanding relationship between doctors and the people of Queens.
Mount Sinai depends on the two Queens hospitals to attract customers and give itself a presence in the borough -- important factors to the managed-care companies that increasingly control how health care is delivered in the city.
"We're very pleased with this agreement," Dr. Rowe said. "Look, we would prefer to get more, but we're satisfied."
City officials have long complained that the agreements they have with private medical institutions to run the city's 11 public hospitals have given them little control over how their dollars are spent. Last year, the hospitals corporation paid $505 million to private medical schools to provide staff for the public hospital system.
But all its latest contracts have included provisions giving the corporation greater say over quality and productivity.
Earlier this month, the corporation won a pivotal victory in its drive to gain greater control when Columbia University's College of Physicians and Surgeons agreed to the new terms in its contract to run Harlem Hospital -- the first time one of the major academic medical centers agreed to such a deal.
Although Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani has been outspoken in his desire to get the city out of the business of running hospitals -- saying the private sector can do a better and more efficient job -- some H.H.C. officials are wondering privately whether reports of the corporation's demise have been greatly exaggerated.
These officials are now saying that the willingness of prestigious medical school affiliates to agree to strict productivity terms highlights the value and the power of the city's public hospitals in the current health care market.
"For Mount Sinai to cave means that H.H.C. is stronger than anyone imagined," said one official, who spoke only on condition of anonymity. "This corporation may yet rise from the ashes and assume a key role in reshaping health care in the city."

HEADLINE: Man in the News: Robert Eugene Boyle;
Trusted Trouble-Shooter for Port Authority

BYLINE: By BRETT PULLEY 

 BODY:
When Robert E. Boyle was named chief executive of the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center two years ago, skeptics wondered whether he was cut out for the task of eliminating the mismanagement, labor problems and mob corruption that had long consumed the state-run center. "I can't wait to prove the skeptics wrong," he said at the time.
For the most part, that is what he did, as he confronted the unions, lowered costs for exhibitors and improved service at the city's primary convention hall. Yesterday, his efforts were rewarded, as Gov. George E. Pataki of New York and Gov. Christine Todd Whitman of New Jersey selected Mr. Boyle to be Executive Director of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey. 
 "Bob Boyle transformed the Javits Center from a mob-infested facility burdened by high costs and poor service into one of the premier convention centers in the world, with a seemingly unending list of shows and exhibits," Mr. Pataki said as he announced the selection of Mr. Boyle, a longtime friend and political supporter, to replace George J. Marlin as head of the Port Authority.
In his new job, Mr. Boyle, a 60-year-old construction industry entrepreneur from the Governor's hometown of Peekskill, will now head a much larger operation with a vastly different, and possibly more challenging, set of problems.
As the guardian of the region's gateways and the developer of major real estate projects, the Port Authority has been criticized by officials in both New York and New Jersey as a bloated bureaucracy whose focus has moved too far beyond its central purpose: to run airports, waterways and transportation links. The agency is in the midst of downsizing its workforce and shifting its focus from real estate development to improving the region's transportation infrastructure, including dredging its port channels and expanding its three major airports, and selling or privatizing management of real estate holdings like the World Trade Center.
"The biggest challenge is the next few years is going to be putting the port back in Port Authority," said Jon Orcutt, associate director of the Tri-State Transportation Campaign, a New York-based policy watchdog. He criticized the agency as being "all over the map in its activities," and said that Mr. Boyle must rein in the agency, focus on issues like dredging the port channel to allow for bigger ships and more trade, and improve cooperation between New York and New Jersey.
The tension between the two neighboring states has often lurked just beneath the surface of relationships at the Port Authority. Mrs. Whitman, who was angered two years ago when Mr. Pataki announced his decision to appoint Mr. Marlin without consulting her, said yesterday she was pleased with the selection of Mr. Boyle.
But in a statement, she took care to note that Lewis M. Eisenberg, the chairman of the agency, whom she appointed, would continue to play an integral role at the agency. Mr. Boyle's "management skills will augment the leadership of Port Authority Chairman Lew Eisenberg," Mrs. Whitman said.
Yesterday, Mr. Boyle referred to "the number of significant issues that are dogging the Port Authority" as "a list too numerous to mention." In a telephone interview from his office in the convention center, he said his first priorities at the Port Authority would be to assess the relationship between New York and New Jersey and to consider how far the current downsizing at the agency should go. Since August 1995, the agency has reduced the number of employees to 7,300 from 9,100.
"Corporate America has been doing that kind of downsizing for years and increasing productivity at the same time," Mr. Boyle said. "You always hear criticism that its too much, too fast. Sometimes it's true. Sometimes it's a little true. Sometimes it's not true at all."
When Mr. Boyle arrived at the Javits Center in April 1995, his experience had been limited to running small and medium-sized companies. Although he had been widely credited with turning around the Hudson Valley Hospital Center, where he was volunteer chairman, not all of his business dealings had fared so well.
After graduating from La Salle University in Philadelphia, Mr. Boyle worked for a New York City concrete company, where he advanced in administration and operation. By the mid-1960's, he was part owner of Westchester Concrete, a company in Tarrytown, N.Y. As part owner of the company for more than two decades, he was financially successful, but one of his partners was accused by a Federal judge of dealing with a union official who was linked to organized crime.
Mr. Boyle, however, has said that he was better prepared for the Javits Center challenge because he had successfully fended off organized criminals in his businesses.
A condominium project that Mr. Boyle attempted to develop in Vermont also went into bankruptcy midway through construction.
As chairman of the hospital board, Mr. Boyle invited Mr. Pataki, a state assemblyman, to join the board. He became treasurer of Mr. Pataki's campaign for governor, and the two were so close that Mr. Boyle gave a $10,000 personal loan to Mr. Pataki in recent years.
At the Javits Center, Mr. Boyle trimmed administrative personnel and lowered labor costs. One of the other major challenges he faced in the $125,000-a-year job was the need to make the center larger and more attractive to conventions, which create far more money for the local economy than the trade exhibitions that currently dominate the center's calendar. In response, Mr. Boyle recently created a plan to expand the center.
The expansion proposal is still in its infancy, but Mr. Boyle will be in a position to help see it through. Even after assuming his $170,000-a-year post at the Port Authority, he will retain his position as chairman of the Javits Center's board.
"Bob has proven he is a good manager and can solve problems," said Charles Gargano, vice chairman of the authority. "I don't think there's a guessing game anymore about whether Bob can do the job."

HEADLINE: A Consensus Is Sought On Coding

BYLINE: By JOHN MARKOFF 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Jan. 28

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration's top overseas representative on cryptography -- or data scrambling -- told a group of skeptical computer industry executives and technical experts today that important allies support the Administration's position that governments should have access-coded computer files and digital communications.
The speech by David L. Aaron, made on the opening day of a computer security conference, was part of a renewed effort by the Administration to gain support for its efforts to control data-scrambling technologies, which are increasingly viewed as crucial components of electronic commerce and communications. 
 The Administration named Mr. Aaron, who is officially the special envoy to the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, to the position late last year as part of its effort to establish an international consensus on use of the scrambling technologies.
The Administration is trying to persuade other governments to support its effort to create a de facto global standard that would force computer users to submit the keys to their codes for scrambling data to a separate authority, which would hold them in escrow.
Advocates for privacy rights have opposed such proposals because, they say, they compromise privacy and can easily be misused by governments and outside individuals and organizations.
The Administration has treated data encryption software as a weapon and restricted exports, angering American companies that believe the restrictions on exporting data-scrambling hardware and software will give competitive advantages to foreign concerns.
Indeed, today's speech by Mr. Aaron renewed the debate, which last year was heard out in Congress and in Federal courts, where the export regulations have been challenged.
Underscoring the likelihood that the debate will not go away soon, Senator Conrad Burns, Republican of Montana, said today that he would soon reintroduce legislation intended to limit the Administration's ability to control the export of the technology as well as efforts to enforce escrow systems that would create electronic backdoors to permit Government monitoring of data communications.
Mr. Burns, speaking to the conference via satellite, said he did not believe that the Government's efforts to control the spread of data-scrambling technology were workable. "I just don't think it will work," he said. "If bad people want to do bad things to good people, they're sure not going to give their keys to the F.B.I."
Mr. Aaron said the Government had made a good-faith effort to balance the interests of all the parties in the dispute, adding that the Government had significantly altered its position to accommodate business interests.
"I want to stress that this policy in no way seeks to expand the powers of law enforcement nor reduce the privacy protections of individuals," he said in the speech to a conference sponsored by RSA Data Security.
The president of the Information Technology Association of America, a computer and communications trade group, sent a letter to Mr. Aaron today that was critical of current draft guidelines of the Organization of Economic Cooperation and Development. The guidelines, which are intended to develop an international consensus on data scrambling restrictions, are supposed to be in place in the spring.
"We believe there is a clear lack of balance in the most recent draft of the guidelines," wrote Harris N. Miller, the president of the association. "This lack of balance could have a detrimental effect on international trade and the world's ability to use the Internet for electronic commerce."
In an interview today, Mr. Aaron disputed the industry's view that the efforts to control cryptographic technology are being outstripped by the pace of the technology. "When I talk to other governments," he said, "they still don't feel that the cat is out of the bag."
Mr. Aaron said he had discussed cryptography issues with representatives of France, Britain, Germany, Belgium and Canada.
