HEADLINE: Louis E. Martin, 84, Aide To 3 Democratic Presidents

BYLINE: By NEIL A. LEWIS 

 BODY:
Louis E. MartinLouis E. Martin, whose work behind the scenes as an adviser to three Democratic Presidents and whose efforts to bring more blacks into government earned him the sobriquet, "the godfather of black politics," died on Monday at a hospital in Orange, Calif. He was 84.
Mr. Martin served as the deputy chairman of the Democratic National Committee during the Administrations of President John F. Kennedy and Lyndon B. Johnson, and was widely viewed by both Presidents as a valued counselor and liaison to the black political community. President Jimmy Carter appointed him as a special Presidential assistant. 
 In 1967, as an adviser to Johnson, Mr. Martin was influential in pressing him to name Thurgood Marshall as the first black Justice on the Supreme Court.
In the 1960 Presidential campaign, Mr. Martin helped persuade Kennedy to place a telephone call to Coretta Scott King to express dismay over the jailing of her husband, the Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. The phone call was widely credited with helping Kennedy win a major portion of the black vote in the general election that year.
Mr. Martin was able to wield considerable influence inconspicuously, his friends said.
Eddie Williams, the president of the Joint Center for Political and Economic Studies, a research organization in Washington, said it was surprising that Mr. Martin was largely unknown to the public at large, given his wide-ranging influence in the White House and his role in the development of black political power in the Democratic Party.
"One reason for this is that in Washington, he was the consummate political insider," Mr. Williams said. "He traversed the corridors of power for many years without calling attention to himself and his achievements."
Mr. Williams said that Mr. Martin was first called the "godfather of black politics" by The Washington Post, and that the nickname stuck.
Mr. Martin was born in Shelbyville, Tenn., the son of an American woman and a Cuban-born doctor, but grew up in Savannah, Ga., where he met his wife of 60 years, Gertrude Scott. He attended Fisk University and graduated from the University of Michigan.
He began his professional career as a journalist with The Chicago Defender, a black newspaper, and within a year moved to Detroit to become the editor of another black newspaper, The Michigan Chronicle.
In an interview, his wife recalled that during his time at The Chronicle he became an active Democrat.
Local preachers were Republicans because they had connections with the Ford Motor Company's management "that would allow them to recommend workers to the plant," Mrs. Martin said, noting that the arrangement enhanced their own prestige.
But she said that her husband decided that the Democratic Party was a more appropriate vehicle for advancing the cause of organized labor, and he made it his mission to express that view in his newspaper.
Mr. Martin helped found the National Newspaper Publishers Association, a group of black publishers.
In 1970, he was a founder of the Joint Center for Political and Economic Studies to provide technical assistance and support for black officeholders and scholars across the country, and served as its first chairman for eight years.
Mr. Martin first came to Washington after he was recruited by a Kennedy brother-in-law, R. Sargent Shriver, to work in the Kennedy Presidential campaign. After Kennedy was assassinated in Dallas on Nov. 22, 1963, Mr. Martin made a remarkably easy transition as a Johnson adviser, a transition few other Kennedy aides would make.
Clifford L. Alexander Jr., a White House counsel under Johnson, described Mr. Martin as his mentor and recalled that he and the President enjoyed an easy relationship. "They talked to each other in the shorthand of experienced political pros," Mr. Alexander said.
Recounting an incident that took place shortly after Johnson's landslide victory over Barry Goldwater in 1964, Mr. Alexander said the President summoned him and Mr. Martin to the Oval Office to ask what percentage of the black vote he had won. "Ninety-six percent Mr. President," Mr. Martin replied. Johnson then asked balefully, "What about the other 4 percent?" Mr. Martin knew the President well enough, Mr. Alexander said, and simply laughed.
Among the leading black public figures whom Mr. Martin helped raise to prominence was Vernon E. Jordan, now a close adviser to President Clinton. Mr. Martin helped recruit Mr. Jordan to head the National Urban League.
Besides his wife, Mr. Martin is survived by five daughters, Trudy Hatter of Diamond Bar, Calif., Anita Martin of Rockville, Md., Dr. Toni Martin of Berkeley, Calif., Linda Purkiss of Oakland, Calif., and Lisa Grant of San Francisco. He is also survived by 11 grandchildren and 2 great-grandchildren.

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 1; Editorial Desk

HEADLINE: The Berlin Wall and China

 BODY:
Bill Clinton offered a revealing glimpse into his thinking about China when he predicted this week that liberty would eventually triumph there, much as freedom brought down the Berlin wall. The demise of Communism may come someday in China, but it would be a mistake to adopt a passive American policy based on that optimistic prospect, as Mr. Clinton seems to be doing. There is already abundant evidence suggesting that Communism in China is not dying but is instead mutating into a new form that tolerates economic liberties while still suffocating political freedom.
Mr. Clinton's press conference remarks, read closely, were an argument against pushing China hard on human rights and internal political reform. The problem with this analysis is that it indulges Chinese repression and may be taken by Beijing as a sign of American weakness. Likening China to Europe, when it comes to the course of Communism, is a risky approach. 
 America does not face a new cold war with China, and does not need a containment policy to deal with Beijing. But Washington does need to be more assertive about its interests, more demanding in seeking an end to China's human rights abuses and less willing to sacrifice American principles for American commerce in China.
There is a good chance that Mr. Clinton's stewardship of American foreign policy will be judged in substantial measure by his handling of China as it takes its place among the great powers. So too will the leadership of Vice President Al Gore, who plans to play a larger role on China in the months ahead, and that of Secretary of State Madeleine Albright, whose commitment to human rights and democracy faces its severest test in China.
Mr. Clinton's faith in the power of trade and information to liberate China is stirring but unrealistic and his analogy to the Berlin wall oversimplified. The increasing flow of information and the allure of free markets helped undermine authoritarian governments in Eastern Europe, but these regimes did not voluntarily surrender power. Communism in Eastern European states collapsed abruptly because the Soviet Union declined to sustain it any longer with force and economic aid, and most of East Europe's corrupt regimes lacked the means and killer instinct to terrorize their citizens. In the case of the Soviet Union itself, economic stagnation, an explosion of nationalism and political reforms initiated by Mikhail Gorbachev played a decisive role in the demise of Communism.
Chinese conditions are quite different. The country, unlike those behind the Berlin wall, is gaining economic strength, not losing it. Communism is not maintained by an outside power but by the Chinese Government itself, with the assistance of the military. Suppressed ethnic nationalism is not an explosive force. With China's leaders maneuvering for power in anticipation of the death of Deng Xiaoping, there is little likelihood of political liberalization anytime soon.
The economic reforms initiated by Mr. Deng in 1979 have indisputably introduced free markets in China, and prosperity has spread to its major cities, producing the beginnings of a middle class. But even China's economic experimentation has been controlled by a Government determined to maintain its political authority.
There is little sign that China's booming economy has produced concrete political change, or even the intangible spirit of liberty that Mr. Clinton considers inevitable. China has done its best to restrict the flow of information to citizens, even limiting access to financial data and the Internet.
In recent months the Government has rounded up political dissidents and consigned them to long prison terms after carefully managed trials. The State Department reports that by the end of last year, all active dissidents in China had been jailed or exiled, a disheartening record that even the Soviet Union could not match once Stalin was gone.
On the clearest current question of Chinese intentions, the return of Hong Kong to China this summer, Beijing has left little doubt it will strip Hong Kong of its liberties, even if that diminishes the economic benefits of the transfer to China.
It is welcome to hear Mr. Clinton acknowledge that "constructive engagement" with China has yielded disappointing progress on human rights issues. Unhappily, he seems to be under the illusion that future results will be different.

HEADLINE: Tax Credits for Welfare Hires

 BODY:
President Clinton's proposal to give tax credits to companies that hire long-term welfare recipients can modestly boost employment and income of this hard-to-hire group. The positive impact is unlikely to be large, but tax credits are probably the most that Congress is willing to do to protect those who are in harm's way of the new welfare law, which imposes time limits on Federal cash assistance.
Tax credits for employers to hire disadvantaged workers have been used on and off since the 1960's without much success. Indeed, in some cases such programs have hurt the intended beneficiaries -- by stigmatizing the subsidized applicant as a problem hire that employers would be better off avoiding. The Administration is well aware of the mixed record of tax credits and has designed its proposal to avoid the major pitfalls. 
 The plan would give employers a tax cut of 50 percent of the first $10,000 in wages paid to welfare recipients who have been on the rolls for at least 18 months. The credit is larger than that provided by previous programs, giving Mr. Clinton's plan a better chance of enticing employers to participate. He would avoid stigmatizing most former welfare recipients in the marketplace because his plan subsidizes only long-term recipients of welfare. At worst the Clinton proposal will fail to create many new hires, in which case it will not cost Washington much money.
There is, however, a chance the plan will do some good. Prof. Lawrence Katz of Harvard has shown that some tax-subsidy programs during the 1970's and 80's boosted employment of disadvantaged young adults by several percentage points, and raised earnings of adult women by 15 percent and of adult men by 10 percent. Tax subsidies worked better when combined with direct spending on job creation in areas of high unemployment. Mr. Clinton takes heed of this cautiously optimistic reading of the past by proposing to turn over about $3 billion to localities where there is high unemployment and welfare dependency to create private and public work slots. The President is also lobbying churches and nonprofit organizations to create jobs for welfare recipients.
But the plan will be hard pressed to register even the modest gains of the best-run programs from the past. Focusing on job applicants with the poorest skills and employment records will make it hard to score many victories.
The truth is that Mr. Clinton, who signed the bill that will deny cash assistance to long-term welfare recipients, has few other options to soften the blow. A Republican-led Congress is not about to spend enough money on public-sector jobs or any other direct relief to accommodate the number of people who will exhaust cash assistance before they find private-sector work. Mr. Clinton hopes to sell the G.O.P. on a tax cut for employers. For now, that is about all he can do.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
New Inflation-Indexed Note Receives a Strong Response

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The Treasury's first auction of inflation-indexed securities went better than expected yesterday, with strong interest from large institutional investors.
The auction took place on a day when Treasury prices rose on unexpected weakness in a durable goods report, and then steadied as the Treasury released the results. 
The price of the 30-year bond rose 8/32, to 94 30/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 6.90 percent, from 6.92 percent on Tuesday.
Initially it was the uncertainty over investor interest in the new 10-year inflation-indexed note that kept a cap on Treasury prices over all.
The inflation-adjusted yield was expected to start at about 3.50 percent to 3.75 percent, with pension funds, insurance companies, and some mutual funds among those seen bidding at yesterday's sale.
Sold in increments of $1,000, the 10-year notes will pay more principal at maturity if inflation accelerates, and thus protect the value of the investment. At the auction, the notes were sold at a high yield of 3.449 percent, with the median yield at 3.40 percent. By the close of trading the yield was down to 3.39 percent.
Lawrence H. Summers, Deputy Secretary of the Treasury, said he was pleased with the market reception, with a lot probably coming from large institutions. "It was a good start to what will be a long process of diverse inflation protection."
Patrick R. Dimick, a Treasury market analyst and a trader at UBS Securities, said there was a lot of confusion in the market about how to handle the indexed-linked bonds."Market participants are very uncomfortable about how to trade and especially hedge these indexed-linked securities," he said, "and that made for volatile trade in the market after the auction results were released. And they really still don't know."
Theodore Ake, government trading manager at Everen Securities Inc., in Chicago, said, "It will take a few months of trading before everyone gets a real handle on these securities,"
Treasury prices received a liftearlier after the Commerce Department reported that durable goods -- expensive items that are expected to last more than three years -- fell 1.7 percent in December.
Maury N. Harris, chief economist at Paine Webber, noted that it was the second consecutive month that durable goods orders fell 1.7 percent and that the latest decline in December was much weaker than the consensus estimate for an increase of 1.1 percent.
The report helped ease concerns that the economy was growing at a fast enough pace that would prompt the Federal Reserve to raise short-term interest rates to head off inflation.
On Tuesday, a report on wage increases in the fourth quarter came in within expectations, helping to ease investors' anxieties on rates.
Following are the results of yesterday's auction of new 10-year inflation- indexed notes:

HEADLINE: U.S. Report on Human Rights Faults China, Nigeria and Cuba

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Despite American efforts, from jawboning to sanctions, human rights performance worsened last year in China, Nigeria, Cuba and Myanmar, according to the Administration's annual report on human rights in 193 countries, which is to be released on Thursday.
According to the document's "overview" and some of the reports, Russia's record is "mixed." Relatively free presidential elections and the withdrawal of Russian troops from Chechnya were offset by discrimination against minorities, hazing of military recruits, uneven legal reforms, little investigation of pressure on journalists and a worsening of already harsh prison conditions. 
 In some other former Soviet states where Washington has pushed democratization, progress has been uneven. In Belarus, the report says, there was "serious backsliding" toward dictatorship, while in Armenia, "progress toward democracy was set back" by "flawed elections."
In Kazakstan and Kyrgyzstan, the report notes a sharp increase of presidential power to the detriment of other branches of government, while Uzbekistan, Turkmenistan and Tajikistan "lag even further behind in the development of democracy and respect for human rights."
These reports, noted for their objectivity even by critics of Mr. Clinton's human rights policies, take aim at both friends and foes -- criticizing Germany, for instance, for increased discrimination against the Church of Scientology.
In Nigeria, the human rights performance of the military council headed by Gen. Sani Abacha "remains dismal," the report says. The regime nullified March elections and security forces arrested or killed opponents, including legislators, and tortured suspects.
Cuba, a regular target, is called "a totalitarian anachronism, where human rights deteriorated in 1996."
Officials argue that the annual reports are gaining importance in American policy decisions and that human rights is a formal criterion for imposing trade sanctions, providing aid and selling arms.
For example, Washington bans the sale of arms that can be used for crowd-control to Indonesia, which the report calls "strongly authoritarian," for its restrictions on opposition and its stifling of dissent. Congressional critics of Indonesia's record are blocking Mr. Clinton's wish to sell F-16 fighters to that country.
But officials admit that with complex, powerful or resource-rich countries like China, Russia or Saudi Arabia, American interests require that human-rights concerns do not "dominate or distort" a "multi-faceted relationship."
For China, the report measures the failure of Mr. Clinton's policy of "constructive engagement" to improve human rights, as Mr. Clinton admitted at a news conference Tuesday.
China's "economic pragmatism and increasingly robust ties of trade and commerce with the United States and many other countries" have not prevented or ameliorated "widespread and well-documented human rights abuses," the report states.
No dissidents were active at year's end, the report says. "All public dissent against party and Government was effectively silenced by intimidation, exile or the imposition of prison terms, administrative detention or house arrest."
During 1996, the report says, "Hong Kong's civil liberties and political institutions were threatened by restrictive measures taken by the Chinese Government in anticipation of Hong Kong's reversion to Chinese sovereignty" in July.
Charlene Barshefsky, the Administration's top trade official, said today that the United States would block China from entering the World Trade Organization, a key goal for the Chinese leadership this year, if Beijing continues to block the import of American agricultural goods.
Ms. Barshefsky, the country's acting trade representative since last May, made the remark during a Senate confirmation hearing on her nomination to the position.
Although Myanmar formally ended the house arrest of the pro-democracy leader Daw Aung San Suu Kyi, the military regime "stepped up its 'rolling repression' and systematic violation of human rights," the report says.
A law signed by Mr. Clinton mandates a ban on American investment in Myanmar, formerly Burma, if Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi is harmed, significantly harassed or re-arrested. Officials say the White House is close to activating that law, which is sharply opposed by United States companies with interests in Myanmar, like the oil company Unocal.
Washington has already imposed a ban on visas for Burmese leaders, suspended economic aid, blocked arms sales and held up loans to Myanmar from international institutions.

HEADLINE: Mr. Kravis's New Neighborhood;
Financier Leverages Influence for Poor Urban Areas

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
James E. Quinn routinely travels to some of the most rarefied shopping districts in the world for his job as an executive vice president at Tiffany & Company. Rodeo Drive in Beverly Hills, for example, is typical Tiffany turf. But until this month, Mr. Quinn -- though born and raised in New York -- had never once been to the Bedford-Stuyvesant section of Brooklyn, where shopping opportunities for even basic needs are scant if not entirely absent.
Mr. Quinn went there to scout potential retailing sites and examine vacant lots and traffic flow patterns -- not for Tiffany, which has no plans to open soon in Bed-Stuy, but for the financier Henry R. Kravis, who does. 
 Mr. Kravis, best known for multibillion-dollar takeovers and leveraged buyouts as a founding partner in the firm of Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, is chairman of a new and still evolving entity, the New York City Investment Fund, which incorporated last fall with the intent of creating jobs in New York, especially in poor and minority neighborhoods.
The fund, with $57 million in assets, is minuscule by KKR standards; Mr. Kravis also has on his desk a new $5.8 billion corporate buyout pool, for instance. The New York fund has yet to invest a dollar and its board of directors will not even have its first meeting until next week.
But business leaders say that as a new entrant on the New York political and economic stage, the Kravis fund will be one to watch -- no quiet issuer of charity checks, but a powerful, if diffuse, organization in its own right, and a new vehicle of influence for Mr. Kravis.
"If you said, 'Who is going to be the Rockefeller of the next 30 to 40 years?' Henry is one of the people you would think of," said Russell L. Carson, a general partner of Welsh, Carson, Anderson & Stowe, a private investment firm that is one of the fund's investors.
The New York fund also seems likely to emerge as a barometer of economic power relationships in New York. The fund's investors include most of the big Wall Street securities firms, which have been intimately tied to KKR and Mr. Kravis through years of hugely profitable deals. As Wall Street has boomed and other old-line New York industries like manufacturing have withered, the securities industry has also played a larger role in the regional economy, and some investors say that a flexing of new muscle helps explain the fund's emergence now.
But the latticework of board members -- from top executives like David H. Komansky of Merrill Lynch to Walter V. Shipley of Chase Manhattan, to lower-level volunteers like Mr. Quinn -- are what makes the fund distinctive. The primary notion, Mr. Kravis said, was to take business executives who mostly focus on the world and turn their focus back to New York.
"We said, 'Don't just give us the money,' " Mr. Kravis said in describing the formation of the fund, which started last fall with $50 million in assets -- $1 million each from 50 investors and institutions, including Tiffany -- and has grown by seven investors and $7 million. "We want the money, but we also want your time, and we want your effort, and we want the effort of your people."
Mr. Carson said he thought the power of the fund would be the cadres of corporate experts who can negotiate deals, pick sites and call on contacts nationwide. "The level of brainpower is all out of proportion to the level of funds," he said.
Goldman, Sachs, for example, in addition to its $1 million investment, has provided access to its investment analysis department. Merrill Lynch offered its experts in retailing. E. M. Warburg, Pincus & Company enlisted executives to search the information technology sector for leads. Tiffany provided Mr. Quinn, among others.
But there is also an inevitable tension in the mixture of capitalism and philanthropy.
The fund intends to invest with the goal of getting its money back and maybe even making a profit. But if an investment makes money without creating jobs, it fails an important test.
The fund, which operates under the New York City Partnership and the Chamber of Commerce, also wants to avoid buying into a company that could go to an ordinary venture capitalist or bank. Charity cases will be avoided because they generally do not grow into giant employers or provide returns that can be reinvested.
The kind of jobs a company would create is also important. Many of the most promising sectors of the New York economy, Mr. Kravis said, involve high technology. But high tech jobs are often high skill jobs that often do not benefit the neighborhoods the fund wants to help.
"Are we doing social good for a community?" Mr. Kravis said. "Are we we taking into consideration issues for the stakeholders other than just making a profit? It has to be both."
Mr. Quinn's trip to Brooklyn with the fund's retailing committee showed some of the strains of Mr. Kravis's new chemistry. The participants, from Merrill Lynch and Citibank and other institutions, were all experts from a world of national brands and chains that has barely touched this part of New York, where commerce remains small scale and intensely local, and when they climbed out of the van with their cellular phones and their gray broadcloth coats, some looked lost.
Some of the tour members were familiar with the mechanisms of economic development, empowerment zones and tax abatements, while others were taking their first steps to learn. Perhaps the only thing they all had in common was Mr. Kravis, and even then the link was tenuous. Mr. Quinn, for example, has yet to be introduced to the fund's chairman.
Certainly, there have been periods in New York when one person did seem able to harness the city's business energy for coherent purpose -- notably the 1960's and 1970's under David Rockefeller.
And Mr. Rockefeller was in fact Mr. Kravis's first $1 million investor. He said in an interview that Mr. Kravis came to see him about 10 years ago to ask for advice about how to contribute something to society.
"When he started making huge sums of money, he had absolutely no background in giving," Mr. Rockefeller said. "He went around and said, 'I haven't any experience in this -- how do you go about it?' "
Mr. Rockefeller said he told Mr. Kravis to get involved personally: don't just be a money man; get out and lead. "I don't think it's necessarily because of my advice, but he has done that," he said.
Economists and investors also say that the fund has benefited from a restructuring of the city's economy in the 1990's. The upheaval in banking, for instance, especially the push into new financial services, has changed perspectives at old-line institutions like the Dime Bancorp, which has diversified from its traditional home mortgage lending business into new areas like brokerage services and business loans.
The new business focus meant that Mr. Kravis's pitch -- to invest in the creation of jobs rather than in more traditional nonprofit work like affordable housing -- found a more receptive audience, said the bank's chairman, James M. Large Jr.
The securities firms in New York have also moved more and more in recent years toward becoming investors themselves. Many became creditors as bank lending dried up in the recession of the early 1990's, and then moved into real estate as prices bottomed out. That makes them think more like entrepreneurs. And with Mr. Kravis's proven record, fund investors say, money follows money.
"You always take Henry's phone call," said Deryck C. Maughan, the chairman and chief executive officer of Salomon Brothers. "But it's being part of New York that makes us commit our time and capital."
A crucial factor of Mr. Kravis's leadership, Mr. Maughan said, is that it is not allied with a particular securities firm or bank. The fees and commissions that spill over from KKR's deals are widely shared.
"Henry's leadership is important, not so much because of the fees, but because he is an independent, he can rally the the banks and the corporations on an equal basis," Mr. Maughan said.
Mr. Kravis said that the power of his firm and his name is perhaps part of what has helped attract investors and raise their expectations. But the larger motivation, he said, is the sense of possibility that he and the other investors feel about New York's future.
The decrease in crime has helped, he said. Just as important is a shift toward the private sector in getting things done -- demonstrated in everything from the business-improvement districts that help clean New York's streets to the declaration by President Clinton that the era of big government is over. Several members of the fund said that Mr. Kravis represented that new sense of assertion.
"We didn't want to be involved in one these massive civic ventures that just spins its wheels and never gets near the goal line," said Mr. Komansky at Merrill Lynch. "Part of the faith in Henry is not only that the goals are verbalized, but that he'll be able to see them through to completion."

WHERE THE MONEY COMES FROM
 $1 million each from 57 companies and individuals, committed for at least 15 years.
 
WHERE IT WILL GO
 To companies and projects that can create jobs in blighted neighborhoods.
 
HOW PROJECTS WILL BE EVALUATED
 Potential to create jobs. Benefits to neighborhoods and the city economy. The ability to pay back the investment within five years. Diversification of the fund's portfolio by location and by industry.
 
WHAT WILL NOT BE CONSIDERED
 Subsidies or grants. Very small businesses that do not offer potential for increases in jobs and growth. (pg. B1)

HEADLINE: With Defeat of Gambling Initiative, Catskill Winter Turns Gloomier

BYLINE: By ANDREW C. REVKIN 

DATELINE: MONTICELLO, N.Y., Jan. 29

 BODY:
Mark Kutsher was going through the motions of running his family's 420-room Catskills resort, inspecting the ski lift, the steamy indoor swimming pool and the darkened Stardust auditorium. But his mind was still focused on the day before, when he sat in the gallery of the State Senate chambers in Albany, watching a dream evaporate.
Kutsher's Country Club and other resorts, businesses and government offices in the sleepy snow-covered hills here were fixated on the prospect that a long decline in tourism might be reversed if the Legislature finally put before New York voters a referendum to legalize gambling. 
 Local officials and business owners, he said, had pushed off and on for 30 years for legalized gambling to compete with Atlantic City and offer tourists a new reason to come to the Catskills instead of flying to Cancun.
Until Tuesday morning, they felt they had the votes. But as Mr. Kutsher and a small Catskills contingent watched the political tussle below, their hopes were buried under a landslide defeat.
Today, all around the Catskills, politicians and citizens were shaking off the shock and scurrying to find a new path to economic renewal. Many people are focusing on the possibility that Monticello Raceway could be transformed into an Indian-run casino. Aides to Gov. George E. Pataki said today that he was willing to allow such a plan, and that officials had been talking with the Oneida Indians about it. And the St. Regis Mohawks, who have also discussed a Monticello casino with the Governor's office, said they planned to renew their effort.
On Mr. Kutsher's desk, the desk of Monticello's Mayor and the desk of the president of the Monticello track here, copies of the local paper, The Times Herald-Record, summarized the news in a two-word headline: "No Dice."
Monticello's Mayor, James F. Kenny, said this village of 6,200 was desperate for the opportunity offered by gambling. He pointed up Broadway, where the sidewalks were once thick with visitors until midnight, but where movie theaters are now vacant, with the words "For Rent" replacing film titles on the marquees.
"In the 1990 Census, Monticello was 63 percent low-income or below," he said. "And it hasn't gotten better. We're going to be another Appalachia up here if nothing changes." With New York State now bordered by casinos in Connecticut, Ontario and New Jersey, people who might otherwise come to the Catskills for recreation and a little luck are now headed elsewhere.
Mr. Kutsher, 49, said the defeat was particularly frustrating because it appeared to have resulted not so much from a consensus about the merits of gambling, but instead from regional battles. Lawmakers from New York City and Long Island had formed a bipartisan bloc against the bill, which would have limited gambling to the Catskills and four other resort areas to the north.
"When your livelihood depends on it and you're sitting there watching, it makes you sick," Mr. Kutsher said. "This is an issue that truly should have gone to the people. You get enough people together and they display an innate intelligence that the Senate doesn't seem to have."
There was at least one place in Monticello where hopes were not dashed -- Monticello Raceway, an ailing 39-year-old harness racing track that has been seeking a casino deal with the St. Regis Mohawks.
With no chance of a referendum on gambling before 1999, the raceway casino project has become the only game in town. Cliff Ehrlich, president of the raceway, answered a stream of phone calls this afternoon as the county's eyes turned toward the track as its last best chance.
The track had once drawn summertime crowds of 40,000 people, but now attracts 500 people on a good day, Mr. Ehrlich said. The defeated bill would have legalized slot machines there and at similar tracks like Yonkers Raceway, but an Indian casino could compete directly with the big casinos in Atlantic City, Connecticut and Ontario, he said.
Edward Smoke, the chief executive of the St. Regis Mohawks, said the tribe planned to renew talks about the raceway casino with Governor Pataki's office now that the Legislature had acted.
Mr. Ehrlich said that if the Federal Bureau of Indian Affairs accepted the casino plan -- the review is under way in Washington -- all that would be needed would be the approval of Governor Pataki.
At Kutsher's, as the staff prepared for an influx of 700 guests on Thursday, Mr. Kutsher said that if casino gambling were not approved, the region would need some state help.
"There are so many assets and such beauty up here, and so many jobs at stake -- 400 jobs at Kutsher's alone," Mr. Kutsher said.
He said there would continue to be a slow decline, although Kutsher's, at least, would find a way to get by. They were always trying something new -- whether mountain bike trails or a summer sports camp for children -- Mr. Kutsher said.
"We're celebrating our 90th anniversary this year," he said. "This resort has survived world wars and depressions. We'll get through this, too."

GRAPHIC: Photos: There are hopes in the Catskills for an Indian-run casino at Monticello Raceway. HEADLINE: Gambling Vote Imperils Yonkers Raceway

BYLINE: By RICHARD SANDOMIR 

 BODY:
Yonkers Raceway, the 99-year-old fixture of the harness racing world, may be sold because of the New York State Senate's rejection of a constitutional amendment to legalize casino gambling, which would have let the track put 5,000 slot machines into a renovated grandstand, raceway officials said yesterday.
"This is a tremendous blow to Yonkers," Frank Drucker, the raceway's director of public relations, said of the State Senate's defeat on Tuesday of the proposed amendment. "And it cripples the state's harness industry." 
 Although all-year racing at the track will continue for the time being, the sport's long-term prospects there are bleak.
"This week and next week, we're going to look at the economic aspects of racing while pursuing a purchaser for the property," Bob Galterio, the track's general manager, told The Associated Press.
The track lost $5 million last year. Mr. Drucker said the track owners, the Rooney family, have considered selling before.
"They're disappointed," he said, "not so much that the Senate voted it down but that voters never got a chance to decide."
The Rooneys, who also own the Pittsburgh Steelers, acquired the track in 1973. But like many harness tracks, including five others around the state that would have been able to add slot machines, Yonkers has seen better days. Twenty years ago, it averaged 12,419 spectators a night with parimutuel wagering worth an average of $1.73 million a night. Last year, the track's average nightly attendance was 1,200, and the average nightly handle was $207,000.
The track viewed the slot machines as a new source of revenue, which would maintain current customers and lure people who now have to travel to Atlantic City, Ledyard, Conn., or Uncasville, Conn., for casino gambling.
The additional revenues from the slots would have improved the quality of horses that ran at the track by creating bigger purses, Mr. Drucker said. "We would have been able to offer the best purses in North America," he said.
The amendment, had it been approved by legislators and voters in a referendum, would have let gamblers play slot machines at the track only on race days, from two hours before the first race, to two hours after.
Mr. Drucker said he did not anticipate that any buyer would want the 37-acre site to remain a harness racing track.
The State Senate's 41-to-19 vote against the amendment scuttled its consideration until at least 1999.

HEADLINE: Many Black Men Barred From Voting

BYLINE: By FOX BUTTERFIELD 

 BODY:
Fourteen percent of black men are currently or permanently barred from voting either because they are in prison or because they have been convicted of a felony, according to a study to be released today.
Of a total voting age population of 10.4 million black men nationwide, an estimated 1.46 million have lost the right to vote because a felony conviction, said the study, by the Sentencing Project in Washington. Of this disenfranchised group, 950,000 are ineligible to vote because they are in prison or on probation or parole, while about 510,000 are permanently barred in 13 states that take away the right to vote for life for most convicted felons, the study found. 
 Felons are prohibited from voting while in prison in 46 states, and 31 of them disenfranchise offenders while they are on probation or parole. Only 4 states -- Maine, Massachusetts, Utah and Vermont -- do not bar prison inmates from voting. The 13 states that permanently deprive virtually all felons of the right to vote are Alabama, Arizona, Delaware, Florida, Iowa, Kentucky, Maryland, Mississippi, Nevada, New Mexico, Tennessee, Virginia and Wyoming.
"The cumulative impact of such large numbers of persons being disenfranchised from the electoral process clearly dilutes the political power of the African-American community," said the study, "Intended and Unintended Consequences: State Racial Disparities in Imprisonment."
The study's findings are likely to contribute to the debate in Congress and the states over whether sentencing laws, especially those involving crack cocaine, are discriminatory and whether the nation is relying too heavily on longer prison terms to combat crime.
Barbara Wright, director of the voting rights project at the Lawyers Committee for Civil Rights Under Law, said of the study: "These figures are truly shocking. Voting is what makes you a citizen. It's tragic to contemplate the creation of an entire class of native-born non citizens. This seems to be almost an inevitable consequence of the tremendous increases in the incarceration of African-American men."
The study also found that the disparity in incarceration rates between blacks and whites has continued to widen, rising to 7.66 blacks in state and Federal prisons in 1994 for every white incarcerated, up from 6.88 in 1988.
In addition, the study tried to depict the economic and social cost of the racial disparities in incarceration by drawing on recent research by other experts. The study noted, for example, that Richard Freeman, a Harvard economist, has found that people who have served time in prison have a 25 percent reduction in the number of hours they work after being released.
The study also cited work by other scholars indicating that large-scale imprisonment of poor blacks might contribute to the decline of inner cities by increasing unemployment and undermining stable family life.
The Sentencing Project, a nonprofit organization that advocates alternatives to prison, is well regarded for its research. In earlier work, it found that 1 in 3 black men in their 20's were under the control of the criminal justice system on any given day, either in prison or in jail or on probation or parole.
Blacks make up 51 percent of 1.1 million inmates in state and Federal prisons, the study said, though blacks are only 14 percent of the nation's population. Although those numbers show that blacks are disproportionately incarcerated, said David Bositis, a political analyst at the Joint Center for Political and Economic Studies, a Washington research group devoted to black issues, it remains difficult to calculate precisely what effect the disenfranchisement of black men has on voting patterns.
For one thing, Mr. Bositis said, 93 percent of the black population is concentrated in 22 states, mainly New York, California and those in the South and the Midwest. In addition, voting has long been linked to social and economic status, he said, and with many blacks at the poorer end of society, "you would expect them to vote less."
Moreover, in the 1996 elections fewer than half the voting age population voted, Mr. Bositis said, "so even if the black males convicted of a felony had the same voting pattern as the general population, the likelihood is that only half of them would have voted."
Nevertheless, he said, the huge number of black men disenfranchised by a felony conviction "clearly diminishes black influence politically" and may contribute to the fact that black voter turnout is 10 to 20 percent lower than white turnout.
Equally important, Mr. Bositis suggested, is the psychological effect on the black population, much of which already believes that the criminal justice system discriminates against blacks.
"You have a prison system where black men are back in servitude, along with an economy where blacks are in the low paying jobs," he said, "so it all ends up in the minds of black Americans that the system is basically rigged to diminish their political power and recreate the plantation system with prisons."
Michael Tonry, a professor of law at the University of Minnesota and a leading expert on racial disparities in prisons, said he believed that because many prison inmates come from the poorest, least educated and most isolated areas in the inner cities, only about 15 percent of the black men who are incarcerated would be likely to vote. That would reduce the practical effect of the disenfranchisement of such a large number of people, Professor Tonry said.
But the disqualification of so many black men is important as a matter of democratic principle, he said, and "further handicaps an already disadvantaged people."
Professor Tonry said that although much of the racial disparity in imprisonment could be attributed to tougher sentences for crack cocaine, favored by black drug users, than for powered cocaine, favored by whites, "there is also this unhappy truth that blacks are arrested disproportionately for violent crimes."

HEADLINE: Safe Food? Not Yet

BYLINE: By Nicols Fox; Nicols Fox, author of the forthcoming "Spoiled: The Dangerous Truth About a Food Chain Gone Haywire," writes for The Economist.

DATELINE: BASS HARBOR, Me.

 BODY:
President Clinton wants to spend $43 million of his 1998 budget on improving food safety. The good news is that $11.5 million of this money would go to an early-warning system to identify causes of foodborne illness. But while this proposal could help us spot oubreaks more quickly, weaknesses in a new Agriculture Department meat inspection plan mean we aren't likely to see fewer such episodes any time soon.
In an age of widespread distribution of mass-produced foods, food poisonings are difficult to trace. Consumers have little idea where their food comes from or how it is processed. Producers cultivate trust with slick packaging, backed up by Government guarantees. Yet food makes millions of Americans sick each year. 
 Most cases are mild and require no medical treatment, but the Agriculture Department says that up to 9,000 people die from foodborne disease each year (as many as 500 of these deaths can be attributed to E. coli O157:H7, usually found in hamburger). Foodborne pathogens have also been linked to ulcers, Guillain-Barre syndrome, meningitis, reactive arthritis and even miscarriages.
The best part of the President's proposal increases the number of food-safety surveillance centers nationwide from five to eight. These operations, which are run by the states under Federal guidelines, are vital. In 1994, for example, the Minnesota Department of Health's center traced widespread salmonella poisoning to a single tainted batch of ice cream, which was quickly recalled. (Trucks carrying the ice cream's pre-mixture had previously hauled raw eggs.) More than 200,000 people around the nation became ill.
In general, when food poisoning occurs producers blame the consumer for failing to cook food thoroughly. Yet some foods have become unacceptably contaminated. An Agriculture Department study of chickens, quoted in the Federal Register, revealed that more than "99 percent of broiler carcasses" had detectable E. coli, evidence of fecal contamination. Another Federal study found that 72 percent of chickens were contaminated with salmonella. Yet meat and poultry companies have agreed among themselves not to make safety a marketing strategy, so Americans do not have the option, as consumers in Sweden do, of buying chickens labeled and guaranteed to be salmonella free.
The Agriculture Department's new "science-based" inspection system, called Hazard Analysis Critical Control Point, which went into effect this week, is neither a quick fix nor a long-term solution. The plan, which Mr. Clinton announced with great fanfare last summer, is a compromise between producers and the Government that will gradually transfer more oversight responsibility to the industry. Such a system has worked well for products like canned foods, which can be sealed and heated before shipping, but its effectiveness is unproved with raw products.
Unlike the old "prod and sniff" approach, the new plan requires that raw meat be sampled for microbes. But meat containing disease-causing microbes (with the exception of E. coli O157:H7) will not be considered adulterated, the legacy of a legal decision holding that microbes are "inherent" in raw meat. The Agriculture Department still will not have the authority to recall contaminated meat or to regulate farms, and chickens contaminated with salmonella will still be sold. Federal officials will eventually shift from inspecting the meat itself to inspecting records of such checks.
Quick fixes in the hands of an industry that has traditionally put profit before safety are not the answer. Mr. Clinton's initiative is a start, but tough regulations from farm to table, even if they result in slightly higher prices, would be preferable to contaminated food.

HEADLINE: Heated Battle Is Expected On Financing Birth Control

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
The Clinton Administration is preparing to send Congress a measure to speed up spending on family planning overseas, setting up what abortion opponents describe as the first abortion-related vote in the new Congress.
Family-planning advocates say the measure has nothing to do with abortion, since none of the money can be used for abortions. But anti-abortion forces say the money would be used to further the Administration's "abortion crusade" overseas. And, given the charged political atmosphere in which such matters are always debated on Capitol Hill, both sides recognize that it will be cast in terms of abortion, as it was last year when it nearly derailed the budget. 
 At stake is the distribution of millions of condoms, birth-control pills and IUD's to men and women around the world. Without the devices, the Administration says, there will be a significant increase in unplanned pregnancies, abortions and maternal and infant deaths.
The spending measure is left over from a dispute when anti-abortion forces in the House tried to block money for international population-control groups that counsel on family planning and perform abortions.
Under a highly unusual and complicated compromise on last year's budget, Congress voted to reduce money for international family-planning programs by 35 percent and prevent any further spending until July 1, 1997. But it agreed that if the Administration could prove that family programs were being hurt, it would reconsider the measure in February and allow earlier release of the money.
If the measure passes, it would allow family-planning money, appropriated last year, to be released by March 1; if it fails, the money would be released automatically on July 1.
The amount of money varies, depending on when it is released. Congress appropriated $385 million for the State Department's Agency for International Development for the fiscal year ending on Sept. 30, but put a "metering" system on it, allowing only a certain percentage of it to be spent each month. If it starts flowing in March, family-planning programs would receive $215 million between then and Sept. 30. If it is delayed until July, it would amount to $92 million, meaning family-planning programs worldwide would get $123 million less than if the money started flowing in March.
That four-month delay will hurt the family-planning efforts, say Administration officials, who are to send their findings to Congress on Friday night to meet the Feb. 1 deadline set by the agreement.
The Administration plans to make the case that the loss of $123 million would mean that 50 million condoms, 4.8 million cycles of birth-control pills and 500,000 IUD's would not be distributed worldwide, officials said.
In Russia alone, for example, the two largest organizations that provide family planning would run out of money and contraceptives in that four-month period and may have to shut down. The two programs provide 1.7 million couples with contraceptive devices.
The Administration officials said the delay could also mean a loss of jobs in the United States among manufacturers of contraceptives.
The family-planning money has frequently become locked in a battle among anti-abortion forces in the House, a divided Senate, and abortion-rights forces in the White House, and the stage is set for the same battle to be replayed this year.
Douglas Johnson, chief lobbyist for the National Right to Life Committee, said, "This is the first abortion vote of the new Congress, and if lawmakers who ran as pro-life vote to give the Clinton Administration $123 million for its overseas abortion crusade, many of their constituents will be pretty upset."
He said he expected the vote to be close in both houses, particularly the Senate, where family-planning measures are not always seen in strict abortion terms.
Representative Christopher H. Smith, the New Jersey Republican who has led fights against family-planning money, insisted that the money would be used to promote abortion, even though the Administration has said that A.I.D. money could not and would not be used for this purpose.
"If we're going to give hundreds of millions of foreign aid to the abortion industry under the guise of family planning, it's a ruse I refuse to be a part of," he said.
Victoria Markell, political director for Population Action International, which supports speeding up the spending, said: "The Senate is reasonable, and we will win that vote. If the House leadership honors its agreement to keep this a clean vote and not mix it into the domestic abortion debate, we'll win that too.
But she said there had been discussion that Mr. Smith may be allowed to introduce an alternative measure, which she said would insure that the measure becomes an abortion vote rather than a family-planning vote.

HEADLINE: Whitman Asks $16.4 Billion In New Budget

BYLINE: By JENNIFER PRESTON 

DATELINE: TRENTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Gov. Christine Todd Whitman proposed a $16.4 billion election-year budget today that provides spending increases for public schools, prisons and the environment, and more than a dozen modest initiatives aimed at Democratic and swing voters.
After three years of cutting taxes and reining in government spending, Governor Whitman took a different approach this year. Instead of the deep spending cuts in almost every state agency that she proposed last year, she recommends that spending either remain at current levels or increase for most programs in the coming year. She would pay for much of these increases by relying on $850 million from sources available only once. 
 She also promises to expand low-tax zones in cities, offer health insurance to uninsured children, expand drug treatment programs for women with children, open a second boot camp for juvenile offenders and eliminate the waiting list for disabled people seeking access to group homes and day care centers.
Describing her spending plan as "the people's budget," Mrs. Whitman said in her budget address to the State Legislature what she can be expected to recite on the campaign trail:
"It's about healthy children. It's about fighting drugs and crime. It's about helping businesses create jobs. It's about cleaner air and cleaner water."
The proposed budget, which raises overall spending by $203 million over this year's budget, was also notable for its contrast with the cuts she proposed a year ago. Last year, Governor Whitman sought to reduce subsidies to the prescription program for the elderly; this year, she boasted of her efforts to help preserve the program.
Last year, for the third year in a row, she recommended deep cuts at the State Department of Environmental Protection. But with her potential gubernatorial challengers poised to attack her environmental record, Mrs. Whitman proposed a $2.1 million increase at the department and touted new programs to monitor water pollution and clean up hazardous waste sites.
As expected, Mrs. Whitman's potential Democratic challengers were quick to dismiss her plan.
State Senator James E. McGreevey of Middlesex County, who is seeking the Democratic nomination for governor, said he thought that voters would see the Governor's proposal as a "completely vacuous budget."
Senator McGreevey added, "This is government by theme, not government by accounting." He also complained that the proposal did nothing to provide voters with more property tax relief.
Under the plan, state aid to municipalities would remain flat for the second year in a row, prompting Democratic lawmakers and some local officials to warn that property taxes would rise this year in many cities and towns struggling to keep up with increasing operating expenses.
But Mrs. Whitman said she was confident that property taxes would not increase significantly. She pointed out that her proposal would distribute $5 billion to school districts, including $286 million for the new school financing plan approved by the State Legislature last month.
The plan is part of the Whitman administration's efforts to comply with a State Supreme Court order requiring New Jersey to narrow the spending gap between the richest and poorest districts. About $138 million would go the state's 30 neediest districts.
"We have held budget growth to less than half the rate of inflation, while dramatically increasing our commitment to property tax relief," she said. "In fact, between school funding, county court takeover, transportation aid and other forms of assistance, the state will provide nearly three quarters of a billion dollars more for property tax relief this year than when I took office."
Although Mrs. Whitman sought to emphasize the good news in her proposed budget, the plan angered some constituents.
Her budget proposal recommends a $20 million decline in aid to colleges and universities.
But administration officials said colleges should be able to avoid tuition increases for residents by drawing on their budget reserves and taking advantage of savings from reduced employee health benefit costs.
She also recommended significant increase in tuition for out-of-state students that would cover "the actual cost of their education."
The plan would also reduce the state work force by 3,235 jobs, with 2,000 of those a result of previously announced plans to shut down the Marlboro Psychiatric Hospital and North Princeton Development Center. At the same time, the plan calls for hiring 900 corrections officers for a new 3,200-bed prison, as well as 100 more investigators to curb fraud in the auto insurance industry and in Medicaid programs.
To pay for her proposed new initiatives and balance her spending plan, Mrs. Whitman anticipates $200 million more in new tax revenues and $850 million from one-shot revenues and spending cuts, sources of money that the administration cannot necessarily turn to next year.
The administration is counting on $200 million from the proposed sale of the State Disability Benefits Fund, which distributes about $326 million in payments for up to 26 weeks to New Jersey workers who are temporarily disabled as a result of injury or sickness unrelated to their jobs.
More than 2.4 million employees and employers now pay about $370 million in payroll taxes annually to the fund, which administration officials hope to sell to a private insurance company before June. New Jersey is one of five states that administers temporary disability benefits. Before the administration can move forward with the sale, however, it needs the Legislature's approval.
The administration is also trying to save $623 million during the next 14 months, primarily by reducing the state's contribution to the public employee pension system.
The Whitman administration has proposed selling $3.4 billion in bonds to help pay off the pension system's $4.2 billion unfunded liability account, which covers cost-of-living increases for retirees.
By using the proceeds from the sale and $800 million from the pension system's surplus to pay off the entire unfunded liability account, Brian W. Clymer, the State Treasurer, said that he can avoid much of the state's regular pension contribution this year. In addition, since payments on the bond sale would not begin until next year, the state can avoid paying $113 million this year in debt service.
This is the second time that the Whitman administration has turned to the pension system to help balance the budget, and help pay for the cost of a 30 percent reduction in income tax rates and other tax cuts totaling $2.8 billion since Mrs. Whitman took office.
By making several changes to the pension system, including raising the assumption that the system will earn higher investment returns, the Whitman administration has reduced the state's contribution by $3.5 billion to the pension system since 1994.
But Mrs. Whitman's pension proposal is not likely to win easy approval in the Republican-controlled Legislature.
State Senator Robert E. Littell, a Republican from Franklin who is chairman of the Senate Appropriations Committee, said he did not like the proposal, and that many lawmakers, Republicans and Democrats, said they had similar concerns.
"I think it is a bad idea," Mr. Littell said. "It would be the biggest borrowing by a state in history. It would lock us into debt service payments for 35 years. We would lose a lot of flexibility."
State Senator Bernard F. Kenny Jr., a Democrat from Hudson County, also criticized the Whitman plan, saying that it gets "failing marks for controlling property taxes, making a college education affordable and protecting our environment."

HEADLINE: British-Dutch Firm Asks for Tax Breaks

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
In the latest round of corporate poker, ING Capital, a giant British-Dutch financial services firm, is threatening to abandon New York for a new headquarters in Stamford, Conn., unless New York grants the company a package of tax breaks and other incentives, real estate and city officials said yesterday.
ING Capital has been in New York since 1820, a spokesman said. City officials were told yesterday that there was a difference of nearly $90 million over a 15-year lease between the location ING is considering in Stamford and a new site in Manhattan: 300,000 square feet at Park Avenue Plaza at East 52d Street. 
 The fast-growing company, which bought Barings P.L.C. in 1995 when the company was on the verge of ruin, operates out of 200,000 square feet at 135 East 57th Street and 667 Madison Avenue, near 59th Street.
In the 1990's, dozens of other companies, like Dillon, Read; Travelers Inc.; Prudential Securities, and Morgan Stanley have been granted hundreds of millions of dollars in tax breaks and other incentives after threatening to leave the city. But in this case, city officialsare apparently reluctant to grant the incentives because they think ING might not be serious about moving, according to real estate executives and public officials familiar with the negotiations.
Many administration officials are said to believe that ING cannot or will not move nearly 1,000 employees, if only because there is little available space elsewhere.
Officials at the Economic Development Corporation declined to comment, and ING officials did not return calls requesting an interview. But Donald A. DiRenzo Sr., vice chairman of Cushman & Wakefield, ING's representative, acknowledged that the company is considering Park Avenue and Stamford. A decision is expected this week.
"They haven't quite made up their mind," Mr. DiRenzo said of ING. "We're still in the midst of negotiating. But it is a poker game with the city. You never know what they're going to say."
The rejection of the company's application for what is known as a corporate retention package would signify not so much a change in municipal policy, experts say, as a calculation that the city's economy and the real estate market in New York have recovered from the recession of the early 1990's. Vacancy rates have come down in midtown Manhattan and in Stamford, where there are few empty office buildings left.
The city has been willing to provide tax breaks for companies moving to lower Manhattan, where vacancy rates remain high, or Times Square, a once seedy entertainment district, but may not be willing to subsidize a move to Park Avenue.
"The city's more sophisticated now," said Bruce E. Mosler, president of the Galbreath real estate company and a frequent critic of the deals. "The city is much less willing to be arbitrary in their approach to giving concessions. In the past, you were able to lever your position with the city, whether you had a real option or not."
ING is hardly the only company casting about for new space and tax breaks. Guardian Insurance, which owns its building on Park Avenue South in Union Square, is looking for 400,000 square feet for a new home in Manhattan or Westchester County.
NatWest is looking for 300,000 square feet in midtown and Connecticut, brokers say, and Reuters is looking for 800,000 square feet and has hired a lawyer who negotiated incentives packages for some of the city's most prestigious corporations. The Giuliani administration has granted 24 corporations a total of $506.8 million in tax breaks, job training and low-cost power since January 1994.
The city says the incentive packages secured 55,791 jobs that might otherwise have left. The tax breaks, the administration says, are a small price to pay for retaining companies that generate $809.12 million a year in tax revenues. A small part of the benefits are tied to job growth.
But some landlords, urban planners and politicians have criticized the retention deals because, they say, it unfairly shifts the city's tax burden from the backs of the largest, most politically connected companies to small and medium-sized companies that have a higher rate of job growth.
Elliott Sclar, a professor of economics and urban policy at Columbia University, said it would be better to cut the tax rate and invest in roads public transportation and schools.
"It would take a very courageous administration to stop this, because it's like disarming," Mr. Sclar said. "But if the city can't do it now then they ought to pack it in. These are the good times."
Companies have not stopped leaving Manhattan, nor have New Jersey and Connecticut stopped offering incentive packages and lower tax rates. Last year, the Zurich Insurance Group announced that it would move its Risk Enterprise Management operation and 500 jobs to New Jersey. And 37 of the 101 firms lured to Connecticut since January 1995, came from New York City, accounting for 2,264 of the jobs captured from other states, state data show.

HEADLINE: U.S. to Put Airline Safety Data on Internet, but Carrier Ranking Is Ruled Out

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Under pressure to release information on airline safety and mishaps, the Federal Aviation Administration said today that it would do so next month on the World Wide Web but that the information would not help rank airlines by safety.
Many of the reports are available now only by filing a request under the Freedom of Information Act. Senator Ron Wyden, Democrat of Oregon, who had called for releasing more information shortly after the crash of a Valujet Airlines DC-9 that killed 110 people on May 11, announced the new policy today and proclaimed, "From now on, aviation secrets are going public." 
 But aviation officials said they had no statistics that could be used to rank airlines by safety, not even information used to decide which airlines need extra inspections.
Barry Bermingham, deputy administrator for system safety, said the F.A.A. would strive to make information that was "valuable in the public's mind" easily available to make them more informed consumers. But, he said, such information would not allow anybody -- not a potential traveler and not even a statistician -- to rank the airlines.
Joining the F.A.A. in this opinion today was the Air Transport Association, which is the trade association of the big airlines.
"Are consumers going to be able to make a judgment? The answer is no, they're not," said David Fuscus, a spokesman. "We're not aware of any way to take that information and come up with a valid way to compare one airline to another."
The F.A.A. did compare safety among airlines last spring, in a report it prepared for the Transportation Secretary Federico F. Pena giving the rates of accidents and incidents per departure for each airline. The report described itself as an effort to prove that low-cost carriers set up under deregulation were just as safe as established carriers. But officials said today that that ranking, which has not been repeated, would not help passengers trying to pick the safest airline.
"Past fatal accidents and nonfatal accidents do not predict safety," said Richard Golaszewski of GRA Inc., a consulting firm hired by the agency to help establish policy on releasing information. "Knowing who had the last accident doesn't tell you a lot about who might have the next one."
"Right now, you really can't make meaningful distinctions among individual carriers," he said.
But the information the F.A.A. plans to make available will allow anyone with access to the World Wide Web to search through nearly 20 years of data, using a software "search engine," to spot incidents of equipment failure, forced landings and other incidents.
Two data bases the agency plans to make available electronically by Feb. 28 are that of the National Transportation Safety Board, on airline accidents and incidents that dates from 1984, and another from the F.A.A. that runs back to 1978. The information will be available on the Web page, www.faa.gov.
The Transportation Department already offers on-line comparative data on lost baggage rates and on-time arrival rates, updated monthly.
Beginning Feb. 1, the F.A.A. will issue press releases on new enforcement actions that seek civil penalties of $50,000 or more, the agency said. Those actions carrying smaller penalties will be announced if the agency thinks the incidents are of particular interest; for example, officials said, if an engine shuts down in flight and the cause is found to be maintenance errors.
In general, the penalty for a single violation on a single flight is $10,000, officials said. The amount collected each year ranges from about $1 million to $1.5 million, officials said.
The acting administrator, Linda Hall Daschle, said in a statement that the F.A.A. would also establish on line "a public education area with narrative materials to help travelers better understand how impressive the U.S. safety record is."

HEADLINE: Blows From Nafta Batter the Caribbean Economy

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: KINGSTON, Jamaica

 BODY:
Three years after the United States, Canada and Mexico agreed to become a single market as part of the North American Free Trade Agreement, their exports to each other are booming. But here in the Caribbean, the economies of America's much smaller neighbors are reeling from the impact of that success and finding it nearly impossible to compete.
From the apparel plants of Jamaica to the sugar-cane fields of Trinidad, Nafta has already resulted in the loss of jobs, markets and income for the vulnerable island nations of the region. The capital and investment projects that are vitally needed for future growth, officials say, are increasingly flowing out of the Caribbean Basin and into Mexico. 
 "The stark reality is that Mexico can now export its products to the United States free of duty, which makes it more profitable for producers to operate from there," Seymour Mullings, Jamaica's Minister of Foreign Affairs and Foreign Trade, said in an interview here. "Putting it very simply, if that is not stemmed, it could do untold damage to our manufacturing sector and economy as a whole."
Nafta's devastating effect on the Caribbean was widely forecast before the treaty's passage in 1993 and Washington suggested it would cushion the blow by extending similar trade preferences to the island nations.
However, the Clinton Administration's proposals to give the Caribbean "Nafta parity" have twice foundered in Congress in election years and now face an uncertain future in a new Congress that has decidedly mixed feelings about the benefits of free-trade agreements.
The Caribbean now exports more than $12.5 billion worth of goods to the United States annually, one-third of which it could lose to Mexico if the existing trade rules remain in effect, according to a recent World Bank study.
The region's once-flourishing apparel sector has been hard hit, officials say. In the last two years, more than 150 apparel plants closed in the Caribbean and 123,000 jobs have been lost "as a direct result of trade and investment diversion to Mexico," according to the Caribbean Textile and Apparel Institute, which is located here.
Textile manufacturing had been one of the Caribbean's few economic bright spots. Between 1980 and 1995, Jamaica's garment exports, primarily underwear and hosiery, rose from less than $10 million a year to nearly $600 million, an average annual growth rate of 28 percent.
Since Nafta took effect in 1994, Mexican textile exports have grown at a rate three times those of the Caribbean as a whole. In 1996, the Caribbean Textile and Apparel Institute estimates, Jamaica's garment exports fell by 7 percent, with 7,000 jobs eliminated.
Similar or even larger decreases were recorded in Guyana, Belize and tiny St. Lucia.
More than 600 people, about 95 percent of them women, felt the effects of Nafta when the Korean-owned Youngone Garment factory on Marcus Garvey Drive here closed just before Christmas.
The plant had been making T-shirts for export to the United States. But a Mexican factory took the business away with a lower bid, prompting the company to shut down operations in Jamaica and send its employees home. The company then shipped its equipment off to Bangladesh.
"I lost my job back in '95 and haven't been able to find another one since," said Beryl Davidson, 26, a former textile worker and single mother of three small children. "I couldn't pay my rent, and I couldn't feed my kids, so I've had to move back in with my parents to survive."
"But my cousin in Brooklyn tells me there's plenty of work there, so maybe I will join her," she said.
Since Nafta went into effect, the creation of new jobs in this nation of 2.3 million people has stopped altogether and overall unemployment has risen to 16 percent from 9.5 percent, according to the Statistical Institute of Jamaica. Among women in the apparel sector, the unemployment rate is now more than 33 percent.
Worse yet, the loss of jobs and American support occurs as the pro-American Government here, with an election due sometime in the next year, is completing an economic retrenchment that had been strongly urged by Washington. Over the past decade, Jamaica has sold off state companies, reduced the budget deficit and increased foreign reserves, but at a high social cost.
"We have no safety net here, no welfare, no Medicare," said Anthony Gomes, director of a large trading company. "So when people go to the street, it has a serious ripple effect. The way things are going, jobs are very difficult to get, and that is not helping our crime rate."
American officials, however, argue that Jamaica and other Caribbean nations are blaming Nafta for deeper-rooted economic difficulties that will remain even if trade rules are eventually eased. In the case of Jamaica, they maintain, those include a revaluation of the currency that increased its value by 12.5 percent last year, making the country's products more expensive, and a host of regulatory obstacles.
"The main problem here is government bureaucracy," one official said. "It is darn near impossible to collect the licenses and approvals you need to get a business off the ground."
Ironically, Jamaica's initial export surge was the result of another American program, the Caribbean Basin Initiative, which the Reagan Administration put in place 15 years ago. A package of aid, trade and investment incentives aimed at the private sector, the program was intended to introduce the Caribbean to what Mr. Reagan called "the magic of the marketplace," and had Jamaica as its centerpiece.
But that arrangement has also benefited the United States. American exports to the region rose 160 percent in the decade ending in 1995, to more than $15 billion a year. The Caribbean is the only part of the world where Washington recorded a favorable balance of trade every year during that time.
The Caribbean Basin Initiative still exists, but places either duties or quotas on those products in which Caribbean nations enjoy a competitive advantage, such as sugar, textiles and footwear.
"All we are asking is to be put on a level playing field with Mexico," said Paul Robertson, Jamaica's Minister of Industry, Investment and Commerce. "We are not seeking a handout, but only the opportunity not to be prevented from taking full advantage of the North American market."
