HEADLINE: Deliberating, Simpson Jury Reveals Little Of Its Intent

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr. 

DATELINE: SANTA MONICA, Calif., Jan. 29

 BODY:
Behind closed doors, with a scattering of the curious and an army of reporters and photographers hanging outside the courthouse, the jury in O. J. Simpson's second trial put in its first full day of deliberations today.
At one point the panel of seven women and five men sent out for a magnifying glass used in closely studying photographs, and for a photograph of a test tube like the one used to store a sample of Mr. Simpson's blood. Judge Hiroshi Fujisaki gave the jurors the magnifying glass but refused to give them the picture, saying they did not need it. 
 Throughout the trial, many photographs were entered as evidence, including those of the crime scene, of spattered blood in Mr. Simpson's home, of his vehicles and of Mr. Simpson, apparently wearing shoes like the ones that left bloody footprints at the slaying scene.
It was not clear from the jury's requests where the jury was headed or how long it might take to get there.
The panel -- which includes a postal worker, a bank employee, a stage manager, an unemployed cement finisher and several homemakers -- was handed the wrongful-death civil suit late on Tuesday. It remained closeted for more than six hours today as it tried to determine whether Mr. Simpson killed Nicole Brown Simpson, his former wife, and Ronald L. Goldman, her friend, and, if he did, how much he should pay the surviving families, the plaintiffs.
The families did not ask for a specific amount in their court papers.
In Mr. Simpson's criminal trial for murder, the jury, after little more than three hours of deliberation in October 1995, found the former football star and entertainment personality not guilty of charges that he fatally slashed Mrs. Simpson and Mr. Goldman outside her condominium on the night of June 12, 1994.
The nation was transfixed by that trial, with its undertones of spousal abuse, unrequited love, police racism and high living in the fast lane. And to isolate it from the intense national focus, the criminal jury was sequestered throughout the nine-months trial. Though national interest in the case remains high, it has somewhat abated, and the civil judge has not sequestered this jury throughout the four-month second trial.
But the civil case is significantly different from the criminal one because it involves only money claims, and not the possibility of a prison sentence.
Much of the legal ground covered in the first trial also was covered in the second, including accusations by the plaintiffs that Mr. Simpson was a cold-blooded, lying murderer and counter-accusations by the defense that he was framed by corrupt police investigators. But the jury in the second case is applying a different, lesser standard of culpability as it determines Mr. Simpson's fate.
In the first trial, conviction required unanimous agreement by the 12 jurors that Mr. Simpson was, beyond a reasonable doubt, the killer. In the second trial, a finding of culpability requires the votes of only 9 of 12 jurors, and they need agree only that, more likely than not, he was the killer and thus should pay.
But on closer inspection, the second case is not so simple.
For one thing, the plaintiffs made clear in their arguments that they are seeking not just money, both for recompense and punishment, but also closure to a horrendous trauma in their lives. They would be happy as a last resort, they indicated, if the jury ignored the money issue and found only that, indeed, Mr. Simpson killed Mrs. Simpson and Mr. Goldman.
Three actions, not one, are involved in the second case, though all were combined into a single action in the interest of judicial efficiency.
In the first two suits, Fred Goldman and Sharon Rufo, the long-divorced parents of Mr. Goldman, each filed wrongful-death claims accusing Mr. Simpson of killing their son and asking that he compensate them for that loss and that he also pay extra as punishment.
In the third suit, Louis and Juditha Brown, Mrs. Simpson's parents, filed a suit on behalf of her estate, known as a survivorship suit, that also demands money from Mr. Simpson for fatally assaulting his wife. If any money is forthcoming, it will by law go not to the Browns but to the Simpson children Sydney and Justin.
The Browns deliberately avoided pushing for a wrongful-death suit because such a pleading would have required that the children become plaintiffs. The grandparents said they did not want to put the youngsters directly in the position of suing their father for murdering their mother.
The first jury was predominately black, a significant factor in a case in which police racism was a major issue. In the second case, in which the court ordered the racism issue played down during the testimony stage, the jury is predominately white.

America Online to Give Refunds and Credits
America Online agreed to give millions of dollars worth of credits and refunds as compensation for the network traffic jams that have been frustrating its customers.
Threats of lawsuits by many states and an outcry from subscribers had clouded the company's prospects, and the announcement lifted the stock sharply even though the deal could be costly. [Page A1.]
 
Ford Profits Increase
Ford Motor reported higher earnings for the fourth quarter and all of 1996, despite continued losses in the European and Latin American divisions. [D4.] 

Strong Quarter for Philip Morris
Philip Morris announced strong fourth-quarter earnings, powered by robust international tobacco profits and the better-than-expected performance of Marlboro cigarettes in the United States. [D2.]
 
Late Surge Lifts the Dow
A weeklong stock decline ended with a rally fueled by late buying. The Dow industrials rose 84.66 points; other indexes were up significantly less or declined. [D8.]
 
Inflation-Indexed Bonds Do Well
The Treasury's first auction of inflation-indexed securities went better than expected, with strong interest from institutional investors. The prices of other Treasury securities rose, with the 30-year bond yield falling to 6.90 percent from 6.92.[D18.]
 
While the inflation-indexed bonds are seen by Wall Street as another way to make a buck, economists' pulses are racing over other uses. Peter Passell: Economic Scene. [D2.]
 
Orders for Durable Goods Decline
Unexpectedly, factory orders for big-ticket goods fell 1.7 percent in December, indicating that economic growth could be more restrained than many analysts believe. [D4.]
 
No Jubilation Over Weaker Yen
For Japanese exporters, the trouble should be over. The dollar has risen almost to the level it was before the recent appreciation in the yen began. But executives who said the yen was too strong then are saying it is too weak now. [D7.]
 
The dollar again rose against the yen, briefly touching another four-year high, but it ended lower against some other currencies. [D15.]
 
CompUSA Meets Lower Forecasts
CompUSA, the computer retailer that warned analysts to reduce their earnings estimates, said its profits rose 27 percent. [D4.]
 
Hilton Putting Offer on Table
Hilton Hotels plans to file its tender offer for ITT, the gambling and lodging company for which Hilton has bid $6.5 billion. ITT reiterated that it would probably take up to 10 business days to consider the offer. [D4.]
 
Chiron Shuffling Its Top Ranks
Chiron, a growing biotechnology company that has suffered setbacks in the last year, said it would seek a new president and chief executive to succeed Edward E. Penhoet, who will become vice chairman. [D4.]
 
Trade Boom Bypasses Caribbean
Caribbean economies are reeling from the effects of the North American Free Trade Agreement, which has lifted trade among the United States, Canada and Mexico but made it harder for smaller nations to compete. [A1.]
 
A New Role For Kravis
Henry R. Kravis, best known for multibillion-dollar deals as a founding partner in Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, has taken an interest in deal-making at a less rarefied level. He is chairman of the New York City Investment Fund, which incorporated last fall with the intention of creating jobs in New York, especially in poor and minority neighborhoods. [B1.]
 
F.A.A. to Provide Safety Data
The F.A.A. said it would release information on airline safety over the World Wide Web but that it would not rank carriers. [B6.]

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1915 words

HEADLINE: Hispanic Households Struggle Amid Broad Decline in Income

BYLINE: By CAREY GOLDBERG 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Jan. 29

 BODY:
If he dared to tattoo gang symbols on his neck, Myrna Morales warned her strapping 17-year-old son, William, she would burn them off with her iron. Or a frying pan.
Such was the desperation of Mrs. Morales, 41, a naturalized American from Guatemala who has watched three of her United States-born children seduced by the worst of inner-city culture and seen her earnings as a housecleaner shrink in recent years. With her diminishing income, she asked, how can she promise that they will prosper like so many other immigrants' children before them? 
 "I tell them go to school and don't be like me, cleaning bathrooms," said Mrs. Morales, who scrubs and vacuums for $300 a week despite her medical assistant's degree from a local college. Her husband earns as additional $300 a week as a painter, but the Moraleses still feel they are on the edge of a precipice.
To judge by the latest Census Bureau statistics, they are right to be worried. The data show that in 1995, median household income rose for every other American ethnic and racial group except the country's 27 million Hispanic residents, for whom the figure fell 5.1 percent. The downturn, which affects the United States-born as well as the newly arrived across a broad spectrum of socio-economic indicators, has baffled social scientists. And it has prompted some to warn that many Hispanic residents, members of the country's fastest-growing ethnic or racial group, may become entrenched as its working poor.
Census data show that for the first time the poverty rate among Hispanic residents of the United States has surpassed that of blacks. Hispanic residents now constitute nearly 24 percent of the country's poor, up eight percentage points since 1985. Of all Hispanic residents, 30 percent were considered poor in 1995, meaning they earned less than $15,569 for a family of four. That is almost three times the percentage of non-Hispanic whites in poverty. Of the poorest of the poor, those with incomes of $7,500 or less for a family of four, 24 percent were Hispanic.
These are not just statistical blips. Over all, income for Hispanic households has dropped 14 percent since 1989, to under $22,900, from about $26,000, while rising slightly for black ones.
Nor do the data simply reflect the recent influx of illegal Hispanic immigrants.
"As we know from the data in other studies," said Manuel de la Puente, the chief of the Ethnic and Hispanic Statistics Branch at the Census Bureau, "the immigrants tend to be low-educated individuals, hold service-sector jobs and have little or no English, and all these things contribute to income."
But when Census analysts separated out United States-born Hispanic people, Mr. de la Puente said, they found their income levels declining as well. Statistics indicate that the United States' Hispanic population is experiencing an almost across-the-board impoverishment.
"It is the American nightmare, not the American dream," said Arturo Vargas, who heads the National Association of Latino Elected Officials, based in Los Angeles.
Though the Hispanic middle class has been growing, Mr. Vargas said, most Hispanic residents are caught in jobs like gardener, nanny and restaurant worker that will never pay well and from which they will never advance.
 
A Mixed Group Difficult to Stereotype
The declining income among the nation's Hispanic population is little understood and requires more study, researchers say, as do new data showing improved economic well-being among blacks.
Researchers also caution that "Hispanic" is an ethnic term and that the group is an amalgam of people, some of them black, and their descendants, from nearly 24 countries. They range from typically prosperous Cuban Americans of Miami to Puerto Rican Americans, largely concentrated in New York and the nation's poorest ethnic group. Generalizations, then, are often of limited use.
Nonetheless, the growing group of scholars who study the United States' Hispanic residents point to several factors that affect most of that population:
*Structural changes in the economy that have drastically reduced well-paid blue-collar jobs.
*The failures of institutions like schools to retain Hispanic students and provide them with a marketable education, resulting in a widening gap in graduation rates from both high school and college. Hispanic residents have by far the highest high-school dropout rate of any group in the country; 1990 Census figures found that even among United States-born Hispanic people, only 78 percent finished high school compared with 91 percent of whites and 84 percent of blacks.
*Discrimination among employers who see Hispanic immigrants, particularly those who speak poor English, as disposable workers.
Experts acknowledge that the influx of millions of Latin American immigrants over the last 20 years -- two million from 1990 to 1994 alone, the Census reports -- have pulled income numbers down because immigrants tend to be poor.
"Everybody's going up the escalator," said Dowell Myers, a demographer at the University of Southern California who has studied the region's Hispanic population, "but there's a big queue at the bottom, and the queue's getting bigger, so the average number of steps people have gotten up has slipped."
The arrival of so many new workers, many illegal and willing to work for little, has also driven down wages in low-skill professions, Professor Myers said.
A new study by the University of California at Los Angeles, based on Census data, notes that in the blue-collar industries of California in which Mexican immigrants are concentrated -- clothing and furniture manufacture, for example -- real earnings declined more than $6,000 a year from 1970 to 1990.
 
Lack of Education Handicaps Immigrants
Government demographers also note that immigration and birth rates have weighted the country's Hispanic population toward lower-income groups, like Mexican Americans, at the expense of higher-income ones, like Cuban Americans. In addition, Hispanic residents tend to be younger than the population at large and Hispanic women with children are less likely to work.
But for many scholars and community leaders, the main explanation for Hispanic residents' plight is education -- or the lack of it. A 1994 study by Frank D. Bean, a University of Texas demographer, and his colleagues found that in several age groups, Mexican Americans whose families had lived in the United States for three generations or more received slightly less schooling than their parents. And people of Mexican descent account for the biggest portion of the Hispanic population.
Another study, by the Census Bureau in 1995, found that Hispanic residents have been falling further behind non-Hispanic ones in rates of college attendance even as the economy is providing growing rewards to the better educated. In 1994, only 9 percent of Hispanic Americans over age 24 had college degrees, compared with 24 percent of non-Hispanic Americans; Hispanic students were doing proportionally better in 1975, when 5 percent held college degrees compared with 11.6 percent of non-Hispanic ones.
Just getting through high school is a challenge for the many Hispanic children in school systems as overwhelmed and underfinanced as the Los Angeles Unified School District, which is 70 percent Hispanic.
Crowding is so bad that 40,000 schoolchildren out of 670,000 must be bused outside their neighborhoods and most schools run on year-round schedules, said Vickie Castro, a member of the Los Angeles school board. Music, art and sports programs have been cut, Ms. Castro said, and with 1 counselor for every 500 children, little dropout prevention can come from that quarter.
"There's a strong work ethic within Latino families," she said, "and we keep telling them education is the tool to get out of poverty, but their immediate needs overtake it."
Those needs overtook Linda Martinez in Houston, a 23-year-old who dropped out of school to work when she was 15.
Ms. Martinez is now attending community college while working part-time at a community center. She hopes to finish soon and become a teacher, she said, but knows that her education may be interrupted again.
Some Hispanic scholars and community leaders suggest that many problems affecting the nation's Hispanic population stem from the fact that members of the poor, ill-educated immigrant segment tend to settle in crime-ridden, gang-infested inner cities, where a culture of easy money might be difficult to resist.
William, Mrs. Morales's son, for example, has left the Alley Cats 13 gang and reformed, helped by Youth Fair Chance, a training and counseling program that is soon to lose most of its Federal Labor Department financing. But he said he remembered what the world looked like when he dropped out of high school and had gang symbols tattooed on his back.
"I saw friends that didn't graduate from high school robbing and getting money," said William, who works as a peer counselor and is finishing school at Youth Fair Chance in downtown Los Angeles.
He was seduced by the cash and the parties and the girls, he said, but in the end, "I didn't want my mother crying every night."
She already had plenty to cry about. William's twin sister, an unmarried mother of a toddler, is now trying to finish high school while working as a waitress, and his older brother, age 20, has been in jail on a gang-related murder conviction since he was 15.

Low Pay And Little Future
Mrs. Morales, who immigrated illegally in 1973 and received a green card through a family whose disabled son she helped rear, is no quitter. But she is thinking of allowing her husband to take their youngest, a 4-year-old son, back to Guatemala and rear him there, "away from all this."
"I wish I had a better job, lots of money, so I could buy a house outside this neighborhood," Mrs. Morales said of the section of downtown Los Angeles where the family lives. But the clinic where she worked closed and house cleaning pays less than it used to because of the competition from new immigrants, she said.
Because so many businesses have moved to suburbs, "finding their way outward has become harder for many urban-dwelling poor and minorities," Rebecca Morales and Frank Bonilla write in "Latinos in a Changing U.S. Economy" (Sage, 1993).
Even those who do escape, like Joe Aravena, 40, a plant utility engineer who moved his family from the South Bronx to the ethnically mixed Castle Hill section five years ago, say they seem to be working harder to keep a toehold in middle-class life.
Born in the United States of a Chilean father and a Puerto Rican mother, Mr. Aravena has seen his $34,000 salary at the state hospital stagnate for years. These days, he said, he can afford to send his 11-year-old son to Roman Catholic school only by putting in frequent 16-hour shifts.
The general problem of Hispanic poverty, researchers agree, begins with the low education and skill levels with which most Hispanic immigrants arrive in this country. But the most critical barrier to immigrants' success, studies suggest, is language.
"Limited English proficiency is the single most important obstacle to upward mobility among Mexican immigrants," said Wayne Cornelius, the director of the Center for U.S.-Mexican Studies at the University of California at San Diego.
Without a command of English, scholars say, it matters little how industrious a Hispanic person is.

INTERNATIONAL A3-11
 
Hamas Official Warns U.S. Not to Extradite Leader
A top Hamas official in Gaza warned of possible retaliation against the United States if it extradites Mousa Mohammed Abu Marzook to Israel. Mr. Abu Marzook has acknowledged heading the militant Islamic organization's political wing. "Israel will gain nothing from more prisoners, and America will gain nothing by gaining a new enemy -- Hamas," the official said. Mr. Abu Marzook has denied any role in Hamas's military actions. A3
 
Vichy Figure Lashes Out
Maurice Papon, a former French official ordered to stand trial on charges of crimes against humanity for deporting Jews to Nazi camps between 1942 and 1944, accused French Jews of helping to organize the deportations. He asserted that Jewish leaders in charge of administrating transit camps in France had, albeit under threat, selected which Jews were to be sent to death camps in Germany. He denied any guilt and blamed foreign forces in New York for orchestrating a campaign against himself and France. A5
 
Court Upholds Bhutto Dismissal
Pakistan's highest court upheld President Farooq Leghari's dismissal of the Government of former Prime Minister Benazir Bhutto for corruption last November. The judges ruled that there was ample proof of corruption as well as evidence the government sanctioned killings of political opponents. A3
 
Peru and Japan to Confer
President Alberto Fujimori of Peru agreed to meet with Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto of Japan in Toronto this weekend to discuss the hostage crisis in Lima, now entering its sixth week. Mr. Hashimoto is known to be concerned that aggressive maneuvers by Peruvian security forces outside the residence of the Japanese Ambassador could jeopardize the lives of 72 hostages being held inside by leftist rebels. A11
 
Bulgarian Socialists Ask to Talk
Bulgaria's governing Socialists, facing widening street protests and a collapsing economy, appealed for talks with opposition parties to set a date for early elections. The Socialists have overseen a caretaker Government since the Prime Minister resigned in December after his party lost the presidential election. The opposition said it would talk only if the Socialists gave up their efforts to form a new government. A4
 
For Some Albanians, Cash Back
The poorest Albanians will get immediate cash payments from the frozen assets of failing get-rich-quick schemes, President Sali Berisha said. Others will receive account statements proving they put money into the pyramid schemes. The schemes were declared illegal this month after their failure to make payments set off unrest. It was unclear how many people would be reimbursed in cash, opening the way for more confusion and possible rioting from investors who fear their money could be lost for good. (AP)

SECTION: Section B; Page 1; Column 4; Metropolitan Desk

HEADLINE: Giuliani To Propose a Budget To Cut Less and Spend More

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY 

 BODY:
Taking advantage of a rebounding economy as he begins his re-election campaign, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani plans to announce a preliminary budget today that would include the first overall increase in city spending and the fewest reductions in services that he has recommended since becoming Mayor.
Mr. Giuliani said in an interview last night that the $34 billion spending plan for the 1998 fiscal year would propose $250 million in tax cuts, including the permanent elimination of the city's share of the 8.25 percent sales tax for clothing under $500. Under the Mayor's proposal, the tax would drop to 4.25 percent, with 4 percent going to the state and one-quarter of 1 percent to the Metropolitan Transportation Authority. 
 Mr. Giuliani urged the state and the M.T.A. to abolish their portion of the sales tax as well, repeating a position he voiced during the one-week repeal of the tax last week, which proved popular with shoppers.
The Mayor also said that he wanted to abolish or scale back the unincorporated business tax, which mostly affects small businesses, and cut the commercial rent tax.
Under his plan, spending for the fiscal year that begins on July 1 would rise about one-half of 1 percent. "It's a very modest increase," Mr. Giuliani said.
The proposed fiscal blueprint is a striking departure from the austere budgets that were a hallmark of the first three years of the Mayor's tenure. Perhaps nothing is more indicative of the change than this: Mr. Giuliani, who has cut 21,000 city jobs since he took office, is proposing no new reductions in the municipal work force, which is now at slightly more than 200,000 people.
Experts who study the city's finances said the big question confronting Mr. Giuliani is what to do with the extra tax revenue from the economic upturn, particularly whether he should salt it away to help deal with the city's chronic budget troubles.
Administration officials said last night that in general, the Mayor had decided to spend about one-third, use one-third to pay for tax cuts and save the rest to cover future deficits.
By law, the Mayor must present a preliminary budget in January that provides an overview of the city's spending over the next four years. After receiving comments from the City Council and fiscal monitors, he then issues a final budget in May, which the Council then approves, often after amending it. Most tax cuts, however, are subject to approval by the State Legislature.
The Mayor acknowledged last night that he would still call for cuts at city agencies, but he stressed that those reductions would be far less than the ones he made in previous years. Administration officials said they would seek about $550 million in cuts for the 1998 budget, though they will probably end up with closer to $200 million.
In the interview, Mr. Giuliani declined to say which agencies would bear the brunt of the cuts, saying he would announce that when the budget is made public today.
The Mayor must cut services even while proposing an overall increase in spending because of the structure of the budget. Labor and other costscontinue to grow at up to 5 percent a year, so for the Mayor to limit the overall spending increase to only one-half of 1 percent, he needs to cut elsewhere.
As he first promised in his annual address to the City Council this month, the Mayor will propose at least $275 million in added spending for the schools, administration officials said. He would allocate $125 million to provide six hours of additional reading instruction a week to grade-school students and $150 million over the next two years to buy 40,000 personal computers for the schools.
Randy M. Mastro, the Deputy Mayor for Operations, said "several hundred million dollars" would be added to the city's capital budget for school construction to lessen overcrowding.
The Mayor's Democratic opponents have signaled that they believe he is vulnerable on education issues, charging that he has neglected the public school system and deprived it of needed funding.
The city faces a projected budget gap of $1.4 billion to $1.6 billion for the 1998 fiscal year, but Mr. Giuliani is presented with a more palatable menu of choices in closing it than he did in the first three years in his term. The local economy, which had been anemic, has strengthened, largely because of robust profits on Wall Street, giving the city more tax dollars.
As a result, the Mayor will have a surplus of nearly $400 million from the current fiscal year that he can roll into the next one. His aides are also projecting that tax revenues will rise for the 1998 budget. "We are going into next year with some room to spare," Mr. Giuliani said.
The budget is not without risks, though. If the stock market sours, tax revenues might plummet, forcing Mr. Giuliani to seek more reductions at a time when he is trying to soften his image as a fiscal hawk.
What's more, the relatively rosy fiscal picture turns darker in 1999 and 2000, when the city must grapple with projected shortfalls of $2 billion to $3 billion annually, largely because expenses are expected to continue to rise faster than revenues.
Asked why he was proposing cutting taxes and services at the same time, Mr. Giuliani said it was important to stimulate the city's economy by lessening the tax burden, which is among the highest in the nation.
"This is really part of a budget philosophy that says that as you are realizing extra revenues, you should do three things," he said. "You should spend some, save some to reduce the gaps, and put the rest back into the private sector. What the city has by and large done in the past is just spend any additional revenues."
He conceded that this less bleak budget might help his re-election. But he insisted that the city would not be in a position to benefit from the new tax revenues if it were still spending at high rate, contending that he had cut $7 billion to $9 billion in projected spending since he took office.
"I understand that some of this is fortuitous," he said, "but in fact we could not take advantage of the good fortune if we hadn't taken the heat for making the budget cuts two and three years ago."

HEADLINE: Real Campaign Reform Will Give Access to All

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re "State of the Sleaze" (editorial, Jan. 28): The scary thing about campaign finance reform proposals is that they always seem to leave room for the purchase of influence with huge contributions. President Clinton, for example, suggests a cap of $100,000 for party donations -- as if that will bring access to government equally within the reach of every citizen. 
 Eliminating the clout of the wealthy, corporations and special interest groups with elected officials requires lowering the maximum for contributions to a level anyone can afford. It is true that a cap of, say, $50 for all contributions would be hard for politicians to swallow, as it would probably mean an end to their packaging by expensive spin doctors like Arthur J. Finkelstein and Dick Morris. But would it really be that bad for the country for folks like that to be unemployed?
 
D. M. LYNN
Dallas, Jan. 28, 1997

HEADLINE: In Business;
Brazilians Excel With Shopping Bags

BYLINE: By LISA W. FODERARO 

 BODY:
Canadians may dominate the tourism landscape in New York City in numbers of visitors, and the Japanese may be the fastest-growing market. But the biggest spenders are the Brazilians, according to a Federal survey of passengers taken during arriving flights.
Tourists from Brazil spent an average of $1,296 each per visit, compared with the second and third place Argentina ($1,070) and Japan ($837), the survey shows. While Japanese visitors actually spend more each day than visitors from Brazil, $279 compared with $216, the average stay for a Brazilian tourist in the city was six days compared with three days for the Japanese traveler. 
 The number of Brazilians planning a holiday in New York City is way up as well.
In 1995, the most recent year for which statistics are available, 274,000 people came to New York from Brazil, a 41 percent increase over the previous year. The survey shows that 84 percent of Brazilian tourists stay in a hotel.
All of this did not happen by chance. A few years ago, the New York Convention and Visitors Bureau zeroed in on Brazil as a fertile but untapped market. "We saw an improving economy, and we traveled down there with our wares, meeting with tour operators and travel agents," said Jeanine Moss, spokeswoman for the bureau, a nonprofit organization under contract with New York City to promote tourism.
"There's one other thing that's really great about the Brazilians," Ms. Moss added. "They come in the winter." LISA W. FODERARO

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Falls as Tokyo Official Hints at Intervention, After All

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar fell against leading currencies yesterday after an influential Japanese official suggested that the nation's central bank might sell dollars in the foreign-exchange market if necessary to defend the weakening yen.
Traders were caught off guard by the remarks, from Eisuke Sakakibara, director of the Finance Ministry's international bureau and an architect of Japan's foreign-exchange policy. Other senior finance officials in Japan had been suggesting that the central bank would not intervene. 
 Many traders reacted by selling dollars to lock in profits from a 4.5 percent dollar rise against the yen this month. It was the first drop in the dollar here in nearly a week.
Further strength in the mark also helped undermine the dollar, after a suggestion on Wednesday by the president of the German central bank, Hans Tietmeyer, that the mark's prolonged depreciation against the dollar was nearly over.
Nonetheless, traders said the underlying demand for the dollar remained strong, and many expected it to resume rising. They attributed the selloff after Mr. Sakakibara's comments to a general nervousness over how quickly the dollar had risen in recent weeks.
Late in New York yesterday, the dollar settled at 121.79 yen, down from 122.08 yen on Wednesday. It traded at 1.6368 marks, down from 1.6434. The British pound rose slightly, to $1.6213 from $1.6210.
Mr. Sakakibara's remarks, made during early Asian dealings, overshadowed comments in support of the dollar by other officials. Secretary of the Treasury Robert E. Rubin stressed that the United States' position on the dollar had not changed, and Yasuo Matsushita, governor of Japan's central bank, reiterated that economic recovery there remained mild.
Some traders said the yen's rise might have reflected Japanese investors' conversion of foreign holdings into yen to help cover losses in the stock market at home. The benchmark Nikkei stock index, which has dropped for much of the last month, fell 2.57 percent yesterday.
"The Japanese are pulling money back," said Ron Hudson, vice president for corporate sales at the Royal Bank of Scotland in New York.

HEADLINE: Now Managed Care Cutbacks Mean That All of Us Get to Suffer

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The draconian economics of managed care cuts across class lines, as your Jan. 26 front-page article on the loss of jobs by senior doctors and nurses illustrates.
When the private clinic we use became affiliated with a large, renowned hospital, experienced doctors retired, disheartened by the imposition of unrealistic controls, and computer technicians replaced nurses familiar with patients. 
 Insurance companies require the pharmacist to question physician judgments and confuse patients by offering, for example, a less effective medication. Patients recovering from certain surgeries find their insurance premiums increased from $1,500 to $15,000.
At the hospital, many skilled nurses have been fired, floor nurses fresh from school are hesitant and cheerless, and aides will not perform basic services. The "state of the art" computers and medical equipment are finicky and break down. My records have been lost for a year, and according to the hospital billing service, I am responsible for the fees of an unknown (to me) 8-year-old.
Perhaps now that greater numbers of dedicated professionals and their middle-class patients are suffering the consequences of the dissection of medical care, more people will speak out against the changes.
 
DIANE T. NORTH
San Mateo, Calif., Jan. 26, 1997

HEADLINE: Spain's Tangled Labor Scene: Some in Payless Limbo

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: ALCORCON, Spain

 BODY:
Last September, the electrical equipment company where Jose Tabanera has worked for 24 years gave him his last pay envelope.
The company neither dismissed him nor disappeared into bankruptcy. Instead, it consigned Mr. Tabanera and 60 fellow employees, most in their mid-50's, to a peculiarly Spanish kind of limbo where nobody knows for sure how many people are unemployed, but everybody knows it is too many. 
 With either 21.8 percent or 13.8 percent of the labor force unemployed, depending on which set of official figures are used, Spain has more jobless people than any other Western European country.
The conservative minority Government says that strict labor laws that are a legacy of Spain's fascist dictatorship and more than a decade of subsequent Socialist democracy discourage employers from taking on new workers.
Prime Minister Jose Maria Aznar's Government argues that Spain cannot function in a globally competitive economy or take full advantage of the common European currency planned for 1999 without labor legislation that gives employers and foreign investors more flexibility. It is hoping that talks under way between labor unions and employers will produce suggestions for acceptable change.
"The Government will probably wait two or three months before deciding what to do," said Jordi Pujol, the powerful Catalan regional leader. He said he agreed that change was as necessary as the budget deficit cuts and lower interest rates the Government is encouraging to permit Spain to be part of the common European currency.
American-style layoffs and job-switching do not appear to be in the cards for Spain, said Nicolas Franco, a nephew of the late dictator who is a businessman here. "Longer temporary contracts that gave small, mobile companies more freedom to lay off employees for two years or so would help, and also give young people more security," he said.
In more turbulent times, Gen. Francisco Franco's Government decreed that workers would basically have their jobs for life. After Franco died, democratic Spain, like most of its Continental European counterparts, wrote job security into social legislation.
Mr. Aznar's Socialist predecessor as Prime Minister, Felipe Gonzalez, modified the law to allow companies to bypass the severance pay requirements by hiring younger workers on short-term contracts. The companies do not have to give these workers severance pay when the contracts expire.
One result has been the creation of short-term jobs, about 250,000 of them last year alone. But insecurity about how long they will last adds to the confusion in the labor markets, economists say.
"The official unemployment figures are higher than the real ones," said Prof. Amando de Miguel, a sociologist who does consulting in market research.
One set of Spanish official unemployment figures -- the higher one -- is based on answers to questions by statistical samples of job applicants at Government employment offices.
"People lie," said Carmen Alcaide, an economist for the Banco Bilbao Vizcaya. The Government also reports the number of people actually registered and collecting unemployment benefits -- 2.2 million people in December, or 13.8 percent, a figure not so far out of line with the European Union average of 11 percent, she said.
Offering anecdotal evidence of a lower Spanish unemployment rate, Mr. de Miguel said: "I gave a training course for college-level people last year and accepted 40 students. All of them were officially registered as unemployed, but on the first day of the course only 10 or 12 showed up -- the rest said they didn't realize the course was so time-consuming, and they couldn't take so much time out of their work schedules."
If this is fraud, for the most part the Spanish wink at it rather than get outraged, perhaps because almost everybody these days has a relative who is out of work or looking for it. Jobless students, like Mr. Tabanera's daughter, often live with their parents at home into their late 20's.
"There is a certain optimism that I detect that the economy is going to do better than they thought it was," said Richard N. Gardner, the United States Ambassador to Spain. "Unemployment is coming down, inflation is down, interest rates are low," he said. "There are no beggars on the street. There isn't a crisis atmosphere."
One of Mr. Tabanera's two daughters, Ana Tabanera Mambrilla, said she believed her father's company, Conymon 2000 S.A., was trying to pressure him into quitting so it would not have to make the legally mandatory severance payments.
"Then they could hire younger workers on short-term contracts and pay them less," she said. She is a 23-year-old Madrid University graduate who has not been able to find steady work since obtaining her degree in psychology last June.
Her father and some of his fellow employees turned to their labor union for help in their situation, which is unusual even for Spain. The union has asked a labor court to rule on whether the company has technically dismissed them and has to pay severance anyway.
"If companies can dismiss old people and pay young people nothing, what have we been working for?" complained Mr. Tabanera, a wiry and wizened 55-year-old electrical equipment installer whose take-home pay was $1,000 a month.
Under the Spanish law, a labor court could find that his employer had dismissed him, and force it to pay him up to 45 days' severance pay for every year he worked, or about $48,000, and possibly back pay since September as well.
Until he is declared officially unemployed, Mr. Tabanera cannot collect Government benefits, so he keeps taking the bus to the company's premises in Madrid every day, showing up for the work that is no longer there and hoping to preserve his rights under the law. The company has not told him why it has stopped paying him.
Meanwhile, like families all over Spain, the Tabanera family rallies. Mr. Tabanera's wife makes some money cleaning apartments. Their elder daughter's husband and a neighbor down the hall of their nine-story building here in a working-class suburb of Madrid have given them loans to help them pay off a home improvement loan on the two-bedroom apartment they bought 15 years ago.

HEADLINE: In America;
One More Time

BYLINE: By BOB HERBERT 

 BODY:
Memo to David Dinkins: It's over.
Like an aging boxer unaware of the degree to which his skills have diminished, Mr. Dinkins is entertaining the idea of climbing into the ring again. He would fight -- and probably win -- a tuneup in the Democratic mayoral primary. He would then go on to be demolished by the current champ. 
 It is hard to believe Mr. Dinkins is serious about this. But there he was yesterday at a crowded midtown press conference, making a campaign-style attack on Rudolph Giuliani and insisting that he -- Mr. Dinkins -- just might make the run. It was clear that the former Mayor was enjoying himself.
A retired politician once told me: "You never get used to being out of the spotlight. When you're on top it's a rush like you wouldn't believe -- adoring crowds, television cameras, your face all over the newspapers. It's incredible. And then you lose, and suddenly it's over. That's very hard to take. It's like a junkie going cold turkey. More than anything, you want to get that rush back."
In New York City there is an additional factor that looms large. Mayors and former mayors, at least in recent years, carry with them at all times a bulging sack of resentments, grudges and fantasies of payback. If you scrapped all the rules and put Ed Koch, David Dinkins and Rudolph Giuliani into a ring together, you would find out what extreme fighting is really like.
Very few people who like and respect Mr. Dinkins think a rerun is a good idea. The former Mayor told The Times's Adam Nagourney that he was being urged by "big fans of mine" to challenge Mr. Giuliani. That's not surprising; it's what big fans do. "You can beat that guy," they say. "He's no match for you. Why don't you take him on?"
It's supposed to make you feel good. If you are David Dinkins at age three score and nine (as he put it yesterday), the correct response to such nonsense is to smile, say thanks and go out and play a few sets of tennis. You're not supposed to be gullible enough to take it seriously.
Now that the story has broken, Mr. Dinkins's biggest fans are asking if he's taken leave of his senses. Not only do they not think he can beat Mr. Giuliani, they think he is unfairly undermining the already slim chance that anybody else can beat him.
"I can't believe this is going anywhere," said a former Dinkins adviser who asked not to be identified. "David hasn't raised any money, he hasn't put a committee together, he had heart surgery a year or two ago, and fairly or unfairly, he is widely perceived to be on the wrong side of the crime issue, which of course is Rudy's great strength. And if he does run, oh boy, Ruth and Freddie and a lot of other Democrats are going to hate him."
Ruth and Freddie are the Manhattan Borough President, Ruth Messinger, and the Bronx Borough President, Fernando Ferrer, who at the moment are the leading Democratic challengers to Mr. Giuliani.
Mr. Dinkins has long felt that he and Mr. Giuliani have been judged by different standards. The former Mayor believes, for example, that he never received the credit he deserved for his "Safe Streets, Safe City" program, which expanded the police force by 6,000 officers and provided funding for beacon schools and other successful youth programs. And he believes that he was unfairly saddled with a reputation for not handling the city's fiscal affairs well.
But that is old business. New Yorkers are benefiting from an improved national economy. Crime statistics have fallen dramatically. And the personal bitterness between Mr. Dinkins and Mr. Giuliani would likely obscure rather than clarify those issues on which Mr. Giuliani should be vulnerable -- education and the delivery of social services, to name two.
At his press conference, Mr. Dinkins described Mr. Giuliani as a "bully" who "rules through fear and intimidation." And he spoke with feeling about Mr. Giuliani's failure to rein in police brutality.
Then the former Mayor hurried off to catch a plane to South Africa.
Mr. Dinkins's friends are already mobilizing, but not for a run for mayor. His closest friends will try, as diplomatically as possible, to talk him out of running. They will try, out of their genuine affection for him, to convince him that it's really over.

HEADLINE: First Lady Re-emerges, With an Aid Plan for Tiny Businesses

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
On her own terms and armed with her famously formidable command of policy detail, Hillary Rodham Clinton edged back into the spotlight today -- though the policies seemed smaller than they once did, and the details less liable to send lobbyists to their telephones, or Republicans around the bend.
For the first time in more than two years, Mrs. Clinton met with reporters who cover the White House, answering questions crisply, cheerfully and confidently for half an hour in the Map Room, where Franklin D. Roosevelt monitored the progress of World War II. 
 She was in part reintroducing herself, aides acknowledged, by promoting an embryonic idea with a small-sounding name -- "micro-enterprise" -- that Mrs. Clinton argued would help confront the big issues of poverty and personal growth that she has grappled with for 25 years.
Micro-enterprise "may look like a kind of small issue at first glance," she acknowledged, after describing how tiny loans of a few thousand dollars or even $300 could begin businesses in developing countries, or in the inner cities of the United States.
"But this is a very big idea," Mrs. Clinton continued. "And it's a big idea because it takes governmental catalytic action, that will directly affect some people's lives, but more than that, create a new environment for private economic activity, that could have very long term consequences."
It was not as big an idea, certainly, as changing the entire health care system, an effort that she once commanded for President Clinton, overseeing a staff of 300 economists, health policy experts and Government employees.
But, when asked if her views had changed, Mrs. Clinton, more in amused exasperation than aggravation, declared that she never favored excessive government control.
"I believe in the balance of power," she exclaimed, using a term she has invoked frequently in speeches in recent weeks. "I believe in the balance of power in government, I believe in the balance of power between government and the private sector, I believe in the balance of power between men and women."
She added: "And I think that if you tilt too far one way or the other, you run into trouble."
But while the Hillary Clinton who testified on Capitol Hill about health care was not on display today, neither was the Hillary Clinton of the 1996 Presidential campaign, who shunned reporters and stuck mostly to safe topics and friendly crowds.
Instead, as Mrs. Clinton sat at a long table, which was covered with a white cloth and ringed by about 16 reporters, she spoke easily about transfer payments and capital formation in the inner city and did not hesitate to describe big dreams for a small Federal program to encourage micro-enterprise -- defined as businesses with five employees or fewer.
"What I would hope that would come out of this emphasis on micro-enterprise," she said, "is a greater appreciation in our society at large about the capacities of people at all levels in our country."
The questions clung mostly to Mrs. Clinton's chosen topic, as she intended. But even at the end, when reporters apologetically began to ask her about her role in creating a White House database whose use Congressional Republicans are investigating, her tone remained even and her manner friendly.
With a deliberate absence of fanfare, Mrs. Clinton has been delicately re-emerging as a public promoter of her own ideas and favorite programs since President Clinton was re-elected.
"She's still trying to find her way on what's the best, most comfortable way for her in her public appearances," a senior official said, adding that Mrs. Clinton had had a "historically rough ride" as First Lady.
After meeting with reporters, Mrs. Clinton made her way upstairs to the East Room, to join her husband in presenting the Presidential Awards for Excellence in Micro-enterprise Development. There, Mr. Clinton said: "I'd also like to thank the First Lady for beginning this obsession, almost, that we have with microenterprise."
"Since I became President, we have tried to go national with this micro idea in a very macro way," Mr. Clinton said.
The White House said today that it would seek $125 million from Congress this year for a Treasury Department fund that in part contributes to organizations supporting micro-enterprise. Over five years, Mr. Clinton said, he would ask for $1 billion.
Last year, the White House also sought $125 million for the fund, but Congress appropriated only $45 million.
In the United States, micro-enterprise loans are defined as ones of less than $25,000. Overseas, where the United States extends such credit through the Agency for International Development, the loans run closer to $300. Repayment rates run well over 90 percent, officials said.
Mrs. Clinton said the programs were examples of the Administration's efforts to act as a catalyst. In keeping with her husband's second Inaugural Address, she offered a spirited defense of Government's role -- combining it with a wry disclaimer of her own influence on the subject.
"There will be and continue to be certain functions that I would argue -- speaking only for myself and not anybody else -- that only the Government can do and other functions that only the market can do," she said. "But as with life you will find that much of the rest of it is in this kind of grey middle, and that's where the debate should be."

HEADLINE: Mayors Now and Then;
A Re-election Budget

 BODY:
Running hard for re-election, Mayor Rudolph Giuliani has presented a $34 billion spending plan for New York City that eases up on the austerity he has pushed since taking office in 1994. In the next year the Mayor wants to cut taxes, including the sales tax on clothing, and spend more money for schools, police and the environment. This bonanza of crowd-pleasing initiatives is to be financed by increased tax revenues from the city's economic boom and an array of fiscal gimmicks that budget monitors and credit-rating agencies have rightly deplored. Mr. Giuliani's thinking on tax cuts and some spending priorities is sound, though his bookkeeping is not.
Mr. Giuliani followed a familiar pattern for New York City mayors by putting his most painful budget cuts in his early budgets, then offering popular spending and tax programs in an election year. This Mayor enters the campaign, however, blessed with a recovering economy and lower crime and welfare rates. With self-confidence and even humor, he claimed credit for everything favorable that has happened in the city, including this season's 92 percent drop in snowfall. It was a pretty cocky performance, masking the fact that the city's legislative battles in the coming year are likely to be as bruising as they have ever been. 
 A major fight is likely to erupt over his demand that the 8.25 percent city and state sales tax on clothing be eliminated, with Democrats worried about helping the Mayor's re-election drive. If the money is available, however, cutting the sales tax is a good idea. The brief experiment of temporarily dropping the tax earlier this month led to a burst in sales, with some shoppers apparently buying clothes in the city instead of the suburbs, where the tax is lower. The evidence needs to be sifted, but cutting taxes on less expensive clothing is a progressive way of reducing the tax burden and perhaps of creating new retail jobs.
Cutting business and rent taxes for smaller enterprises also makes sense, in theory. But it remains to be seen whether there are enough savings in Mr. Giuliani's proposed budget to justify talk of new tax cuts, including those for clothing. The Mayor's budget, for example, would cut subsidies for municipal hospitals and embrace the Medicaid cuts to hospitals, nursing homes and community health centers proposed by Gov. George Pataki. The Legislature has rejected such cuts in the past and is likely to do so again. Mr. Giuliani also wants the state to increase aid to New York City by advancing revenue-sharing payments from the future into the current fiscal year, the kind of gimmickry that plunged both the city and state into their fiscal crises of two decades ago.
The Mayor's plans call for projecting savings from hundreds of millions of dollars in revenues from dubious sources like sales of assets and rent from the Port Authority for the city's airports. His budget would be balanced further by rolling over the revenue surplus from the current year. Using such one-shot savings to pay for recurring expenses is one of the main reasons the city projects multibillion-dollar deficits into the future, a pattern that this budget fails to break.
Mr. Giuliani's plans are sure to provoke fights in Albany this year over school aid and welfare. In both areas, Governor Pataki's proposed budget punishes the city. Another unfortunate feature of the Mayor's budget is its failure to use the police more efficiently by replacing police officers performing clerical duty with civilians earning lower pay.
Generally, there was too little talk in Mr. Giuliani's budget of demands for more productivity from municipal employees. But of course no mayor likes to stir up city workers at election time. As campaign budgets go, this one was predictably political in places, but also marked with flashes of creativity.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Salinas Speaks Out, About Plot, Brother and Regrets

BYLINE: By SAM DILLON 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Former President Carlos Salinas de Gortari broke a long silence this week, detailing his views on the political mayhem and economic chaos that marked the end of his six-year tenure and converted him into one of the most vilified presidents in Mexican history.
Mr. Salinas's statements, apparently an effort to regain some measure of public esteem by vigorously defending his own record, were his most extensive since he left office in December 1994 and went into exile three months later. 
Mr. Salinas, who now lives in Ireland, made his remarks in a lengthy interview in Boston with Ramon Alberto Garza, executive editor of the jointly owned newspapers Reforma of Mexico City and El Norte of Monterrey. The papers published transcripts of Mr. Salinas's statements on Wednesday and today. The final installment is to be published Friday.
In the interview, Mr. Salinas made these points:
*He described a conspiracy by a clique of senior politicians from his governing party in the days after the 1994 assassination of Mexico's leading presidential contender to impose a replacement candidate allied with their interests.
*He acknowledged his failure to supervise his brother Raul, who transferred more than $100 million into foreign accounts, but insisted he has no idea where the money came from.
*He repeated his belief in Raul's innocence on murder charges.
*He said he misses Mexico and intends to return.
Mr. Salinas, whose Institutional Revolutionary Party, known as the PRI, has controlled Mexico's authoritarian system since the late 1920's, defended the electoral and free-market reforms he carried out between 1988 and 1994.
Those changes, which led to the North American Free Trade Agreement, hurt many PRI officials, and those whose business and other interests were harmed saw in Luis Donaldo Colosio, Mr. Salinas's choice to succeed him, a champion of further change, he said in the interview. Mr. Colosio was shot to death at a rally in Tijuana on March 23, 1994.
"If until now the investigation has not produced elements to prove there was a plot to assassinate Luis Donaldo, I nevertheless believe there was a plot to impose his replacement," Mr. Salinas said. "And those that promoted it appear to be continuing without rest in their tremendous struggle for power."
Once before, in a public letter released in December 1995, Mr. Salinas referred to a conspiracy he said he had first perceived shortly after Mr. Colosio's killing. In the interview he provided new details, describing how the assassination nearly overwhelmed his Government and rocked the country financially.
"In the dawn, we had to convoke an emergency meeting of the economic cabinet," Mr. Salinas said. "During more than a week we had the highest levels of capital flight in our history."
"And in the middle of this personal and national tragedy and the financial danger, a political struggle broke out to occupy Luis Donaldo's place," he said. "That was probably Mexico's most delicate political crisis in more than 60 years."
Hours after the assassination, Mr. Salinas said, former President Luis Echeverria, whose rule from 1970 to 1976 was devoted to extending state control of the economy, appeared suddenly at the presidential palace and proposed Emilio Gamboa, then the Secretary of Communications and Transport, as a candidate to replace Mr. Colosio.
Other politicians allied with Mr. Echeverria, Mr. Salinas said, carried on an "intense offensive" to promote another PRI candidate he did not name. A third group fought to prevent Mr. Salinas from choosing Ernesto Zedillo, who nonetheless was nominated and elected.
When Mr. Salinas first referred to a conspiracy in December 1995, Mr. Echeverria ridiculed the theory, describing himself as a grandfather incapable of coordinating a conspiracy. "I don't coordinate anybody, not even my children," he said.
In Mexico's political system, presidents have been all-powerful while occupying the executive palace but are expected to fade away quietly when they leave office. These rules have been eroding, however, and Mr. Salinas's latest statements have provided a striking example of that.
Raul Salinas is imprisoned here on charges of financial wrongdoing and accusations that he was the mastermind behind the slaying in 1994 of Jose Francisco Ruiz Massieu, the PRI's No. 2 official, whom prosecutors have described as a rival to the Salinas brothers. The former President reiterated his belief in Raul's innocence on the murder charges.
In a development unrelated to Mr. Salinas's published interview, Mexican prosecutors questioned Mr. Salinas for 16 hours in Dublin on Monday about the Ruiz Massieu killing, but have released no information about his statements. In November, prosecutors questioned him about the Colosio assassination.
Questioned in the newspaper interview about Raul's vast foreign deposits, Mr. Salinas repeated assertions that he knew nothing about them during his presidency, but moved further toward acknowledging apparent wrongdoing.
"My brother Raul's behavior has profoundly hurt me, my siblings, and my Government," Mr. Salinas said. "Above all, it has hurt the public."
"I can assure you that whatever their origin, those funds have no relation with any action taken in my administration through my direct decisions or instructions," he said.
"I never tried to review his personal finances," he said. "Now I lament that."
After Raul's arrest in February 1995, Mr. Salinas criticized the detention, and when the former President left Mexico days later, presidential aides said Mr. Zedillo had ordered him into exile. But this week an adviser to Mr. Zedillo said that, like any other citizen, Mr. Salinas was free to come and go.
Mr. Salinas said that since he left Mexico he has lived in the United States and Montreal as well as in Ireland, and that last year he and his wife, a former aide, traveled to Cuba for the birth of their child.
"If the daughter of an ex-President of Mexico could not be born in Mexico because of the atmosphere of persecution that has been whipped up against me, she should be born in a country with dignified people who love their sovereignty," he said.
He acknowledged that he misses Mexico. "I'll come back," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Carlos Salinas de Gortari, Mexico's ex-President, in Cambridge, Mass. He denied any connection to the slaying of his chosen successor in 1994. (Reforma)

HEADLINE: Groundbreaking Near for Trump Project, Despite Upper West Side Objections

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
With several of New York City's long-dormant projects springing to life in Times Square, Columbus Circle and elsewhere, one of its biggest and most contested real estate ventures, Donald J. Trump's plan for a small new city along the Hudson River, is about to take the form of bricks and mortar.
Mr. Trump received a building permit this week to break ground on the first of 16 apartment towers that are to be part of the project, called Riverside South. It would cover 75 acres along the Hudson River between 59th and 72d Streets. Mr. Trump said construction would begin within a week. 
 The first building, a 46-story, 377-unit condominium tower between 69th and 70th Streets, east of the West Side Highway, cleared its biggest hurdle in December, when the Department of Environmental Protection issued a permit for it to be connected to the North River Sewage Treatment Plant, despite protests that it would overburden the plant.
The groundbreaking will be a turning point in what might be New York City's longest development dispute, pitting the fervent political activism of Upper West Side community groups against a series of ambitious developers who have coveted the Hudson River property, now controlled by Mr. Trump, since the 1960's.
Now, with construction set to begin, many West Side residents are resigned to accepting some, but not all, of Mr. Trump's plan. When completed, the Riverside South complex is to include 5,700 apartments.
As is his frequent pattern, Mr. Trump has received financial commitments from outside investors, in this case Asians, for almost all of the project's cost. The project is expected to take at least 10 years to complete, depending on demand for the apartments. But because of the city's improving economy and tight housing market, that timetable might be shortened.
"People on the West Side still do not want to accept this project, but it seems clear that Trump is going to build," said Ronnie M. Eldridge, the City Councilwoman whose district includes Riverside South. She has long opposed Mr. Trump's plan.
The biggest question confronting the project now is over the future of the recently rebuilt section of the West Side Highway that passes on elevated pilings between the Hudson River and the lower floors of the buildings planned by Mr. Trump.
Despite the protests of many on the Upper West Side, a political fight over the highway appeared to be resolved last year when Representative Jerrold L. Nadler succeeded in cutting off Federal funds for environmental studies that might have led to reconstruction of the highway.
The intent was to rebuild it under Riverside Drive, removing the elevated section of pavement, and opening the land beneath it for a park with unimpeded views of the Hudson.
"People will make an effort to revive it, but they will fail," Mr. Nadler said.
Efforts to resurrect the highway project are expected to reach a high point by Feb. 24, the deadline for Congress to submit transportation projects to be considered for Federal funding.
"It is an uphill fight, but I don't know how anyone would want to try to explain to people on the Upper West Side how they got Donald Trump and not a decent park," said Richard Kahan, a Manhattan lawyer who led an unusual coalition -- the Municipal Arts Society, the Parks Council and others -- that worked with Mr. Trump to create the current Riverside South plan, which until last year was to have included the highway reconstruction project.

HEADLINE: News Summary

 BODY:

INTERNATIONAL A3-13
 
India Remembers Gandhi As His Ashes Enter Ganges
Some of the ashes of Mohandas K. Gandhi were poured into the Ganges River near Allahabad, 49 years after he was assassinated. Most of Gandhi's ashes were spread throughout India in 1948, but some had remained forgotten in a provincial bank vault. The event prompted a national debate about the legacy of the father of India's independence from Britain. A1 

Salinas Speaks Out, From Exile
Breaking a long silence, the exiled former Mexican President, Carlos Salinas de Gortari, spoke of the political and economic chaos that made the end of his six-year tenure a debacle and forced him to flee to Ireland. He intends to return to Mexico. A3
 
3 Israelis Killed in Lebanon
A roadside bomb killed three Israeli soldiers in southern Lebanon, and the Iranian-backed Hezbollah, or Party of God, claimed responsibility. Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu demanded that Syria, which controls large parts of Lebanon, rein in Hezbollah. Mounting casualties in southern Lebanon have fueled a debate in Israel over the value of keeping troops there. A8
 
Sharansky Visits a Prison Cell
On the last day of an exhausting visit to Russia, during which he met with many Russian Jews, Natan Sharansky, the former Soviet dissident turned Israeli Cabinet minister, visited the punishment cell in which he spent 16 months at a Moscow prison. He also fulfilled a childhood dream by seeing his winning chess game against the world champion, Garry Kasparov, published in a Pravda youth publication. A10
 
Moscow Digs In on NATO Talks
No good, but only bad, would come from the expansion of NATO to Russian boundaries, Prime Minister Viktor Chernomyrdin of Russia warned in an address to business and political leaders attending the World Economic Forum in Davos, Switzerland. But NATO's Secretary General, Javier Solana, was optimistic that an agreement could be reached. A10
 
Albright Updates a Tradition
Traditionally, new Secretaries of State take a fast-paced trip overseas with the focus on allies. But Madeleine K. Albright is spreading her net wider, visiting nine countries, including Russia and China. She departs on Feb. 15 and makes her last stop on Feb. 24 in Beijing, where she is expected to discuss the thorny issue of human rights. (AP)
 
Arafat Opposes Extradition
Yasir Arafat added to the pressure Arabs are putting on Washington not to extradite a leader of the militant Islamic group Hamas to Israel, an aide to the P.L.O. leader said. Hamas wants Mousa Mohammed Abu Marzook, its political leader, sent to a third country. He has been held by immigration officials in New York since July 1995, but this week he withdrew an appeal against his extradition. (Reuters)
 
NATIONAL A14-24
 
Medicaid Spending Limit Draws Governors' Fire
The National Governors' Association has decided to oppose President Clinton's plan to set firm limits on Federal Medicaid spending, officials said. The governors fear that it would force states to spend more on health care for poor people. A1
 
Technicians Criticize F.B.I. Lab
Technicians at the F.B.I.'s crime lab were highly critical of sloppy or unscientific handling of evidence in the Oklahoma City bombing, Justice Department investigators say. A1
 
Bankers Lost, Rubin Says
When President Clinton and top banking regulators met with leading bank officials at a coffee last May, three issues worth billions of dollars to the bankers came up. But the Treasury Secretary, Robert E. Rubin, said the Administration opposed the bankers. A1
 
I.R.S. Concedes a Debacle
Because its $4 billion project to develop a computer system to handle tax returns does not work, the Internal Revenue Service proposed contracting out to private companies the processing of paper returns filed by individuals. That move would allow private workers to see confidential information about taxpayers. A1
 
Hurdles for Clinton Nominees
The Senate Intelligence Committee has delayed hearings on the nomination of Anthony Lake to head the C.I.A. for two weeks because he had failed to sell some energy stocks. The White House had advised him to sell the stocks to avoid a conflict of interest. Alexis Herman, President Clinton's choice to head the Labor Department, is also facing a potential roadblock. But Mr. Lake and Ms. Herman are the first of Mr. Clinton's nominees to encounter problems in the Republican-controlled Senate, which confirmed William M. Daley as Commerce Secretary. A20
 
3 Die as Mall Wall Collapses
A wall at a Houston shopping mall collapsed as people walked through the mall for exercise before it opened. At least three people died. The wall was between the mall and a department store that was being demolished. A16
 
New Diabetes Drug Is Approved
The Food and Drug Administration approved the first of a class of drugs to attack the underlying cause of the disease. The new treatment, an insulin sensitizer, will offer those who contract the disease as adults an opportunity to reduce or eliminate their daily insulin injections. A24
 
2 Get Life in Internet Sex Case
Two California men have been sentenced to life in prison without parole in an Internet pornography case that began when a 6-year-old girl returned home from a slumber party and told of sexual abuse by the father of one of her young friends. The two, Ronald Riva and Melton L. Myers, were part of an on-line pedophilia club where users swapped stories about child sex and exchanged sexually explicit images of young girls. (AP)
 
NEW YORK/REGION B1-6
 
Giuliani Seeks $500 Million For Overcrowded Schools
Mayor Giuliani proposed spending $500 million to reduce overcrowding in the public schools. Much of the money would be spent on customized trailers and other prefabricated buildings to create places for 40,000 students. The proposal was part of the Mayor's preliminary budget. A1
 
Crown Hts. Judge Has Doubts
The judge in the Crown Heights trial strongly suggested that he would not accept a guilty verdict against one of the two defendants, who is accused of inciting the attack on a Hasidic man, unless the jurors concluded that the man was actually present at the fatal stabbing. B1
 
Dinkins in the Spotlight
Former Mayor David N. Dinkins took a turn in the spotlight after announcing that he might seek a rematch this year against Mayor Giuliani. The four announced Democratic candidates spent their day answering questions about Mr. Dinkins. Mr. Giuliani's advisers were elated. B1
 
Terms Change on Casino Road
New Jersey officials agreed to share more of the financial risk in building a road and tunnel to the site in Atlantic City where Mirage Resorts plans a casino. Transportation officials said the project was likely to cost far more than the $330 million originally expected. Mirage had threatened to walk away from the project. B6
 
Emigre's Widow Is Convicted
Rita Gluzman, a Soviet emigre who went on an 18-day hunger strike in 1971 to gain her scientist husband's exit from the Soviet Union, was found guilty of killing him with an ax last April. She faces life in prison. B5
 
Deadly Gas in Apartments
Carbon monoxide, apparently from a malfunctioning furnace, seeped through three apartment buildings on the Upper East Side. An elderly woman was found dead, possibly from the fumes, and four people were found unconscious. The toll could have been much worse if the fumes had happened at night, instead of in the early afternoon, the Fire Department said. B3

The New York Jets opened talks with the New England Patriots over Bill Parcells, one day after a ruling by the N.F.L. commissioner strengthened the Patriots' hand. B9

Greenspan Urges Inflation Fix
Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, urged Congress to move quickly to limit cost-of-living increases for Social Security and other Federal benefits by fixing statistical flaws that, economists say, cause the Government to overstate the inflation rate. His statement may provide political cover for lawmakers to make the change, which would give less money to the elderly. A1
 
Online Customers Still Angry
Many customers of America Online said they were not mollified by the company's agreement to pay millions of dollars to compensate subscribers for two months of network congestion. D1

HEADLINE: A Gentler Budget as Giuliani Faces an Election Year

BYLINE: By DAVID FIRESTONE 

 BODY:
Bucking the trend of state governments around the country that have chosen to bank much of their tax windfalls, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani presented a financial plan yesterday that would spend more than half of next year's surplus on tax cuts and new education programs.
This shift in his budget priorities coincides with the year of his re-election campaign, though he dismissed any suggestion that the two events could be related. 
 Nonetheless, the choices laid out by the Mayor in his four-year plan will have unmistakable political effects at a time when he is under attack from his Democratic challengers for having cut too much in years past. Fiscal monitors disagree on whether he is saving enough now. But there is no dispute that Mr. Giuliani has placed deficit reduction far lower on his agenda than in his first three years in office. In its place, he is now talking about "critically important" areas of new spending.
"In prior years we basically had to spend a great deal of attention on how to straighten out the huge increases in spending that had been built into the financial plan of the city," he said. "And in this financial plan we had a different challenge, which is, how do we deal with the fact that we now have what are substantial surpluses?"
As the Mayor pointed out, 42 percent of the $1.2 billion in new money he now has available will be used to reduce the city's budget gap. The remaining 58 percent will be spent this way: $442 million in new programs for education and law enforcement, and $250 million given back to taxpayers in the form of reduced sales taxes on clothing, commercial rent taxes and unincorporated business taxes -- assuming the Legislature agrees.
That contrasts with the decisions made by many states around the country that have had similar-sized surpluses in recent years due to the rebounding economy. National surveys have shown that while some states have let money trickle back to residents through tax cuts, most are salting away the larger part of the money in the face of uncertain Federal aid and fear that the boom may not last.
"It's a cardinal rule that you don't raise spending or cut taxes with revenues you can't count on year after year," said Raymond B. Horton, president of the Citizens' Budget Committee, a business-financed watchdog group. "But perhaps the Mayor believes we have entered an era when stock markets always go up." With the city having faced bankruptcy in the 1970's, and still showing a $2.5 billion gap in the year 2001, Mr. Horton said, it should not spend money so freely.
Douglas A. Criscitello, director of the city's newly formed Independent Budget Office, warned that if the economy turned sour, the city would suddenly face life with a reduced base of revenues due to the tax cuts.
Yesterday, however, the Mayor was very clearly saying that deficit reduction had its limits. Though he proposed $550 million in new cuts, that number is likely to shrink later in the year, and in any event pales in comparison with the $7 billion in projected spending he had previously cut.
Over the last three years student reading scores have declined and children are increasingly squeezed into overcrowded schools, events that have coincided with $1.2 billion in cuts to projected school spending by Mr. Giuliani. His challengers are trying to link him to deficiencies in the schools. But with his new budget proposal, the Mayor is proposing to spend $500 million on new classroom space, and $125 million on new reading instruction.
At the very least, the Mayor can now face a debating opponent later this year and point to the large new checks he has written, while opponents work to remind voters of previous years of cuts. And the Mayor will also be able to cite compliments he is now receiving from advocacy groups that have in the past been quite critical.
"The city's solvency is still key, but the Mayor has come to recognize how important education is to the quality of life and the future solvency of the city," said Gail B. Nayowith, executive director of the Citizens' Committee for Children of New York. "Whether it's a political choice or a decision he's come to after a lot of soul searching, it doesn't matter, because it has to be done. The schools are in miserable shape, and we've waited entirely too long."
Government largesse, either in the form of spending programs or tax cuts, is commonplace when elections approach, even from elected officials known to be abstemious. Though municipal spending has actually risen during his term, Mr. Giuliani had become so closely identified with budget cuts that he was the subject of charges that city services or children were suffering as a result of his actions.
In that sense, the new financial plan shows just how fortunate the Mayor has been that the economy began to bloom just when he needed it most. Without the new revenues -- $712 million of which come from increased tax collections -- it would be difficult to justify the tax cuts, and the new spending probably would not be so generous. Aides to Mr. Giuliani say they had originally calculated that the economy would accelerate much earlier in his term, which might have made the spending programs seem a bit less political than they do this year.
But a Mayor who would take credit for this year's mild winter -- as Mr. Giuliani joked yesterday -- was not about to concede that good luck had anything to do with the windfall. Without the cuts he had made earlier, he said, the city would never even have noticed the surplus.
"Luck," he said, paraphrasing Branch Rickey, "is very often the residue of design."
