HEADLINE: Wall Street Sends the City a Windfall

BYLINE: By VIVIAN S. TOY 

 BODY:
The preliminary budget Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani released yesterday not only provides a guide to his spending priorities in fiscal 1998, but it also offers New Yorkers an insight into the city's economy and a quick refresher course on how a banner year on Wall Street produces a windfall for City Hall.
The Mayor's $32.9 billion plan for the fiscal year that begins on July 1 includes more than $450 million in unanticipated tax revenue resulting entirely from the record profits on Wall Street last year. Of that amount, $391 million is already in city coffers, and the city expects to collect at least $60 million more by the end of 1997. 
 New York City's revenue from the personal income tax and the corporate business tax can surge or fall dramatically depending on the kind of year Wall Street has.
Personal income tax revenue is expected to rise to $4.17 billion in the current fiscal year from $3.9 billion in the 1996 fiscal year. City officials attributed most of the $265 million increase to income taxes on the multimillion-dollar bonuses that hundreds of Wall Street executives received at the end of 1996 and to taxes on capital gains.
General corporation tax revenue is expected to increase to $1.36 billion this fiscal year from $1.2 billion in the 1996 fiscal year. Much of that $155 million increase has flowed directly from Wall Street profits.
During his briefing on the preliminary budget yesterday, Mr. Giuliani stressed that while Wall Street's boom created a windfall, the city had been enjoying a steady economic recovery ever since 1992. He said that tourism and retail sales also rose last year and that 24,000 jobs were created in 1996.
"We had a better year last year than we anticipated," he said. "But the growth is also a product of design, because all of those increases would have been absolutely wiped out if the city had remained on the course that was set for the city on the day that my administration took office."
Rosemary Scanlon, the Deputy State Comptroller for New York City, said that despite the strong economy the city enjoyed last year, its job growth lagged behind that in the rest of the country.
The city's unemployment rate was 8.6 percent in 1996, significantly higher than the national rate of 5.4 percent, Ms. Scanlon said.
"That tells us that while the income yield has been very good in the city, primarily because of Wall Street, job growth is still very sluggish and we're still not generating enough jobs to bring down the unemployment rate," she said.
Security industry profits topped $11 billion in 1996, twice what the Giuliani administration forecast a year ago. City Hall aides said the Mayor's plan for fiscal 1998 contains conservative projections for all sources of tax revenue.
Fiscal monitors and economists yesterday applauded the Mayor's estimates, noting that his are more conservative than those of Gov. George E. Pataki.
"We'll be looking at this more closely, but it looks like a very sensible forecast for the outlook on Wall Street," said Ms. Scanlon.
"New York is as heavily dependent on Wall Street as Houston is on oil and Seattle is on Boeing. And over the years we've had plenty of reminders of how highly volatile Wall Street is."

HEADLINE: Greenspan Urges Action to Curb Cost-of-Living Rises in Benefits

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, urged Congress today to move quickly to limit cost-of-living increases for Social Security and other Federal benefits. In doing so, he waded directly into one of the most politically sensitive issues facing the Government.
Mr. Greenspan said the Labor Department should speed its efforts to fix a range of shortcomings in its main measure of inflation, the Consumer Price Index, that have led, according to estimates by some economists, to overstating increases in the cost of living by about one percentage point. But in the meantime, Mr. Greenspan said, Congress should establish an independent national commission to set more accurate cost-of-living increases. That would avoid payment of what are seen as overly generous benefits to Social Security recipients and others whose annual increases are tied to the inflation index. 
 Over time, putting a brake on cost-of-living increases could save the Government hundreds of billions of dollars and make it easier to balance the budget without cutting other programs. But it would do so by every month giving a little less money than now planned to tens of millions of voters who receive checks from the Federal Government.
"This type of approach would have the benefit of being objective, nonpartisan and sufficiently flexible to take full account of the latest information," Mr. Greenspan told the Senate Finance Committee.
Mr. Greenspan's suggestion for a commission revived an idea he had proposed two years ago, before the issue had any political visibility. It was immediately embraced by Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan of New York, the committee's ranking Democrat, and aides said it intrigued some Republicans, including the panel's chairman, Senator William V. Roth Jr. of Delaware.
Mr. Greenspan's stature provides some political cover for members of both parties who support revising the price index downward but fear the outcry from the elderly and other benefit recipients. Labor unions and the American Association for Retired Persons have already expressed opposition. Nonetheless, Mr. Greenspan's strong advocacy for the proposal could help its prospects in negotiations between Congress and the White House over how to balance the budget and could also offer a mechanism to extend the long-term solvency of Social Security.
Asked about Mr. Greenspan's call for a commission, the White House press secretary, Michael D. McCurry, said the Federal Reserve chairman would be "an influential voice" in the debate over how to address the issue. The Clinton Administration has said it is open to considering a change in the price index, but only if there is a consensus among economists about how to proceed.
Some Republicans said they were considering introducing a nonbinding "Sense of the Senate" resolution in the next few weeks calling for an improved measure of inflation. They said the vaguely worded resolution would be a first step in gauging how much support could be mustered for a change with which neither party wants to be closely identified.
The Administration also said today that it would propose increasing funds for the Bureau of Labor Statistics by $2 million next year and $7 million to $10 million for each of the succeeding six years for additional research into the price Index. The bureau now spends $40 million a year administering the index.
Katharine G. Abraham, the Commissioner of Labor Statistics, told the Senate Budget Committee that the increased funds, amounting to $55.1 million over seven years, would allow the agency "to speed up the process" of updating and expanding the price index to achieve a better approximation of changes in the cost of living. The bureau has always said that the Consumer Price Index is not a pure measure of inflation and should not be used to set Federal benefit payments. But it has taken on that role anyway, and it also adjusts tax brackets, private employment contracts and other transactions.
Mr. Greenspan has said publicly for years that he believes the Consumer Price Index overstates inflation. Last week he told the Senate Budget Committee that research by the Federal Reserve concluded that the overstatement was around one percentage point a year, roughly the same conclusion reached by a Congressionally appointed panel of economists last month.
That panel, headed by Michael J. Boskin, a Stanford University economist, said the overstatement would cost the Government hundreds of billions of dollars over the next 12 years in more generous benefit payments and smaller tax collections.
The Boskin report said that inflation was being overstated by 1.1 percentage points. Social Security payments for this year are being calculated on a 2.9 percent increase in the cost of living. If that increase was 1.1 percentage points lower, the average monthly payment, now $724, would go to $737 rather than to $745 -- a difference of $96 a year.
In his statements today, Mr. Greenspan appeared to be making his most concerted effort yet to put his reputation as a tough-minded apolitical policy maker behind a revision of the price index.
Urging senators to ignore criticism that tinkering with the price index would be viewed as a "political fix" to a technical problem, Mr. Greenspan argued that failure to address the overstatement of inflation would be a decision to ignore overwhelming evidence that the nation was overpaying benefit recipients.
"There is almost a 100 percent probability that we are overcompensating the average Social Security recipient for increases in the cost of living," Mr. Greenspan said, "and almost a 100 percent probability that we are causing the inflation-adjusted burden of the income tax system to decline more rapidly than I presume Congress intends."
Asked who should serve on an independent commission, Mr. Greenspan said he would "tend to choose academics who clearly have no evident interest in the outcome."
Congressional aides said it was unclear what consideration the Senate and the House might give to the idea of a commission. Mr. Moynihan, one of the leading proponents of revising the price index, said he intended to discuss the idea with Mr. Roth. Republican aides said Mr. Roth was interested in a commission but was concerned that it would take too long to establish.

HEADLINE: GOVERNORS OPPOSE CLINTON PROPOSAL FOR MEDICAID CAP

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
The National Governors' Association has decided to oppose President Clinton's plan to set firm limits on Federal Medicaid spending, contending that it would saddle states with more of the cost of providing health care to poor people, state officials said today.
Mr. Clinton says such limits are needed to impose fiscal discipline on the Medicaid program, which finances care for 37 million people. 
 But in a confidential draft of their new policy, the governors say: "Any unilateral Federal cap on the Medicaid program will shift costs to state and local governments that they simply cannot afford. The governors adamantly oppose a cap on Federal Medicaid spending in any form."
The governors' views are significant because state and local governments pay 42 percent of Medicaid costs: $67 billion of $159 billion last year.
The bipartisan opposition to a Federal cap represents a political setback for Mr. Clinton's Medicaid proposal, a major element of his plan to balance the Federal budget by 2002. But Republicans on Capitol Hill said they would seriously consider the proposal because it was one of the few sure ways for the Federal Government to achieve substantial Medicaid savings.
The governors open their winter meeting here on Saturday, and debates over Medicaid and welfare are expected to dominate the four-day session.
Leaders of the association sent a copy of their Medicaid recommendations to the White House today, in the hope of influencing Mr. Clinton's budget, which will be made public next week.
The Medicaid policy was developed by a panel of six governors including Bob Miller of Nevada, a Democrat who is chairman of the National Governors' Association, and George V. Voinovich of Ohio, a Republican who is vice chairman. "It is our expectation that our recommendations will be formally adopted as N.G.A. policy," they said in letters sent today to Mr. Clinton and Congressional leaders.
The governors have drafted a separate policy urging Congress to restore some of the benefits taken away from legal immigrants by the new welfare law. But Republican Congressional leaders have repeatedly said that they do not intend to make major changes in the law this year.
Among the states that would be most severely affected by a cap on Federal Medicaid spending is New York, which has one of the nation's most generous and costly Medicaid programs. It has more cases of AIDS than any other state. The cost of care is higher for them than for other Medicaid recipients and is likely to rise faster than the Federal cap, as AIDS patients seek treatment with expensive new drugs. In addition, New York has many teaching hospitals, which treat patients with complex, costly illnesses.
Mr. Clinton's proposal for a limits on Federal Medicaid spending is opposed not only by the governors, but also by a wide range of consumer groups and advocates, including the AIDS Action Council, the Alzheimer's Association, Catholic Charities USA, the Children's Defense Fund, Consumers Union and Families USA.
Half of Medicaid recipients are children, but more than two-thirds of Medicaid outlays are for people who are elderly or disabled.
Since its creation in 1965, Medicaid has been an open-ended program, providing health insurance to poor people who meet eligibility criteria set by Federal and state laws. While preserving the entitlement, Mr. Clinton would, for the first time, impose a firm limit on Federal Medicaid spending, to guarantee that per-capita Medicaid costs rise no faster than the nation's per-capita economic output.
Mr. Clinton's proposal would limit the financial obligations of the Federal Government, but not the states'. State officials worry that they will have to pick up many costs now covered by the Federal Government. Advocates for children, elderly and the disabled worry that states will cut services, reduce coverage of fast-growing benefits like prescription drugs or use cheap, low-quality nursing homes and health maintenance organizations.
As they arrive in Washington, the governors also bring a new perspective to the debate over Medicare, the Federal health insurance program for people who are elderly or disabled. They express concern that Congress and the Administration will solve Medicare's fiscal problems, too, by shifting costs to the states.
Medicare and Medicaid are usually viewed as separate programs. But the governors point out that the programs are intertwined, so that when the Federal Government cuts Medicare payments to hospitals and health maintenance organizations or increases premiums for Medicare beneficiaries, states incur new costs.
Medicaid fills the gaps in Medicare coverage for elderly people with low incomes. Six million people are eligible for both Medicare and Medicaid. In addition, state Medicaid programs must pay Medicare premiums and the patients' share of medical bills for any Medicare beneficiaries with incomes below the Federal poverty level ($9,491 a year for an elderly couple).
President Clinton says a cap is needed to make sure Medicaid costs never again explode as they did in the late 1980's and early 1990's. But the governors, in the draft of their new policy, say they "have already contributed to significant budgetary savings by controlling Medicaid growth rates."
Federal spending on Medicaid, which increased an average of 15 percent a year from 1985 to 1995, rose only 3.3 percent last year. The Congressional Budget Office has radically reduced its estimate of future Medicaid costs, but says that without any change in Federal law, the program will still grow 7.8 percent a year in the next five years, much faster than the economy or the rest of the Federal budget.
In the draft of their new Medicaid policy, the governors make these points:
*States should be allowed to require Medicaid recipients to get their health care through H.M.O.'s. Under current law, states must seek permission from the Federal Government whenever they want to impose such a requirement.
*Congress should repeal the Federal law that guarantees "reasonable and adequate" payments to hospitals and nursing homes treating Medicaid patients. Health care providers, who have filed many successful lawsuits to enforce this requirement, should not be allowed to sue states in disputes over reimbursement.
*States support President Clinton's efforts to make sure that all children eligible for Medicaid are enrolled. But the Federal Government must not do anything that encourages private employers to provide less health insurance for children.
The governors give several examples of how changes in Medicare may shift costs to the states. If the Federal Government reduces Medicare payments to H.M.O.'s, as President Clinton has proposed, health plans will be less likely to offer prescription drug benefits, and state Medicaid programs must "assume the cost of providing this important benefit," the governors said.

HEADLINE: Claims for Jobless Benefits Rise by 10,000

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
First-time claims for jobless benefits increased by 10,000 last week, the second consecutive jump after the steepest drop in six months.
The Labor Department said today that applications for unemployment insurance totaled a seasonally adjusted 351,000, the highest since 366,000 during the week that ended on Jan. 4. 
 New claims rose by 20,000, to 341,000, during the week ended Jan. 18, and many analysts had expected them to decline last week. After reaching 366,000 during the week ended Jan. 4, claims plunged by 45,000 a week later, the biggest drop since July.
Analysts have attributed some of the volatility to year-end problems adjusting the data for seasonal variations.
The four-week moving average of new weekly jobless claims fell by 5,500, to 344,750, last week, the lowest since 343,500 during the period that ended on Dec. 21. Many analysts track the less volatile four-week average.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Fed Chief Seeks Curbs In Price-Indexed Benefits
Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, urged Congress to move quickly to limit cost-of-living increases for Social Security and other benefits. 
 Mr. Greenspan said the Labor Department should speed up its efforts to fix shortcomings in the Consumer Price Index that have led, according to some estimates, to overstatements of about one percentage point a year. Over time, putting a brake on cost-of-living increases could save the Government hundreds of billions of dollars and make it easier to balance the budget. [Page A1.]
 
Damage Control at Mercury Finance
Officials of Mercury Finance sought to salvage credit agreements that were thrown into violation when the company reduced its reported profits for the last four years because of bookkeeping irregularities. But the Bank of Boston said it was canceling the sale of its Fidelity Acceptance unit to Mercury. [D2.]
 
I.R.S. Calls Computers Inadequate
The Internal Revenue Service conceded it spent $4 billion developing modern computer systems that a top official said "do not work in the real world," and it proposed contracting out the processing of tax returns. That would allow nongovernment workers to see confidential information about Americans' incomes. [A1.]
 
Stocks Surge for Second Day
Stocks rose for a second day, largely on the strength of technology and drug stocks and rising bond prices. The Dow industrials surged 83.12 points, to 6,823.86. [D6.]

Treasury prices rose, with the 30-year bond yield falling to 6.86 percent from 6.90. [D13.]

Conrail Talks May Avoid Big Issues
The three companies involved in the Conrail takeover battle are expected to sit down today to begin discussions on redrawing the rail freight map of the eastern United States. But because no confidentiality agreement was reached to cover the talks, it is likely that no substantive issues will be discussed. [D3.]
 
Samsung to Buy Rest of AST
Samsung of South Korea said it would acquire the 51 percent of AST Research it does not already own. AST, a personal computer maker, has had heavy operating losses. [D3.]
 
A Race to Bring Out Flu Drug
The shares of Gilead Sciences soared earlier this week after the company said that it would soon begin clinical trials of a drug to treat influenza. But a little-known Australian company, Biota Holdings, has a similar drug well advanced in human trials, and could beat Gilead to market. Market Place. [D6.]

F.D.A. Approves Diabetes Drug
The F.D.A. approved a diabetes drug produced by Warner-Lambert that is the first in a new class of medications intended to attack an underlying cause of the disease. The drug, Rezulin, offers to a million diabetics the possibility of reducing or eliminating their dependence on daily insulin injections, the F.D.A. said. Warner-Lambert stock rose $3.125, to $78, on the news. [A24.]

Mirage Gets Agreement on Casino
Officials in New Jersey have agreed to share more of the financial risk in building a $330 million road and tunnel that would help gamblers reach a casino complex that Mirage Resorts plans to build in Atlantic City. [B6.]
 
Study: On-Line Users Watch Less TV
Homes that subscribe to America Online watch 15 percent less television than the average home, the on-line service said, citing a study by Nielsen Media Research. [D5.]

HEADLINE: Giuliani Budget to Spend Millions On Trailers for Crowded Schools

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY 

 BODY:
Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani unveiled a preliminary budget for New York City yesterday that includes $500 million to lessen overcrowding in the public schools. Much of the money would be spent on customized trailers and other prefabricated buildings to create places for 40,000 students in the aging and overburdened system.
The proposal comes as the Board of Education is wrestling with sharp growth in enrollment that has forced some schools to hold classes in bathrooms and other makeshift locations. 
 The Mayor's overcrowding plan was part of a budget package that also called for $550 million in cuts at municipal agencies, the largest of which is a $115 million reduction at the Health and Hospitals Corporation, which runs the municipal hospital system.
He would also cut $58 million from social services, $81 million from the Correction Department, $12 million from the Transportation Department and $42 million from the Police Department, although the size of the force is not expected to be affected.
City officials have already allocated almost $4 billion for the repair and construction of schools in the next four years. But with the number of students rising by 20,000 a year in a system that has 1.1 million, they concede that they must pursue stopgap measures until new buildings are finished.
The 40,000-seat increase would represent a significant rise in temporary housing in the system, which until now had only 5,000 such spaces. Still, at the beginning of the school year in September, the school system had 91,000 more students than seats.
The $500 million would also help pay for 20,000 more seats that would be created through renovations, the leasing of buildings and the purchase of air-conditioners so schools can stay open in the summer.
About half of the $500 million is new money the Mayor is adding to the city's capital budget, officials said. The rest is funds that he had planned to spend in 1999 and 2000 but is instead moving up to use in the current year and the 1998 fiscal year, which begins on July 1.
"We have given a substantial priority to the Board of Education," Mr. Giuliani said at a news conference called to present his election-year budget. The Mayor's Democratic rivals have attacked his record on education, saying that he has neglected the public schools.
The preliminary budget is an overview of the city's finances for the next fiscal year, as well as the three years after that. After receiving comments from the public and other officials, the Mayor by law files his formal, or executive, budget in May.
Even as he hopes to increase spending on education and cut taxes, the Mayor is asking for budget cuts in addition to the $115 million he wants to take from the Health and Hospitals Corporation. The Mayor said that though tax revenues had grown steadily in recent months, there was still waste at some city agencies.
If recent history is a guide, Mr. Giuliani will probably not get all of the $550 million in cuts. The release of details about the preliminary budget begins an annual ritual at City Hall: the Republican Mayor calls for reductions, knowing that the Democratic-controlled Council will restore some of them before approving the budget and thereby demonstrate that it had some influence in the budget-making process.
In fact, almost as if by script, a few minutes after Mr. Giuliani finished presenting his spending plan at the news conference, the Council Speaker, Peter F. Vallone, was denouncing some of the cuts and emphasizing that the Council would play a role in fashioning the budget.
"There is still a lot of pain" in the budget, Mr. Vallone said.
The reductions proposed by the Mayor represent the starkest part of a spending plan that is generally less grim than the budgets he put forth in the first three years of his tenure. Tax revenues have jumped sharply, largely because of the strong performance of Wall Street.
Still, Mr. Giuliani's potential Democratic rivals for the fall election quickly pounced on the preliminary budget, pointing to it as evidence of what they contended was the Mayor's fiscal mismanagement and coldhearted priorities. They focused in particular on his calls for spending to lessen overcrowding and to bolster reading, arts and computer programs in the school system.
Ruth W. Messinger, the Manhattan Borough President, said that no matter what the Mayor proposed, his education record was still poor. She asserted that even as the Mayor was trumpeting the money for temporary classrooms, spending to build new schools had been delayed because of mistakes by his administration.
"This Mayor has left a trail of broken promises," Ms. Messinger said.
The Mayor yesterday also fleshed out plans that he had first given details about on Wednesday. He proposed cutting $250 million in taxes, including the city's share of the 8.25 percent sales tax on clothing under $500. Over all, he called for the city to spend about one-half of 1 percent more than it did last year -- the first increase that he has proposed in a preliminary budget since he became Mayor.
The total amount of the budget was $32.9 billion, which should rise to at least $34 billion, because of accounting rules, when he presents the final budget in May, the Mayor said.
Fiscal monitors, who roundly criticized the Mayor's preliminary budget last year, were divided over his new plan, with some contending that he had allocated too much for spending and tax cuts and should have saved more.
But some of the state officials who oversee the city's budget said the Mayor had generally done a competent job of shaping it. Even H. Carl McCall, the State Comptroller and a longtime critic of the Mayor's budget policies, praised him, saying his preliminary budget focused more attention on the city's long-term problems than in the past.
Mr. McCall said, "He clearly recognizes and is attempting to address what I and the other monitors have long said: the city's health demands long-term care and not simply a Band-Aid approach."
But Mr. McCall and other budget experts also cautioned that the city's faced several dangers. It must grapple with projected deficits in 2000 and 2001 that are estimated to range from $2 billion to $3 billion. And if the State Legislature does not approve several bills, the underpinnings of the budget could crumble.
The city, for example, will soon reach the limit on its debt outstanding set by the State Constitution, preventing it from selling bonds to finance its $14 billion capital budget. It has asked the Legislature to approve a bill that will allow it to avoid the limit. If that does not happen, it will have to postpone an array of projects, including the $500 million package intended to lessen school overcrowding.
The Legislature is also considering extending a 12.5 percent surcharge on the city's personal income tax, a bill that both Mr. Giuliani and Mr. Vallone support. The surcharge, which is largely used to finance salaries of police officers, gives the city about $500 million a year.
If the Legislature approves those plans, as the Mayor has been urging, this budget will probably be less rocky than Mr. Giuliani's earlier ones.
"I think that he has made sound choices, given his priorities and given the economic environment," said Robert A. Kurtter, a vice president at Moody's Investors Service. "He has invested resources in strengthening the financial plan and also invested resources in the city. The plan is not without its flaws and weaknesses, but our view at this point is that those risks are manageable."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani outlined his budget yesterday at City Hall. (Marilynn K. Yee/The New York Times)(pg. A1)

HEADLINE: Bill on Deficit Clears Panel Buts Hits Snag

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
The Senate Judiciary Committee today approved a constitutional amendment that would require a balanced Federal budget, but the eventual prospects for the amendment may have dimmed.
Two Democratic Senators on whose votes the Republican proponents of the amendment had relied for the two-thirds majority needed for Senate approval said at the Judiciary Committee meeting that they might vote against it on the floor without substantial changes. 
 The two, Joseph R. Biden Jr. of Delaware and Robert G. Torricelli of New Jersey, voted for an almost identical amendment two years ago, Mr. Biden in the Senate and Mr. Torricelli when he was in the House.
But today they advocated changes including the exclusion of Social Security from deficit calculations and the creation of a separate capital budget to allow borrowing for "physical infrastructure that provides long-term economic benefits."
A Republican staff assistant who has been keeping track of probable votes said it would be difficult if not impossible to reach the 67-vote threshold if Mr. Biden and Mr. Torricelli voted "no." All 55 Republican senators plan to vote for the amendment, which means 12 Democratic votes will be necessary.
Mr. Torricelli said in an interview that he was trying to put pressure on Republicans to open negotiations.
The amendment's chief Republican sponsor, Senator Orrin G. Hatch of Utah, who heads the Judiciary Committee, said the changes suggested by the two Senators would open "big loopholes" he could never accept.
The panel approved the amendment today in a 13-to-5 vote. It would require the budget to be balanced by early in the next century and every year afterward unless the Senate and House voted by three-fifths majorities to waive the requirement.
Mr. Biden and Mr. Torricelli were among those who voted for the amendment. But they said they could vote differently on the floor.
Many Democrats, as long as they are not identified, acknowledge that the issue of excluding Social Security from budget calculations is something of a Trojan horse.
The latest Congressional Budget Office calculations show that without counting Social Security, which now runs a large surplus, the lawmakers would have to find an additional $465 billion in tax increases or spending cuts over the next five years. As a practical matter, that would make it impossible to balance the budget early in the next century.
But politically, few other issues carry so much weight; no politician wants to be seen as jeopardizing Social Security. Proposals like those of Mr. Biden and Mr. Torricelli allow lawmakers to say that they favor a balanced budget amendment but want to make sure Social Security is not at risk. President Clinton, who opposes the amendment, said in a letter to lawmakers on Wednesday that it could put the elderly at risk.
In the House, where a close vote is also expected, a small group of Republicans called a news conference today to advocate excluding Social Security from the calculations.
Democrats held a news conference to advertise a letter signed by more than 1,000 economists warning that an amendment could make recessions worse and restrict necessary Government borrowing.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Senator Orrin G. Hatch, center, Republican of Utah, at a Judiciary Committee meeting on a constitutional amendment to require a balanced budget. At right was Senator Patrick J. Leahy, Democrat of Vermont. (Paul Hosefros/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Index Funds Are Next Best Thing to Luck

 BODY:
To the Editor:
 "Riding Wall St. on Autopilot: Indexed Funds Draw Investors" (front page, Jan. 28) leaves out a few points about index investing.
The theory behind an index fund, which is a mutual fund designed to mimic the performance of the stock market, is that a stock is worth what the market says it is worth at any given moment. The market takes account of all the information available about it and about the broad economy. Individual stocks seem to move with the market in the absence of information about them (plus some random movement that accounts for inexplicable short-term price variations). 
An individual can only do better than the market by acting on inside information or by luck. Not many individuals can claim either, and that includes the managers of actively managed funds.
The risk from buying an individual stock arises because of variations in its performance. The risk can be reduced by diversification; hence the idea of investing in the market as a whole: an index fund. If market risk is still thought too high, some assets can be invested in Treasury bills, the lowest-risk securities.
The high rate of return from index funds comes from the growth of the economy and of corporate profits.
 
JOHN T. BENNETT
Alexandria, Va., Jan. 28, 1997

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bonds Rise On Jobs and Factory Data

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:
Treasury securities traded higher yesterday for the third consecutive session after reports suggested the economy might be slowing enough to keep inflation benign and the Federal Reserve from raising interest rates.
The price of the 30-year bond rose 15/32, to 95 13/32. The yield was 6.86 percent, down from 6.90 percent on Wednesday. 
 Bond prices received an earlier lift when the Labor Department reported that initial claims for unemployment benefits for the week ended Saturday rose 10,000, to 351,000, after a revised increase of 20,000 the week before. Analysts' expectations were for a gain of 8,000. The report eased concerns that recent signs of robust economic growth would prompt the Fed to raise short-term rates.
Speculation that foreign central banks were buying 5- and 10-year notes also bolstered prices, as did a private survey showing that factories in the United States saw a decline in shipments, new orders and production in January. The APIC/ Evans Economics Business Outlook fell to 45.6, from 51.8 in December. Analysts note that a reading below 50 suggests manufacturing is unchanged or declining.
Market participants also took some solace from testimony by Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, before the Senate Finance Committee, because his comments focused on changing the Consumer Price Index rather than on the state of the economy. Fed policy makers will begin a two-day meeting on Tuesday.
Jack Ablin, director of fixed income and senior fund manager at the Private Bank of the Bank of Boston, said Mr. Greenspan did not disclose anything in his testimony.
Dealers and pension funds were in the market yesterday but were trading in low volume, and retail buyers stayed mostly out of the way, some analysts said.
Market participants are now divided into two groups, Mr. Ablin said. One group does not appear to fear inflation and sees economic activity under control. The other includes technical traders who buy and sell on variables like prices, volume and price movement, and who are worried about the relationship of stocks to bonds and foreign participation. The market is oversold in the near term as it waits for the employment report next week, Mr. Ablin said.
The market must contend today with the first real look at fourth-quarter gross domestic product, the Chicago Purchasing Managers survey for January, the University of Michigan's final index of consumer sentiment for January and the report on new home sales for December.
Doug Burtnick, senior fixed-income analyst with Technical Data of Boston, said, "The market seems to be shedding some of the negativism that we have seen lately."
"But the market is far from out of the woods yet," he said. "Friday provides a huge slate of data that will show us how strong the economy was in the end of 1996 and will include the first 1997 data."
At yesterday's Treasury auction of new one-year bills, the average rate was 5.34 percent, up 3 basis points compared with last month's auction.
Following are the results of the auction:

HEADLINE: Now a Tightrope Walker: Bulgaria's Toppled King

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY

DATELINE: MADRID

 BODY:
While the country he briefly ruled writhes in political and economic turmoil, the former King of Bulgaria, Simeon II, sounds these days like a man resigned to an outsider's role, despite his triumphant return visit late last spring.
"I'm turning 60 next June, so I don't give myself too long in maneuvering," he said recently in an interview in his baronial residence here. Rack after rack of deer antlers were mounted outside the back door, which one of his sons opened with an apology for making a visitor wait, because all the household help was off for the afternoon. 
 "I'll do what I can, but I do not have the vocation of a spare wheel," the former King said, his blue eyes flashing. "If things go fine without me, then this is a blessing for Bulgaria and for me."
King Simeon was proclaimed monarch by the Bulgarian Parliament in 1943 after his father, King Boris III, died suddenly after a summons to Hitler's eastern field headquarters in August 1943. The young King's uncle and other regents who ruled in his name were executed in 1945 after Communist-led forces drove out the Germans, and Simeon and his mother fled to Egypt after the monarchy was abolished in 1946. He has lived in Spain for the last 50 years.
The collapse of Communism brought democracy to Bulgaria, but not the end of the Communists, who have been governing again, this time as Socialists. Complaining of mass starvation and national financial ruin, crowds have stormed the Parliament in Sofia in recent weeks, demanding new elections.
From here, King Simeon sent a statement to his supporters urging a "statesmanlike" solution to the crisis. "A strong dose of generosity and patriotism are needed to reach the consensus I have been insisting upon for years," he told them, trying, like any good constitutional monarch, to set himself above the fray. The trouble is, he said himself, there is a new Constitution in Bulgaria that does not include the role of monarch.
"During my trip there last summer, I tried to do my best in telling people that a constitutional monarch is not a dictator," he said in a conversation in his study. "In three or four cities I had the feeling that one word from me would have been enough to start a march on Sofia, but when I started talking about how the country needed all its children to work together, the crowds deflated."
Speaking the fluent if mannered English he learned in boarding schools in England and the United States, he said that his visit had received a flawless reception and that a poll by Gallup International afterward showed that about 12 percent of the people supported a return of the monarchy.
"Not even a clenched fist, sir, if you like," he said. But he decided against a return visit later last year.
"The Socialists were celebrating the 50th anniversary of the downfall of the monarchy," he said. "In December they had the presidential elections, and I didn't feel it right to go then and interfere." Some expatriates abroad had urged him to run for the presidency, but, he said, "The Constitution had some clauses that seemed specifically engineered to keep me out of the race."
Likening his position to that of a tightrope walker, he said: "I don't think it's dignified for me to be an eternal candidate. I don't feel that I'm a pretender to the throne, or a claimant. If people don't feel I could contribute to the stability of Bulgaria, I have many other interests I can pursue."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Ex-King Simeon, who lives in Spain, visited Bulgaria last May. (Agence France-Presse)

HEADLINE: Drugs and Technology Reinforce Stock Rally

BYLINE: By DAVID BARBOZA

 BODY:
Any jitters the stock market had this month temporarily disappeared as stocks soared yesterday for the second consecutive session, largely on the strength of technology and drug issues.
The Dow Jones industrial average surged 83.12 points, to 6,823.86. Since losing about 3 percent in a late-month downturn, the Dow has gained 168 points in two days and now stands just 60 points away from its high of 6,883.90, which was set on Jan. 21. 
 Stocks were again bolstered by a strengthening bond market and steady interest rates. Bonds have been rising on reports indicating that the economy is slowing and that inflationary pressures are diminishing.
"The stock market continues to move in lock step with the bond market, and economic news of the last couple of days has all been indicative of slower growth," Robert H. Stovall, president of Stovall/21st Advisers, said. "And for that reason, January is finishing on an up note."
Bond prices rose yesterday after the Labor Department reported an unexpected increase in the number of jobless claims filed last week. The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond fell to 6.86 percent, from 6.90 on Wednesday.
Laszlo Birinyi, a market analyst at Birinyi Associates in Greenwich, Conn., said another significant factor in yesterday's rally was a sharp decline in the price of gold, which some analysts still see as a barometer of inflationary expectations. Gold for February delivery, the most active contract, fell $5.60 yesterday to a three-and-a-half year low of $346.20 an ounce on the Comex division of New York Mercantile Exchange.
"That was the key today," Mr. Birinyi said, noting that cyclical industrial stocks, like General Electric and Du Pont, gained. "What the market is saying is that the economy is going to get better, but gold is saying that it's not going to be inflationary."
Mr. Birinyi, who has long been bullish on the market, added that those signs suggested strong corporate profits in the future but low inflation, the best of all worlds for this stock market. "That's even better than a slow-growth economy," he said.
Drugs, chemicals and consumer products were the backbone of yesterday's rally. Procter & Gamble, Philip Morris and Merck, all of which reported earnings this week that either met or beat analysts' expectations, advanced. P.& G. was up 4 9/64, to 115 3/8, a 52-week high; Philip Morris was up 3 1/2, to 119 7/8, and Merck was up 2 1/8, to 90 3/8.
Du Pont gained 2 3/4, to 110, despite reporting earnings on Wednesday that were below expectations. And G.E. was up 2, to 105 5/8.
Broader market indexes were also strong. The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks rose 11.67 points, or 1.5 percent, to 784.17, and the Nasdaq composite index was up 15.85, to 1,371.02.
Technology stocks continued to prosper. Microsoft and Intel once again led the pack, both setting records yesterday. Microsoft reached the century mark for the first time, rising 3 7/8, to 101 1/8, and Intel soared 5 3/8, to 160 1/8, after the semiconductor company said that it was lowering the price on some of its Pentium computer chips less than expected, suggesting that demand was still high.
Several other semiconductor concerns gained on the news. Texas Instruments was up 4 3/4, to 74 1/2; Micron Technology gained 2 3/8, to 35 3/8.
Richard E. Cripps, chief market strategist at Legg Mason, said the market's two-day surge, coming after a five-day decline, was evidence that it was consolidating; that rather than a vertical rise, investors were selecting big-capitalization industries, and that the market was beginning to solidify within a trading range.
"The broader market is needing to consolidate," he said. "On the surface, it's relatively flat, but underneath the water, you're seeing the money rotating. Now we're rotating into the drug and health care industry."
Among drug stocks, Warner-Lambert, which said yesterday that it had won Government approval for the diabetes drug Rezulin, rose 3 1/8, to 78. Others also benefited. Eli Lilly was up 4 7/8, to 86 7/8, and Pfizer was up 4 1/8, to 90 3/4.
"We're beginning to see a shift in leadership into consumer products and drugs," Peter Cardillo, director of research at Westfalia Investments in New York, said.
